Gyroscopic Poems
EGO.
Yea — L am a love-child: in love beyond all
encompassing, and imagining
Did my mother conceive and hear me, —my
mother the Earth.
A myriad-million years was my gestation,
ere l was perfect,
Enough grown to be born,
and expelled onto the bared knees
of the World.
(And who dare blame me if I know not my
father: perchance he was King of
Chaos! who knowst)
Born!
Heir of all past ages, all ages, yet I
laughed
And at my birth rejected my heritage, be-
cause I desired nothing—
Laughed into the face of Night, who was
my nurse, so that
She smiled back at my laughter, and
covered me with
A very small portion torn from the hem of
a cloud.
Yet my desire was for nakedness, and poiver.
I AM—I AM—I AM—I AM —1 AM—I
AM.
And I delight in poiver and in life. POWER,
oh wouarous word and symbol, breathe in
me, round me, expand
my lungs, and — pouf —blow through my
nostrils. I am life.
I hate, I love, and I desire to propagate, all
to express
The power in me.
(But I hate my kin. because they are the
spawn of earth, but I
I am the World's child of love.)
Let me rejoice thaUthe hate is in me, for to
hate is life;
Did not I bite hard the nipple when my
mother nourished me,
so that she cried aloud in agony of
pain and joy.
And I go naked that my mother might re-
joice in my strength,
For all save my mother I despise.
JNO. M. PRENTICE.
CROWS.
I Stumps, stumps ; stumps, stumps of trees —
- uncountable, ad libitum, one might
almost say ad infinitum.
'Stumps shrinking into the opalescent bush
perspective
Stumps for —roughly speaking, for nothing is
so sincerely deceptive as distance—a trio
of miles ahead.
Stumps in a silent landscape.
Grows, crows, crows! Immobile silhouettes:
coal-black, carven out of the veritable
combustible itself!
A crow to each stump; not a stump without
its crow.
Stump, crow; crow, stump. Stump, stump,
stump. Crow, crow, crow —ad libitum,
ad infinitum, or, at least, three miles.
Caw, caw, caw, caw, caw!
Sudden movement! The shock of sound!
See, the crows have awakened. The landscape
palpitates.
Whirr! Whoosh! Flap! Flip! Caw', caw,
caiv, caw!
The crows fly, aviate, mount upwards, blob-
bing the empyrean and singing their
heart-piercing song of freedom: u Caw,
caw, caw, caw!' 1
Meantime the stumps are left desolate.
Oh, stumps, stumps, stumps. Oh, stumps of
trees! ' _
EARDLEY TURNER.
THE VORTIOIST ARTIST DECLAIMS.
I sing Chaos and tlic glory of it
Yes, and the glory of Myself who have disin-
tegrated
Out of the slavish orderliness of Nature
Chaos primaeval and have set it aslant on
canvas,
And have christened it “Portrait of a Lady ’
So that the brain, of the beholder,
The orderly, slavish, custom-ridden Brain of
the Beholder,
Seeing it > is set free and is filled with
emotion
And goes swirling vortically
Seeking for something
to hang
onto.
What is Form but the delusion of the Eye
And Color but a conceit that changes with
the Sun
And Substance but a trick of Atoms bound
slavishly?
I'-r-r-r-r-r-r-r-t with a Noise as of torn calico,
we will rend them asunder
Casting them into the Vortex that vorticates
for them.
And if it be the wrong sort of a vortex,
Swirling in rhythm,
Bound and enchained by the gripping Laws
of the Universe
Forcing all things to its mould,
Then will we make for ourselves a New
vortex,
Somewhere over the Edge of Beyond, out in
the Back Blocks of Chaos
And There shall this Vortieular movement
of ours Fall Silent
Like a Top that has gone to sleep while it is
spinning,
So that the orderly, slavish and custom-ridden
Brain mentioned at the beginning of the
Poem
Shall no longer perceive it, shall basely con-
clude it has stopt
But we, the Gifted Ones within the Heart of
the Vortex '
ShaU icnote that we have attained to Perfec-
tion,
And are gone, clean out of sight and of
hearing.
M. H.
THE KOOKABURRA.
I “Ah, ah, ah, ah! ou, ou, ou, ou!
Ah ou, ah ou, ah ou! ah, ah, ou, ou!
Behold the Futurist Jackass, the Jackass of
the morrow.
Whose laughter echoeth through the South.
The Trees bend down with laughter
Ah, ah, ah, ah, ou, ou, ou, ou
Ah ou, ah ou! ah ou! ah ou! .
Whispering gums, gloiuing wattle,
But above all the laughter, the laughter
Mocking, echoing, echoing,mocking,
Mocking the sunrise, ah, ah, ou ou!
Impotent anger, furious heat.
What availeth? Nothing availeth.
From the sheltering trees, ah ou, ah ou!
Ah, ah, ah, ah, ou, ou, ou, ou!
And noiv the end of Bay —
Sinking, sinking, slowly, slowly
Silently, peacefully the Sun would set.
But no, no, no — jeering, mocking
Ah, ah, ah, ah, ou, ou, ou, ou!
Ah ou, ah ou. ah. ou-. ah ou!
TJnrest Eternal the laughter of Fiends,
Echoing, mocking, jeering, following
Into the Future, into the day that is to be!
Ah ou, ah ou, ah ou!
Ah, ah: ah! ou ou ou!
Ah ou, ah on!
LETTY FRENCH.
The Red Page
The Australian-bred horse, Gyroscopicism
(by Vorticism, out of Futurism) has entered
for the Poetry Stakes. From the form he is
showing on the track it is evident that the
favorite, Pegasus, even with the help of his
wings, will be but an also-ran.
It is somewhat difficult to define Gyro-
scopic Poetry. Let’s try. It must deal
with a modern subject in a modern manner;
it must not refer to the past, nor make use
of the hallowed cliches of classical verse;
it must deal only in futures; it must not
rime, and its metre must be irregular. This
is merely the ABC of Gyroscopism. The
main thing'is that It must be gyroscopic
(See dictionary.) Most of the competitors
refused to take Gyroscopicism seriously: it is
so much easier not to, Good gyroscopicism
was provided by “Uloola,” “L.5.D.,” Nella
Campbell, Roi Ah Rede, J.J.S. (alack, too
vortically torrid for publication !).
Jane furnishes this: —
Wow-ow-ow + Eee-ee-ee =
Screaming medley of mutinous motors madly leap-
ing—■
Roaring clattering rattle of seemingly intoxicated
Thin ankles -f- indecent blouses -f large hands + small
hats = flappers in multitudes.
Mauve socks + purple ties + green hats + American
shoes = k nuts outside teashops.
Thousands of powdered noses—pug (or retroussS),
Roman (or Ike) pressed to windows of hat
shops.
Gabble-gabble-gabble + sssssssss (= scandals whispered
loudly) + “she told me" + “and so I said” = con-
versation in a tearoom.
Total of above = Female Sydney any old Saturday
morning.
“Undine” discourses gyroscopically upon
“A Prude Ascending a Ladder,” X.X. has a
pleasing appreciation of Snails and Slugs.
E.M.O. and “Pontius Potter” get nearer the
truly gyroscopic
O EARTH! O EARTH!
1 laugh. I shout, I caper in my alee,
1 see vow Tolling through illimitable space,
like a derelict ship deserted by passengers and crew,
wrapped in a blood-red mist.
O eailh! why did you nourish Them?
That they might kill aim strive against each other
and plot arid plan and scheme and love and hate,
all in the end to bring annihilation?
What will you do now, O Earth?
Will you roll on forever void?
Will you breed up anew race of beings?
Will you return to the Sun wiience you came?
or wiil you become a Moon ? O Earth ?
If you breed anew race
breed them without teeth or claws,
for all too soon will they learn to bite and scratch ;
breed them without brain or mind,
for all too soon will they learn to crush their
fellows,
and the weak wiil learn to fawn upon the strong,;
breed them not at all,
but scatter yourself unto the smallest fragments,
and cast yourself from out the limits of Space.
Your work is finished
Your record is written as Failure.
Hahahahahahaaa!
E.E.M.O.
OPPOSITES.
A two-headed demon that gloats
On sweat, pangs, aches and sundry other unpleasant-
. nesses,
Laughs derision as we rest from toil,
And lures us back with siren murmurings.
They call it Work.
A curse on pick and pen and- every tool
That man is married to for lean drab years.
I His reward is a crust, and twisted kisses,
And the unbounded joy of fostering purple pomp
For others,
Who have viewed work through a telescope
And called it pleasant names and lauded it;
But spat when it approached.
The world is sick to death of making things:
Railways, straw hats, hoarse shrieks and stuff to
blacken boots ;
Vile things that clamp us to the dustv earth
The figtree’s now a flaunting draper’s shop.
Each sylvan pool disports a drowned pup.
Behold the rapture in the knacker’s eye
Amid the wreckage of what men have wrought.
The suffragistte’s a knacker with illusions.
An army is a disillusioned knacker magnified.
They shatter gcstatically
The dross of centuries.
Soon- —men will scorn to delve within the earth:
The poet will not strive for tinkling rbvmes :
Gaunt Work will creep to some dank spot.
And die of laughter.

The dainty 'booklet from which the above
verses are quoted contains 13 other little
pieces of similar even quality, some reprinted
from The Bulletin. With little artifice,
Dorothy Frances McCrae contrives to com-
press the feminine view-point of war into her
simple metre. The appeal of these verses
is immediate. They are hurried jottings,
rushed into publication ; but their directness
and simplicity give them a permanent value.
Despite Armageddon, the Peace Society of
N.S.W. is not dismayed. It intends to con-
tinue the publication of Pax, its monthly
magazine. Indeed its editor proudly
announces that he believes that the war will
increase the circulation, and triumphantly
states that “already we have got several new
subscribers.” One wonders if these new sub-
scribers bear German names: it would be a
decent act on the part of that nation to sup-
port the organ that has always fought—as
far as an insect can fight—to keep Australia
defenceless. The magazine even reprints its
a'n«*s: “The adoption of arbitration in all
international disputes instead of war; by the
simultaneous reduction of armaments to re-
lieve the people of the world of the increas-
ing burden of taxation; removal of inter-
national misunderstandings and cultivation
of international brotherhood and goodwill.”
If the Peace Society, headed by Miss Rose
Scott, would only make a deputation to the
Kaiser, surely that kindly monarch would
adopt arbitration, reduce his armaments, and
cuRrvate international brotherhood and good-
will ?
After 1h- Storm (Pratten Bros., Sydney)
is a very foolish prophecy made by Arabi
Pasha, in the form of a lecture at Alexandria
in 1M1. Under its German title, Nach dem
Sturm, it is alleged to have had a wide cir-
culation in Germany. All that need be said
of it is that there are 32 contributors to
The Bulletin who have made better
prophecies, and any one of them is prepared,
for the price of two beers, to prophesy till
they are elected from the bar.
War Verses
SECOND THOUGHTS.
I wished you back at my breast
When I saw this war descend,
Notv I d scour tne east and west
For another son fo send;
Oh. that the parents, too, might go
To lead the way, and slay the ]oe.
I tvislied your toddling feet
Played round the house again,
Now I want them strong and fleet,
And sure in the grand campaign.
Our prayers will follow you, my son;
God take you, then —His will be done.
I wanted your baby tongue
To prattle with laughter gay,
Now give me an order flung
Like a rifle-shot away —
Houv parents' hearts are on the field
Die if you must , hut do not yield.
WAR.
In the foreground streaming cges,
In the distance battle cries,
Down the skyline blue and dim _
Goes his ship. Christ shelter him.
Just a soldier gone to war,
But he was the babe I bore,
And I buckled on his sword,
Like a soldier’s mother, Lord.
Other mothers need his care,
Babes that other mothers bare,
And I were the poorest thing
Did I grudge him to his king.
So with smiles l bade him go,
Choked the sob, and anguish throe,
But above the martial hymn
Wails my heart, Christ shelter him !
Dorothy Frances McCrae,
in “Soldier, My
Soldier!” (G. Robertson, C.A.)-
Australian Fiction
Vera G. Dwyer is to be congratulated on
a great advance from the crudeness of her
first attempt at flapperature, With Beating
Wings. Mona’s Mystery Man (Ward,
Lock) has an absurd and preposterous plot ;
but, for her special class of readers, this
does not matter. The flapper loves the in-
credible provided it is romantic. But the
merit of this newel lies in its portraiture.
The two men are merely the sort of_ men
that the flapper imagines ; but the family of
the Worths is a genuine Australian
suburban family, stereoscopically photo-
graphed. These people are delightful ; their
talk is real, their humor human. And
Mona, the affected poseur, whose veneer
is rudely rubbed off in her contact
with life, is a type universal, admir-
ably and unshrinkingly drawn. This novel
places Vera G. Dwyer high up in her class
—a class that has easily established a repu-
tation in Britain. It may be said that the
only successful exponents of flapperature in
English fiction are Australians.
Two Island stories, South Sea Sinners, by
Arthur Crocker (N.S.W. Bookstall), and
Where Bonds are Loosed, by E. L. Grant
Watson (Duckworth, per George Robertson,
C. A,), offer an interesting contrast in
method. Crocker’s shilling-worth is a
luscious picture of brown maid and beauti-
ful white Australian girl, with the mag-
nificent hero the object of their rivalry.
Of Course, he drinks, and lives with some
brown person’s pleasing brown wife. But
as he persists in rescuing the white damsel
from dangers that sensible person recovers
from her shock and marries him. Other
things helped: “Her beautifully-modelled
arms glinted a golden tan in these early sun-
rays; and the wet, light silk clung and
moulded to her superb form, revealing its
every line and beautiful curve.” On page
74 her “whole body throbbed against him.”
On page 76 “she felt the throb of his great
body against her own, and knew that the
strings of his own soul were ecstatically
vibrant and responsive to the pulsing of
hers.” What hero wouldn’t go on rescuing
such a heroine? Despite this lusciousness
of description, the author has put some
solid work in his description of coolie life
on the Island copra plantations.
When Bonds are Loosed is a much more
ambitious work. E. L. Grant Watson has
written a few interesting brutal stories of
Australia, published in The Monthly
Review, This novel is brutal enough, too.
Kne' is a couple of islands oft the
coast of semi-tropical Australia, u&ed as a
sanatorium for sick natives- Sherwm is
appointed as the overseer of the island on
which are the male natives, where he lwes
iu solitude except for the occasional visit of
i-hp slovenly medical superintendent. A
oung doctor arrives on the other rig*
to prosecute some researches In disease
and the antagonism that springs up between
two unpleasant personalities leads to the
dismissal of the superintendent and the
appointment of the newcomer to his plaxji.
The author has spun out these inessential
preliminaries to wearisome length. He has
evidently not learnt the first lessons of lus
craft. A new matron is appointed for tne
women’s quarters, a matron with a past.
She seduces the young doctor trom his work,
and other things, till he tires of her. He
flies to the island where Sherwm, lording it
iu solitude, has sunk in the scale of civilisa-
tion. But the woman refuses to be lett
behind, forces her way on to the boat, and
arrives at Sherwin’s island. On a previous
visit he, who had not seen a woman tor a
year, had forcibly kissed her. Now he sees
that the affair has progressed far enough
for him to take a hand.
“Now, look here,” he says to the doctor.
“You’re tired of her, I can see that. Ive
been tired of a woman myself before now.
Play the game. Pass her on to me. IU
take her off your hands.”
The doctor refuses so easy a solution of
the difficulty. The men quarrel, and ulti-
mately Sherwin, in a fair fight, kills the
doctor. Then he returns for the woman,
the necessary blood-stained knife in his hand.
She purchases her life at the usual price.
So far the drama has hardly been a pleasant
one; but the final act shows that Sherwin
and the woman, unsuspected of the murder,
live happily ever after. Each has found in
the other the only possible mate. The con-
clusion. at first sight incomprehensible, has
some basis of probability in the fact that
both characters are of a low moral organi-
sation, both are unpleasant and unworthy
types, and consequently neither would have
any difficulty in finding his or her comple-
ment in the other. Why it should have been
necessary to write such a novel for the sake
of such "a moral is known, presumably, to
the author. It is a very unpleasant tale,
crudely though forcibly told.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Popular Pathos.
Prattle from English fool-paper: “1 heard a charm-
ing story of Queen Mary the other dav. Iter
Majesty, who felt tired after the effects of her Ion"
Scottish journey, requested one of her ladies-in-wait-
to read tlie newspaper to her. The lady com-
menced with the foreign intelligence. ‘No, no,’ said
Queen Mary, ‘read me the police reports. That is
where I learn the pathos in the lives of my people.' ”
Don’t read “intelligence” that’s labelled
foreign,
For foreigners have none.
Tired from the land of haggis, reel and
sporran,
The weary day is done.
Dead me police reports of the Old Bailey,
Vine-street and Bow-street from mv penny
daily.
Dead me the lines imposed on costers’ wives;
I fain would learn the pathos of their lives.
If there’s a tale of fortunes in the city,
Deshrew me, turn it down :
’ p would I know the pathos and the pitv
Of all this sordid town.
Bet me not. hear of Bond-street and its
fashions,
lint turn unto the elemental passions
Of suicides from off the Fish-street steeple,
And all the pathos of the English people.
Not any tale of bishop or of preacher,
I know ’em, every one,
And do not me inform or be a teacher—
For me there can be none.
But read to me of the new suicide,
Dr of that poor, proud one who killed his
bride.
Dead me of how poor men desert their wives.
Dead me the pathos of my people’s lives.
I do not wish to hear of Earls and Flossies
Of chorus girls and such,
Upon my moral code grow ancient mosses—-
A Duke should love his Dutch.
I know that whom the Lord lovetli he
chaseth,
I do not wish to hear whom He embraced!,
But tell me of the busy human hives,
And all the pathos of my people’s lives.
The pathos of the sweated industries,
Mention them all to me;
Of Cradley Heath’s chain-makers at their
ease
In starving liberty.
And tell me how the Decent Poor exist—
'■Tad not of Cousin Wilhelm’s mailed fist.
But how Bow-street is over-filled from dives.
And all the pathos of my people’s lives.
I "’ant no yarn of high patrician glory ;
Dead to me the intense
Tale homicidal, or the old, old story
Of the dear burglar’s booty and his fence.
Death ! Maintenance ! Divorce ! I fain would
hear it;
Tales of the Submerged Fifth, for I can bear
it;
Tales of Rill Sikes and blokes who fight with
knives,
And all the pathos of their daily lives.
•Tust find me Deynold’s, Lloyd’s and the
Despatch,
Or (better) Snorting’s Truth ;
Dead of the magistrate who made a match
For Boaz out of Shoreditch and his Dutli.
Dead of the. prisoner who keenly feels
His sad. position—laid down by the heels ;
Of magisterial jokes, black eyes of wives,
And all the pathos of my people’s lives.
VICTORIA.
MARTIN LUTHER.
Amaranth and Others.
I feel no hate for Amaranth,
Because she broke her vows to me;
No hot and jealous anger burns
Within my bosom smould’ringly ;
In fact, I am quite friendly with
Her chubby husband, John H. Smith.
Nor for the fickle Jessica
Have I a hurt that will not heal;
Because I missed fair K ate or Anne
jr No wild resentment do I feel.
’{is true they wedded place and pelf:
Hut 1 took someone else myself.
I do not deem that Ethel tried
To fool me with a tender kiss.
1 am no love-lorn egotist;
She liked me well enough for this:
Ho lean her head upon my chest,
And urge me to be better dressed.
There have been wounds; but they are
healed.
r .,{ keep but tender thoughts, and sweet,
-though some of them 1 see no more.
i3,ni n ir Some T they l )ass me in the street,
*tiH Mrs. Jones and Mrs. Smart
Are Kate ’ and '‘Mary” in my heart.
I only wonder, as I glance,
If ever, in a tender way,
Jessie has time to contemplate
w^. h at sweet night at Maroubra Bay.
Hi'nil joi’ a while we dreamed was Heaven,
filer little angels” now are seven.)
Hoes faded Dora, driving past.
Recall how once she dreamed with me
A fair, fond plan of future days—
Hove, and Ourselves and Poverty!
A brief but very sweet pretence,
>etoie we both were cursed with sense.
Uli; I remember scorc-s of things:
Sweet nights, gay days and tender dreams.
And sometimes, when one smiles at me.
hint of recollection gleams
Within her eyes; my memories flow
Back to the joys of years ago.
1 £i ew them all with kindly eye.
t hough some are stout and o've'rdresesd
And some are worn, and faded some. ‘ ’
cj-'ot IP'e.ying are the raven-tressed •
>till I. for sweetness shown to me
1 reserve their youth in memory

THE CONTACT FINGERS IN AN ALLEN WEST
CONTROLLER ARE DIFFERENT—TO OTHERS.
They arc securely attached to machined terminal bases,
rigidly clamped on to a mica insulated steel bar. Each
finger consists of a gunrnetal casting of great mechanical
strength, pivoted to a solid bearing on the base, and is
pressed against the drum by a spiral compression spring of
tested pressure. All fingers are fitted with heavy, renewable
contact tips of hard-drawn copper, and the current is carried
front the finger to the terminal base by a heavy shunt of
laminated ccipper. The renewable tip has a stud perman-
ently screwed into it, and is held in position by a nut and
lock washer. .
We are the Sole Australian Representatives for Allen
West & Co., of Brighton, England, and we will appoint
Agents for all their Controllers in each State.

THE IMPORTING DISEASE AND THE MELBOURNE WATER SHORTAGE.
MELBOURNE CITIZEN: "Let's see! Melbourne wanted a better water supply. I lost my job at the pipe-factory because it was our policy to import the
pipes, even if they cost more than our own. We borrowed the money abroad to pay for the imported pipes. I was taxed to pay interest on the loan.
The pipes didn't come because of the war. We're all taxed to pay for our share of the war. Now I have neither a job nor a wash!"

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 22, 1914.
FINANCIAL SCRAPS AND FANCIES
AND SUNDRIES.
Dear Bulletin, —Last week the N.S.W.
State Government again postponed its be-
lated financial statement on the ground that
the war had stood business on its head, or
its ear, or some other unusual portion of its
anatomy, and that the said Government was
more confused than usual, if such a. thing
could be imagined. Possibly the document
will appear all of a sudden, before these
remarks reach the public, for The Bulletin
is issued on Thursday, and Holman gener-
ally goes broke in his public capacity on
Wednesday night. At least he goes broke
more frequently on Wednesday than on other
days, though he is capable of going broke any
day. Many people who have much less stable
and remunerative billets than Holman’s have
been waiting anxiously to know what the
prospects may be. They have heard rumors
of new taxation. They have also
heard about schemes for floating short-dated
loans at as much as 6 per cent. There is
evidence in the monthly Treasury returns
that the State, after increasing taxation on
a very large scale in a time of peace and
prosperity, is building up an even _ larger
shortage than usual, and that, despite the
fact that it has lately run the borrowing
business up to something like £10,000,000
a year, it is living to a remarkable extent on
bank overdrafts and trust funds. And a
ioyous intimation has come to hand that
the Government, which hardly knows a field
of oats from a crow, thinks of investing
£1,000,000 in a great job of growing wheat
in drought-time, by labor paid at city trades-
union rates. Where it will get the
£1,000.000 nobody knows.
But about plain, hard economy and some
plain, hard schemes for raising revenue or
creating new assets in the way that will
bear least heavily on the people, there has,
so far, been very little information. Hol-
man remains just Holman —the sketchy,
adventurous politician who believes that
things will last his time. His own salary
hasn’t been reduced. His devices for spend-
ing money are as glorious as ever. He still
goes on the principle that sufficient for the
day is the slump thereof. Yet it is quite
possible that the war has only commenced,
and in such a case there is need for a man
with a long outlook —not a man who is con-
tent to float a .three months’ loan to go on
with.
At this time the illegal strike habit should
be sat upon with the weight of all the pyra-
mids of Egypt. \Vhen the N.S.W. farmer
has been ordered to sell his scanty wh r at
crop at less than current rates, the other
kind of worker, who wants to sell his com-
modity at more than current rates* should be
told in a strenuous fashion to go to the
Arbitration Court —or else to gaol. Such
a course might make the Ministry unpopu-
lar, and it might lose Holman his job, but
N.S.W. doesn’t exist entirely for the pur-
pose of supplying Holman with a job. If
that person can’t bhy sufficieht votes to keep
him in his high-salaried position save by
selling posterity into bondage to the foreign
Private Enterprise money-lender; and by
winking at breakage- of the law by his own
supporters; he had better quit the scene.
What kind of a living he could make when
he travelled on his own merits, and had no
£10,000,000 per annum of loan money to dis-
pense, it is hard to say. That is his own
affair. A country with so much debt behind
it, and such dubious prospects in front of
it, requires to economise labor and every-
thing else. And strikes are a lamentable
waste of labor; quite apart from the fact
that they bring the cherished principle of
industrial arbitration into contempt. The
latest thing of the kind was an effort by
certain marinfe stokers, who worked under
an award supplied by their own Court, at
the instance of their own Government; to
make a cornet or trust or lock-out in their
commodity of coal-shovelling. The occasion
was singularly inappropriate, for work has
a great tendency to diminish on its own
account; without assistance of this sort.
* * - * *
A great deal is heard these days about the
need to eliminate the middleman. When a
community is taking in sail the needless and
unproductive variety of middleman is a
luxury that it can’t afford. The bookmaker
is hereby recommended as a person who is
neither useful nor ornamental. He may
be a pardonable luxury when times are
good, but he is an absurd burden when times
are bad. The tote would do his work at
one-tenth of the cost, and would yield the
State a handsome revenue to boot. The
community would also get a better return
for its sinful bet on the unreliable horse,
and it would still have its pleasant Saturday
diversion* Something, also, might be done
with the very numerous city hotel which
doesn’t supply board and residence; but is a
mere drinking shop or beer abyss. Its position
is wholly illegal, and it is a waste of good
ground floorage and good beer-money_ and
good barmaid. The lady may be pictur-
esque, but in a hotel of the sort described
she is a costly and useless interloper between
the rum keg and the public thirst. The
individuals who underwrite our loans in
London, at very heavy commission, and the
London banks which charge a heavy com-
mission for paying the interest, are some
more costly middlemen. The State Govern-
ment has no direct control over the tariff,
yet its members have some influence in the
matter. And if they used that _ influence
towards the elimination of the importing
disease —the disease whereby the importer
(who is often a foreigner), and the
ship (which is mostly, foreign), and
the marine insurance co. (which is
usually foreign), and the indent agent
(named Schmidt as often as not),
and the broker (probably Gottschalk),
and the bank (which is very likely owned in
London), intervene between the manufac-
turer and the consumer —they might help to
save a trifle of money. Again, a good many
hundreds of men —railway employees of. one
sort and another —are kept doing middle
work of a wholly useless kind by the policy
which requires that the exports of two-thirds
of N.S.W. must be dragged for 100 or 200
or 300 miles further than is necessary in
order to reach a port. And the coal and
oil and grease and wear and tear involved
in these superfluous journeyings also repre-
sent the middleman in an aggravated form.
* * * *
It is essential to remember that this is
no time for high-flown moralities. Britain
itself seizes horses and carts and motor-cars
on its highways. Its allies do the same
things, and also gamble in forced loans and
all manner of wild devices. Its enemies do
all these things, and do plain highway rob-
bery in addition, by levying almost impossi-
ble contributions on occupied towns. If
N.S.W., or any other State, or the Aus-
tralian Government started public lotteries
by way of bringing some scrap of money
into the Treasury they would still be quite
as pious as circumstances allow. Even a tax
on church collections would be justified, or
a stamp duty on top hats. The great essen-
tials are (1) to make the finances balance;
(2) to get into a position which will enable
the country to do without loans; and (3) to
get together some kind of a cash surplus for
emergencies. The emergencies haven’t really
commenced vet. The position of France,
which has sent most of its manhood to the
war, and has lost its grand old cathedral of
Rheims, is pitiable enough, but still France
lives on its own resources. It isn’t depen-
dent pn the forbearance of a Hebrew in
Tobolsk or Timbuctoo. If N.S.W. lost all
its cathedrals and also lost Sydney, it would
still be better off than it is, provided that, at
the same time, it lost Holman, and the
borrowing habit, and the chronic deficit, and
the grand old foreign debt.
One of Premier Holman’s problems is the
finding of work for the people. Just at pre-
sent wheat-growing doesn’t pay by reason
of the drought. Also, wheat-growing never
pays well enough to allow of Sydney trades-
union hours and wages —partly because the
price of the article is fixed in a foreign,
cheap-labor market, partly by reason of the
congestion of the railways which lead to the
only port at which export is allowed, and
partly because of the awful congestion on the
wharves at that port. Also, the Government
has always alleged that it has no great area
of wheat land available, and its attitude in
this matter has been very rough on the
humble toiler who wanted a scrap of soil.
Furthermore, Premier Holman doesn’t know
how to grow wheat. Incidentally, he hasn’t
got the money for the great experiment. And,
as a side issue, wheat-growing isn’t on the
party programme, while decentralisation and
railway construction and the public owner-
ship of land are. If the Holman conglomer-
ation resumed a great area about
Port Stephens and Twofold Bay, where
land is always cheap, and is now even
cheaper than usual by reason of the drought
and the depression; and built two scraps or
railway and some wharves, and promised
that the hew settlements wouldn’t be killed
Sydney influence as Boydtown was, it
could start & pair of cities- that tvould rival
Sydney without doing Sydney any harm.
That is to say, it could lease the city sites,
and then sit back and collect the rents. Ike
experiment would cost about half the amount
which is required for the State wheat-
growing dodge. It would develop a large
extent of country that is now dead, or there-
abouts, and practically double the productive
area of the State, It couldn’t be killed by
drought. It would be permanent, and wheat
ish’t: The profits would increase year by
year, whereas wheat is here to-day and gone
tb-morroW. Eveh HolmAN; however little lie
mfly know about agriculture or eVen about
law; is capable of collecting rents, whefi lie
has an agent to do the work fflr him. This
matter is suggested to a Government which
wants to find new sources of revenue as
something worthy of consideration, assuming
that the Government isn’t too deeply com-
mitted to the policy of doing everything that
isn’t on its programme and nothing that is
on its programme.
* * * *
A lot of advice similar to the above might
be addressed to the other State Premiers of
Australia. Holman is specially selected
because he is less likely to profit by wise
counsel than any other politician except per-
haps the awful and impossible Scadpan, of
Westralia; and Scaddan doesn’t signify. In
other words, llolman receives special atten-
tion aS the most hopeless case on record. —
Yours, etc., Solomon Malaprop.
THE FIVE DISSENTIENTS.
Five members of the House of Representa-
tives— Anstey, Burns, Catts, McGrath
and Yates —voted against the proposal to
grant £lOO,OOO in aid of our burned-out
Belgian allies on the ground that the money
is required to help unemployed folk in Aus-
tralia. It seems a sorrowful business, and
a seriohs reflection oil the borrowing habit,
or the semi-Freetrade habit; or on something,
that this country should be suffering from
lack of employment. It is mentioned in its
own Federal statistics as a place which pro-
duces alunite, antimony, asbestos, bismuth,
coal, copper, diamonds, gold, gypsum, iron,
kaolin, lead, manganese, molybdenite, opal,
platinum, radium, scheelite, shale, silver, tin,
wolfram, zinc and other gems and minerals
too numerous to mention. In the matter of
gold alone it has dug out nearly £600,000.000
worth at one time and another; and though
the business has somewhat faded, our land
still produces over one-tenth of the world’s
gold supply. Then there are the “magni-
ficent natural resources” of which so much
has been heard, and said, and even blithered.
Australia has more land per inhabitant than
any other white man’s country on earth, and
land is supposed to be something of an asset.
It is the boss wool region, and meat region,
and hide and skin region. It has a vast
amount of cheap water power in Tasmania,
and North Queensland, and the Northern
Territory, and Westralia, and Victoria, and
in the southern part of New South Wales,
though very little use is made of the power.
In fact, so far as regards the southern part
of New South Wales, it was decided by a
crowd of parish-minded legislators that the
water-power was a curse and a thing to be
avoided, lest great manufactures should arise
a: the Australian capital. So the capital site
was shifted to a place where there is no
water power, in order that it might be a dead
loss, and a constant tax on the loan funds,
and its manufactures might not interfere
with the importing habit.
* * * *
Australia hasn’t been struck by a single
shell. It hasn’t paid an indemnity, or seen
one alderman hanged by the invaders, nor
has Boggabri been burned, or Mayor Tunics,
of Dandaloo, been held as a hostage, or fined
2s. 6d. or disembowelled. The “natural
resources” being as good as ever, and the
people as good as ever, and foreign competi-
tion in manufactures having been wholly
knocked out as regards Belgium and Ger-
many and Austria, and partly knocked out
in the case of Britain, France and Russia;
also the demand for foodstuffs and other raw
materials being almost unprecedented,
because most of our rival wheat-growers are
out shooting one another, this should be our
great time. Australia ought to be fattening
on other people’s misfortunes, like the wolves
which follow the Russian armies because
they have heard that there is dead German
lunch ahead. Possibly the difference lies in
the fact that the wolf doesn’t export raw
material, or import manufactured goods, or
depend on foreign loans. He is a self-con-
tained animal, as Australia would be save
for its Freetraders and loan-mongers and
importers and anti-immigrationists. He is
ready to take advantage of any chance that
turns up.
There are five Representatives, as already
mentioned, who declare that this allegedly
great and wonderful country, which has
produced £600,000,000 in gold within the
lifetime of people who are not yet quite dead,
and has little to show for it save £300,000,000
of public debt and a very considerable
amount of private debt, can’t spare £lOO,OOO
to aid a friend who has sacrificed his coal-
scuttle and his shirt and even his infant for
a cause in which the existence of Australia
is largely involved. An inquiry ought to be
held into the exporting and importing and
foreign-loan disease, or any other disease,
which has brought Australia to this pass.
A large and peaceful and very rich country,
which is capable of producing and manu-
facturing everything required by man and
woman shouldn’t slump at such short notice.
If the five dissentients had said that the
trifling sum shouldn’t be paid that would be
a different matter. But to say that a whole
continent can’t afford to pay it is a reflection
on our management of things. There are
five continents in this world —or six, if North
and South America are reckoned separately.
Unless this is the worst-governed place of
the lot the inference is that the whole world
can’t raise £600,000 even when all its hopes
and prospects are at stake.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Federal Notes and Cheers.
IS view of the fact that a New South Wales
Labor organisation recently suggested that
the Australian Government should finance
its war troubles by a greatly enlarged issue
of paper money, this item, from the State
Parliamentary reports, is specially interest-
ing :
SOLDIERS’ PAY.
Colonel Onslow called attention to the fact that
members of the expeditionary force were being paid
with Commonwealth notes, which were not legal
tender outside Australia. He suggested that they
should be paid in gold; (CHEERS.)
The Premier suggested that the representations
should be made tb the Commonwealth Government.
It was no part Of their function to deal with Federal
actions;
The soldiers haven’t started yet (or at least
they hadn’t started when the Colonel
made his few remarks). They are
spending their pay in Australia at present,
and a great many of them will presumably
leave with their wives and families and rela-
tives any surplus pay which they may have
on hand when they do start, or else deposit
in the Savings Bank the fragment which
remains. If any soldier wants to take all
his pay with him, so that some German
looter may find it in his clothes when he
perishes at the storming of Berlin, he can
easily change his Australian notes into gold
before he gets away, or he may even change
them on board ship. Onslow's appeal is
simply one more of the hundred devices
which are employed by Tory politicians and
writers and papers, to cast discredit on an
Australian financial scheme which doesn’t
involve foreign borrowing and the paying of
heavy interest to foreign capitalists—many
of them Germans. It is almost the only
financial scheme of the sort which Australia
possesses, and it appears to be specially hated
by Toryism in consequence. The problem
stated by Onslow is said to have been greeted
with “(CHEERS)”. The names of the
people who cheered are not given, but if
Kaiser William had been present he would
assuredly have cheered also. Likewise he
would probably have thrown his spiked hel-
met into the air, and wouldn’t have cared
one pfennig if it had never come down dgaiit.
The loss of a spiked helmfet wofild Have been
ft smdll matter in the circumstances. A
stronger man than Premier Holman, ana a
taan with a wider Australian outlook, might
possibly have told Onslow not only that the
matter of the Australian forces didn’t con-
cern an obscure State politician, but that
it was a sample of darned impudence to
interfere with it, and that to interfere on
the anti-Australian side was a very unwise
proceeding at this juncture. He might even
have explained to Onslow that Australian
gold is no more legal tender in the countries
where the war is raging than Australian
paper, and that this fact made his veiled
attack on the solvency of the Commonwealth
note look like plain malice or plain ignor-
ance. But Holman isn’t a strong man.
Kingston, when he was at his best, might
have said something to Onslow which
would have made that person flee into the
backblocks and take up a selection on the
Lower Darling under the name of Jones, in
order to conceal his identity.
«
The “Conscription” Principle Comes
Into its Kingdom.
The war has killed or half-killed a number
of Conservative causes. Freetrade —the in-
sanely altruistic policy which has done so
much to build up Germany’s strength at the
expense of the British Empire—has few
defenders left. The citizen who now had a
contemptuous word to fling at the Australian
Fleet, would be regarded, even in the Tori.est
club, as an imbecile as well as a disloyalist.
But the most utterly stultified “Liberal” of
the lot is the anti-“conscriptionist.” Who
would have imagined, a few months ago, that
by October 12, 1914, Sydney Evening News
would be discoursing in this fashion? —
Belgium has suffered because of its lack of compul-
sory and universal service....
It has also suffered because Britain has neglected
to arm her whole manhood.... However, the lesson
that ought to have been learned long ago is being
learned now.
As regards the last sentence, the trouble is
that, owing to the self-satisfied blitherings of
papers like the News, compulsory and uni-
versal service has been delayed perilously
long in Australia, and is still waiting to be
born in the United Kingdom. An army takes
a deal of making. The most difficult problem
which faces the organisers is neither tc
collect the rank and file nor to train them,
but to manufacture the officers. The fact
that the Germans have special corps of
sharp-shooters to pick off their enemy’s
officers amounts to a recognition on their
part that the latter are more or less irre-
placeable. One officer is needed, roughly,
to 30 men, so that about 16,000
officers will be required for Kitchener’s
proposed army of half a million. Now, a
modern officer is as much a specialist as a
doctor or a lawyer. He must have a heap
of mathematical and scientific" knowledge;
he must be acquainted with aeroplanes and
their possibilities, also motor-cars and
-bicycles, ordinary bicycles and balloons. He
must know something about the instal-
lation and working of field telegraphs
and telephones. He must be able to
help, when needed, in the direction
of clothing and feeding his men. And
all this is in addition to the technical
information which he is required to absorb
as a matter of course, and it is in addition,
also, to the indispensable knack of handling
men in a social way. It is not astonishing,
in all the circumstances, that a couple of
years’ hard training is required to transform
the intelligent civilian into one who is com-
petent to lead troops in the field. There is
no profit to be got from moaning, in the
News’ fashion, over the might-have-been.
Plainly it never can be now, and the next
best thing for the British Empire is, accord-
ingly, to get a move on where it was admit-
tedly remiss in the past. There is a com-
fortable theory prevalent in most of our
great public-houses that after the horror now
raging Bellona will go out of the business.
It is an idea which always has existed when
war raged with unusual violence, and it has
always proved fallacious and always must
so. long as man remains combative and


acquisitive. The next imitation of Arma-
geddon may be provided by China against
the Caucasian races.' The development may
be a long way off, but since it is the imme-
morial tendency of the Asiatic to surge west-
v. ards, the world is bound to see it some day.
In that dark period the Anglo-Saxon who
knows as little of the art of w r ar as the
average member of the race does to-day, will
wish he were dead. And the chances are
that his Avish will soon be gratified.
Capturing Germany’s Trade.
J. M. Joshua, of Melbourne, heaved this
solemn truth at the Yic. Chamber of Manu-
factures the other day :
All this talk about varied and elaborate methods
or capturing- Germany’s trade is so much waste,
mere is only one natural, direct and effective way
ot capturing Germany’s, or any other country’s, trade.
instance, Australia wanted to capture the
piano-making trade—if it wanted to make pianos
instead of importing the £300,000 worth it annually
akes frorn Germany—the only thing necessary was
to have sufficient tariff protection, and the rest would
tolJow. Inquiries by commissions, public discussions
and academic discourses are merely talking round the
subject and touching it nowhere.
For the moment the war has indirectly given
Australia’s manufacturers and prospective
manufacturers a prohibitive tariff on many
lines of merchandise. But that advantage
is not going to last for ever. The din of
battle will not have died down before Ger-
many will be making superhuman efforts to
its lost business in Australia, as
elsewhere. Sentimental considerations won’t
be a bar to that aim. They never are in
such circumstances. At present, of course, a
man wouldn’t be seen dead buying a Benz
motor-car. Human nature is so painfully
trail, however, that within a month of the
ending of the present horror the same citizen
would not look at an F.N. —which used to
be the product of martyred Belgium—if lie
were offered an equally good Benz for a ten-
pound note less. If Australia really wants
to hit Germany good and hard —if it wants
to deal that arrogant land a blow that will
hurt in all the years to come—it will best do
it by bumping up its tariff to the exclusion
or pianos, motor-cars and all other goods
that are of Teuton extraction. The more it
helps to build up its own industries, the
® or e it will be helping the harassed old land
to which it owes so much money; and the
fewer industrial opportunities it leaves to
Germany, the harder it will be for that
nation to arise out of the mud of its own
making. The institution of a genuine Pro-
tective policy is good patriotism as well as
good business at the present juncture.
Party Government and the War.
It is fairly clear now that the French and
British Governments have for years past
been waiting for trouble from Germany with-
out taking adequate measures to prevent it,
or making full preparations to meet it when
it came. They had the information; but
Party considerations deterred them from
recofnmending sufficient expenditure in mili-
tary armaments. Britain’s Government
certainly contrived to get Dreadnoughts built
on the old “rule-the-waves” ticket; but Lord
Roberts’s bitter cry for more soldiers was
ostensibly treated by the Asquitii Govern-
ment as the vaporing of a false-alarmist,
because Ministers were not prepared to adopt
it as a party cry. When war broke out the
full strength of the Government’s knowledge
was revealed, in the astounding announce-
ment that Britain would have to send an ex-
peditionary force across the Channel. “To
defend the neutrality of Belgium,” thought
the man in the street; and the truth was
not out till General French’s expedition
landed at Boulogne. After that came
Kitchener’s explanation of what the War
Office hoped to do, his talk of the Allies im-
proving their position as time went on, prac-
tically giving away the fact that France
then needed a lot more army than it could
put in the field, and that every little aiding
force from anywhere would be necessary to
save Britain from an extension of the Ger-
man menace to the Belgian, Dutch and French
coast line. If Kitchener’s policy speech
left the denser section of the public in
doubt as to how matters stood, the bellicose
German press soon cleared the doubt away.
At first, everybody but the Governments had
been taken by surprise. Thev knew what
was coming—or, at any rate, they knew
something about it. The occupation of Bel-
gium, the kerosene-sprinklers for conflagra-
tion purposes (intended for France, but
prematurely brought to light in poor little
Belgium), the storage of cement by genial
Teutonic residents of the countries to be
invaded —these were things which perhaps
they didn’t know. Had they known every-
thing, not even their precious party politics
might have kept them dumb to the truth.
As it was, the world was allowed to remain
in pitiable ignorance of the inevitable. Know-
ing 20 years ago what they know to-day of
Germany’s purpose in militarism and
navalism (which purpose hasn’t altered one
jot in the meantime), France and Belgium
and Britain should have forced the Bully
to stop, or fight the matter out there and
then. It could have been done. Party
politics would have been subordinated to
patriotic considerations, as they now are,
had the nervous Governments talked blunt
common-sense to the nations, instead of fool-
ing along in the old diplomatic way. The
case against Germany was something to be
frankly stated in a stern, official style; and
if it had been stated, the Governments that
knew what must come if Germany was
allowed to go on preparing for Napoleonic
conquest would have been simply driven to
give the Kaiser the option between reason-
able disarmament or mortal combat within
three months. But Party Government feared
to take the risk —feared to appeal to
national common-sense, for the reason that an
unscrupulous Opposition was certain to have
raised a howl. Now, when Germany’s un-
checked militarism has got to work, Party
Government can only tearfully protest that
the same thing shall not be permitted to
occur again. But it will occur again, and
will go on occurring so long as ever Party
Government prevails.
—— ♦ ——
The Latest Anti-Referenda Argument.
Sydney Telegraph, being a Liberal journal,
which is to say one whose chief aim is to
delay legislative change of any sort for as
long as possible, is opposed to a‘ re-sub-
mission of the Referenda questions by the
Australian Parliament. It has dropped its
old arguments about the horrors of Unifica-
tion and the infamy of an arrangement
under which every fruit-barrow in the Com-
monwealth may be nationalised. These
belong to the dead and buried days when
the Australian Defence policy was claimed
to be a manifestation of disloyalty to
Britain. The very latest anti-Referenda
gag runs this way:—
The war is still in progress, its upshot still for the
future to decide. The people are deeply preoccupied
with affairs of vital, significance to them, and are
neither inclined nor able to give the necessary atten-
tion to local political discussion.
Australia has just elected a Parliament for
three years, on party lines, in face of Kit-
chener’s intimation that the war may be
going strong when the Parliament in ques-
tion is dead. Kitchener seems to have
mentioned three years, because it was de-
cided to ask volunteers to sign on for that
period—the average volunteer, no matter
how patriotic, being averse from becoming
a soldier for an indefinite time. The war
may, however, last much longer. Wars
have endured for 30 and even 100 years.
The Telegraph, in the article under notice,
discusses what would happen “if the war
should result adversely to Great Britain.”
But the basic explanation of the stolid calm-
ness which is noticeable all over the British
Empire is the conviction that the war can’t
result adversely to Britain. Even though
Belgium were wiped off the map tempor-
arily, and France were beaten to its knees
and forced to stop, and Russia were bought
off, Britain would still have to go on while
the Germans have a footing anywhere
between Cherbourg and the Zuyder Zee.
Britain’s sea power is at stake ; and what-
ever happens on the Continent, Germany’s
deepwater trade is going to be ruined, and
Germany’s cautious navy is going to be har-
ried tirelessly, till Britain’s supremacy is
established. Even if Germany were not
likely to be exhausted, financially and other-
wise, before its troubles on land come to
an end, its chance of. coping with the
British navy is an almost pathetically slim
one. There is, nevertheless, every . indica-
tion that it means to try its luck in this
direction some day, and the whole situation
points to a lengthy as well as a desperate
struggle. _ And all that Liberalism has to
suggest, in the circumstances, is that Aus-
tralia, after it has despatched as many
troops to the scene of bloodshed as it can
afford, shall subside inertly into an arm-
chair and look on! The fact is, Australians
have for the last two months allowed them-
selves to become “deeply preoccupied with
affairs of vital significance,” far more than
is nationally healthy. Special war editions
of the newspapers, and frenzied military dis-
cussions in bars are permissible enough for
a while, but no rational country wants to
make a habit of them. It would be all the
better for Australia if it was encouraged
to turn its attention a little from the war,
in which it can’t assist much more than it
has done, and focus the same on its own
local business. Such a move would be all
the better in the long run for Britain* too.
Half a dozen years ago the Commonwealth,
snurning the protests of Liberalism, insti-
tuted local defence, and Britain has gained
immensely thereby. It has now an oppor-
tunity to institute a system of commercial
defence, and if it seizes' the chance one of
the chief winners will again be the United
Kingdom. The Australian people will help
very effectively in that direction by giving
an affirmative answer to the Government’s
Referenda proposals. As a natural out-
come of the upheaval in Europe, the TJ.S.
Trusts have already started to butt in and
collar Australian markets. If Australia
remains "deeply preoccupied” with the' war,
to the exclusion of “local political discus-
sion,” the business left for our relatives and
allies on the other side of the earth will be
hardly _ worth mentioning. The sort of
patriotism which consists in reading the war
news and then giving three cheers for King
and Empire, is easy and typically Liberal'.
The Labor way is more strenuous, and less
thrilling _ and picturesque. It consists,
roughly, in starting, without delay, to build
up local industries, in which Britishers may
get jobs, and in discouraging the institution
in Australia of U.S. Trusts, whose opera-
tions are aimed impartially against the
world —Britain included. It is to be hoped
the Australian' Government speedily givx's
the electors the chance to show which variety
of Imperial patriotism they prefer.
The Birth-rate Bogey Again.
“Henry Horsecollar” writes:—
In 1851 it is estimated that what was then France
and what is now Germany were about equal in
population, so far as the rather inaccurate statistics
of the time went. Since then, France has grown
larger by the annexation of a bit of what was once
Sardinia, and on the other hand it has grown smaller
by the loss of considerable territory to Prussia. On
the whole, it has become smaller. Germany has
become larger by reason of annexations from France
and Denmark —at least, Prussia, the boss German
State, and the one which owns the capital and the
emperor, has grown much larger, for it took practi-
cally all the annexations as its own share of the
loot. Bavaria gained a scrap of territory, but the
humbler German States of Baden, Wurtemberg and
Saxony were all robbed by the glorious Prussian, and
he took Hanover and some minor German princi-
palities bodily. Prussia had 18,000,000 people in
1861, and no less than 40,000,000 in 1910. The
German Empire had 65,000,000 people in 1910,
while France, the alleged land of the “empty cradle,”
had less than 40,000,000. France had dropped
behind badly in the race for overcrowding, and suffo-
cation and dependence on imported food supplies.
It was quoted by fools as a horrible example of race
suicide, though its population kept increasing—but
very slowly.
Yet France, where the people have room to breathe,
is putting up a much better fight against Germany
than it did in 1870-71, when the numbers were more
equal. The Belgians, who occupy the most crowded
country in Europe except Saxony, have put up a very
decent engagement. The English, who come next
in the matter of sleeping on top of one another, have
done well so far as they have gone. So have the
Irish, whose hungry land is steadily losing popula-
tion. Ditto the Scottish Highlanders, whose country
has been growing more empty for many years. The
Russians, with the most thinly-peopled land in
Europe, and about the biggest birth-rate in Europe,
have knocked large holes in things. One way and
another, it is hard to decide whether the- dense
population or the empty cradle is the best military
element. '
On the present appearance of things, the Allies
are going to win. They include, as already hinted,
the country with the second thickest population;
the country which ranks third in density; the one
white man’s country on earth which is steadily
losing population; the notorious land of the empty
cradle; and the most thinly-peopled land in Europe,
which is also the land with the biggest birth-rate.
These confused facts are reiterated lest the reader
should lose the run of them. On the other hand,
the Austro-German enemy includes in its ranks the
country with the very thickest population ; the land
with the second biggest birth-rate, and the land-with
the second-largest number of hogs to the square mile
—also the one with the largest production of beer.
The greatest hog-raising country in proportion to its
area is on our side, also the one with the highest
death-rate.
The weary old population problem has a poor show
amid these conflicting elements, but two things are
certain: Austria and Germany are better qualified
for a big war than they were 40 years ago because
they have far more people. And they are much
worse fitted for a long war than they were 40' years
ago because their population has increased so greatly
that it can no longer feed itself, and anybody who can
cut off the food imports can starve two empires out.
To-day the army of the nation with the thinnest
population and the biggest birth-rate is putting up
a great fight to junction with the forces of the
nation which has the biggest death-rate and the
greatest number of pigs to a certain allowance of
space. And if they meet at Budapest it will be
rough on the country that is ruled by the Oldest
Inhabitant (if he isn’t dead), and the adjacent coun-
try which is ruled by the Maddest Inhabitant.
Thereupon the country with the thickest population
will probably desert its ruler, the Maddest Inhabi-
tant ; after which anything may happen. Hereabouts
the writer grows weary of complication and reitera-
tion, and of the falling hog-rate and the empty
pig-sty, and he goes out to drink some steak and
onions.

Rahab and Co.
The annual reports of Dr. W. E. Jones,
Victorian Inspector-General of Insane, and
Dr. Gamble, of the Ivew Asylum, are docu-
ments that ought to have more attention
than they will get. Last year, according to
Dr. Jones, the registered - lunatics in the
State increased by 157; and, as “many more
cases of congenital defect than was previ-
ously suspected are caused by the inherit-
ance of the syphilitic taint, what is more
to be desired than a cure for general
paralysis is a means for the prevention of
venereal disease.” Dr. Gamble, for his
part, declared that "“too much importance
has been .-attached to alcoholism as a cause
of insanity.” The opinions of these experts,
taken in conjunction, constitute an interest-
ing commentary on the public attitude to-
wards two brands of evil. The drink evil
has been more overworked by amateur socio :
legists than any other. It has been pointed
to as the cause of poverty and disease when
more often it has beeh merely the result of
these ; things. Alcoholism may produce
wasters, but, except in rare cases, it does
not injure the health of innocent persons,
Yet the drink curse can be sold only in
places that are licensed, under conditions
that are strict and numerous, and the person
who transgresses these conditions is in
danger of having taken from him the means
by which he lives. On the other hand, the
much more terrible syphilis curse is almost
boycotted by social reformers ; they not only
refrain from grappling with it, but often
even decline to recognise its existence. The
idea that brothels should be licensed the same
as pubs is howled down, on the alleged
ground that this would be “giving official
recognition to sin” —as though sin would
worry if it wasn’t recognised. Bung is
compelled to supply his clients with pure
beer; but neither the Pure Foods Act, the
Thistles Act, the Hackney Carriages Act
or any other old measure compels Rahaß
to take similar precautions. If Lais be-
haves herself in an objectionable manner
she has not a license which can be endorsed,
and therefore she is in no danger of losing
her living; at the worst, she is shooed into
another street or suburb to enlarge the area
of her evil influence. And the reason of
this policy, it seems, is that Rahab, being
very liable to be a genuine, nasty, insanitary
curse, the ecclesiastical Oiiadband and
political Stiggins alike shrink from dealing
with her, preferring to devote their atten-
tion to a picturesoue, melodramatic and
largely-bogus curse like the Demon Derink. '
«
“Hexham” takes a cheerful view : : —
My theory, is that the German-Austrian resistance
will collapse as suddenly and unexpectedly as that of
the Confederate States, in 1865.
Though very hard pushed during the siege of
Paris in 1870-71 —so hard pushed indeed that only
the release of Frederick Charles’s army on the fall
of Metz and its 30-miles-a-day march to Paris saved
the raising of the siege—Germany never had much
over a million soldiers in France. This was about
one in 40 of the population at the time, exclusive of
home garrisons. If it be true .that William has now
put into the field on the two war frontiers 4l
millions, one in 16 of the inhabitants must have been
sent to the front. To keep that army afloat for three
or four months another million men must be found.
Can he do it? No modern civilised manufacturing and
commercial nation has ever maintained under arms
even for a very short period one in 16 of its popu-
lation, and one in 13 seems quite out of
the question. On the other hand, while
France must experience a similar difficulty in
maintaining its strength in the field, Russia’s legions
are practically innumerable; and Britain, with the
help of the dominions and India, can for a long time
continue sending detachments across the Channel or
through the French Mediterranean ports. Also, while
the Allies (bar Belgium) have free communication
with the world outside, and can, to a large extent,
carry „ their ordinary industries, Germany and Aus-
tria are absolutely cut off, except for what little busi-
ness can be done through Holland and Switzerland.
The Germans are supposed to be a stubborn lot;
but the collapse of Prussia after the single battle of
Jena-Auerstadt in 1806 has no parallel in history
for many hundreds of years. That of France after
Sedan was nothing to it.
Finally, the Prussian is nowhere loved in the
Fatherland outside of his own territory, least of all
in Bavaria, and one German in every 10 is a
Bavarian. Is it so unlikely that, in the event of
the Germans being forced back over their own fron-
tiers, in a war originating purely with the Prussian
king and the Prussian military caste, the other
States, indignant at the failure of the Hohenzol-
lerns and their clique, might secede and form a
South-German Confederation, possibly in conjunction
with the Teutonic (and likewise Catholic) provinces
of Austria, which seems on the eve of falling to
pieces ?
Much correspondence has appeared of late
in the Sydney papers about the neglect of
the Cook and Fisiiek Governments to hurry
on with the construction of the Australian
capital at Canberra. Unfortunately Can-
berra is its own worst enemy. It lacks the
coal and water-power which might make it a
great inland manufacturing centre. It can’t
be a gold town like Bendigo or a silver town
like Broken Hill. It isn’t a seaflort, and
though there is a seaport attached to it the
Federal authority has such a very humble
scrap of ground there that it can only hope
to build a sort of fishing village. The place
has no special advantages in the way of
agriculture. If Canberra promised, either
by reason of mines or manufactures or com-
merce, to be a great dividend-paying propo-
sition, the Government’s financial embarrass-
ments would make it hustle all the harder
with the construction job. Being regarded
as a mere luxury it is one of the matters
that are liable to be dropped in a time of
stress.
By “Solomon Malapeop” :
Melbourne begins to realise the glory of the im-
porting disease. The very old and inadequate Yan
Yean reservoir holds 26ft. of water when it is full,
but one miserable day last week it wasn’t half-full. In
fact, it contained only lift, of water, some of which
is mud. The subsidiary Maroondah supply was fading
very fast. Melbourne realised some time ago that it
must get an additional water supply, and the Board
of Shirks set to work manfully. First of all, it decided
to import the pipes, for it is the whole gospel of the
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Shirks that it is
better to pay 6d. to the foreigner than get a better
article made locally for 6d., or even sd. Whether
any hangcr-on of the-Shirks gets a commission from
the foreigner, or whether the whole thing is merely
an expression of hatred for Australia and Australian
industry, no one seems to know. So a number of
Australians lost the jobs which they ought to have
had at making the pipes. And, as a sort of side
issue, they are to be taxed to pay interest on the
loan that pays for the foreign pipes which were
ordered so that the taxpayer who pays the interest
might lose employment. Now the war has inter-
rupted the delivery of the foreign pipes, and Mel-
bourne, after losing a good job, and incurring a big
interest liability, began to see a dry spell in its
dreams. Still, it serves Melbourne right. A
place that allows its rulers to tax it so that it may
be dry and jobless deserves to be jobless and dry.
Mater Dolorosa.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Over the sea there comes,
More clear than trumpet sound —
And deadlier than the drums,
An awful Voice profound :
A Voice I must obey,
That bids me haste and prepare,
And send, by night and by day,
All that is mine, nor spare
Even to the ultimate life,
The last of goods and gear;
Dread is the Voice I hear,
Above (he noise of strife,
Above the cannon roar
It echoes round and round
To Earth’s remotest bound—
Hark !. . . .
More ! More ! More !
My sons ! My gallant sons—- v
My well-loved sons are there!
The unseen, ravening guns,
Each in a hidden lair,
With swift, unpitying stroke
Smite from above, beneath—
There the line reeled and broke;
My sons went down to death
There , where the shrapnel tore
Body and soul apart
O God—my heart, my heart!
Hark !. .. .
More! More! More!
Neither by day nor night
The sacrifice may cease;
They are flung forth to the fight
For a hopeless world’s release —
For the lifting of heavy loads—
For. the breaking of many gyves,
By alien fields and roads
My sons lay down their lives
Where the whistling bullets pour,
Where the vengeful bayonet gleams,
Where the stricken charger screams—
Hark!
More! More! More!
Anguish, famine and thirst—
Heat, rain and gripping cold —
Bolts that in thunder burst,
Blasting the strong, the bold.
Back to the primal clay,
Never so red as now!
Who taketh my sons away,
Great God of Battles, but Thou?
The spirit that moves and lives,
The essence that is Thy breath
One resolute Purpose gives
Which leads them down to —Death :
In them. I do but restore
Thy gifts— yet, shall I rejoice
At the Voice which is Thy Voice?
Hark !. . . .
More ! More! More !
Surely the end shall come
Though it be strange and far?
Shall not the guns be dumb
Under the morning-star
Of some new-risen Day,
And Peace resume her sway?
But.till that golden hour
Gilds the dark world anew,
Battle and Death devour
The brave, the kind, the true—
ihose whom Thou didst endue
With courage, strength and pride,
So that like gods they grew;
„ So , that they heard and died.
xea ! even as I now hear,
Over the trumpets clear—
Over the bellowing guns.
Sounding from shore to shore,
The insatiate Voice of War
Calling my noble sons.
Hark !. . . .
More! More! More!
N.s.w.
O. C. CABOT.

“The Gondoliers” was revived at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night. One
of the dailies wrote of the “maintenance
of the best traditions of Savoy opera,” and
of the setting of “a perfect standard,” and
so forth; and other sheets blithered along
similar lines. Such talk is flagrant non-
sense. The production is fair to middling,
and that is all there is to it. The wigs
in Act 11. look as though the moths had been
at them, and the stern, controlling hand of
the economist is visible in other directions.
Edward McKeown, who plays Marco, has an
exceptionally sweet and powerful tenor, but
he is two or three stone overweight for gon-
doliering purposes, and his acting isn’t good
enough to make one forget the anomaly. Miss
Ruth Lincoln (Gianetta) is another who
always sings acceptably, but her mannerisms
rob her of some of her charm. The star per-
former of the production is Charles AValenn,
who plays the Duke. He is a sound com-
edian, with a better voice than most of his
kind, and the knack of clear articulation.
Frank AVilson is a satisfactory Guiseppe, and
the same observation applies to Miss Dorothy
Vane (Tessa). G. Villiers Arnold (Luiz)
and Albert Kavanagh (the Inquisitor).
Miss Pearl Ladd is an attractive and tuneful
Casilda, and Miss Ethel Morrison, as the
Duchess, is everything that is graceful and
attractive. The chorus, though homely for
the most nart, does the sparkling Sullivan
music justice.
* .-[: *
“Lord and Lady Algy,” with which the
Iluckler-Paget company opened its new sea-
son at the Little Theatre, Sydney, on Satur-
day, belongs to the innocuous type of frivolous
comedy that was popular a decade ago. The
chief items in this concoction of R. C.
Carton’s are: A hero, belonging to the use-
less aristocratic class, who gambles and
drinks too much, but never plays up with
women; and a heroine of the same class,
who, in her. flirtations, always manages to
keep herself out of danger. Both are, of course,
at heart, real good sorts. Then there are the
inevitable strange woman found on the
hero’s immaculate premises ; an incongruous
assortment of caricatured aristocrats; and
the misunderstandings that arise from the
presence of the strange female in the wrong
place. You are not expected to take any-
body seriously ; all you need do is to laugh.
In fact, “Lord and Lady Algy” has about as
much solidity in its plot as the average
musical play. Also, it is quite as amusing.
Lord Algy and his wife are separated. They
like each other, but they back dif-
ferent horses and smoke different brands of
cigarettes. Algy’s brother is enamored over
a Mrs. Tudway, and prevails upon Algy,
who has apparently no reputation to lose,
to allow him the use of his rooms to meet
the lady. Of course, Lady Algy, and also
Tudway, see the lady’s photograph in Algy’s
rooms and suspect the worst. There is,
necessarily, a costume ball, in the second act,
where Algy innocently further compromises
himself with Mrs. Tudway. The trouble is
that Algy arrives at this ball drunk —not
disgustingly drunk, for that would be “A
Fool There Was”; not merely pretending to
be drunk, for that would be “David Gar-
rick.” He is just humorously drunk.
According to the principles of polite comedy
a decade ago, Algy is allowed to pervade the
ballroom, insult the guests, and generally
make himself an hilarious nuisance. As
lohg as he is a humorous nuisance neither the
dramatist nor the audience nor the guests
really mind. In the third act Mrs. Tudway
once more takes refuge in Algy’s rooms ; but
her presence there is explained away by Lady
Afgy, with a lie that satisfies everybody. She
is‘a Good Sort, you see. So is Algy. He
hqs already disspaded Mrs. Tudway from her
projected elopement with his brother. There
is a host of other characters, but they don’t
much matter except to decorate the stage—
rather angularly and awkwardly—in the ball-
room scene. The arrangement of this scene
on such a small stage is, however, a triumnh
of management.
* * * *
Hugh Buckler is at his ease in a highly
congenial part. His occasional moments of
seriousness are given their full dramatic
value, and his antics, while drunk, are
sources of innocent merriment. A pity
some dramatist does not write him a play in
which he could be politely and ludicrously
drunk all through. Violet Paget is also ad-
mirably suited in a part that does not call
for any but the lighter emotions. Gerald K.
Souper supplies, as the elder brother, a wholly
delightful piece of character acting; but
Lillian Lloyd, as Mrs. Tudway, fails by not
placing sufficient stress upon the ultra-
romantic and sentimental temperament of the
character. She is much too matter-of-fact
for a “hazy dream.” Of the rest of the
long cast some are different, others indiffer-
ent ; but the comedy, as a whole, rattles on
pleasantly and amusingly enough, despite
the occasional weaknesses.
The stagecraft of “Seven Keys to Bald-
pate,” at Sydney Criterion, is full of interest-
ing, and in some cases daring, experiments.
On i of the most striking is the use made of
the one entrance to the inn. Every new
character must come through that door.
Everybody must open that door from the out-
side—and there is only one key to Baldpate.
By stressing this fact, the dramatist con-
trives to give to the shut door—with un-
known possibilities always just outside it —
an element of the highest dramatic suspense.
The mystery play continues to draw good
audiences even in these days.

At Sydney Palace “The Glad Eye” is
winking like a flagship’s Morse lamp. The
signals are understood even by the most
moral, and rural deans and curates are
understood to be clamoring round the box
office for special cheap rates. The Church,
with its unfailing instinct for economy, re-
fuses even.to revel at the usual prices.
Sydney, Adelphi, having put in a fortnight’s
good work dredging unhappy females out of
the mud, will now proceed to drop them in
again, so as to be able to repeat the dredg-
ing later on. Consequently, on Friday night,
it will remove “The Ever Open Door,” and
substitute “Driving a Girl to Destruction”—
this being reckoned a lightning chucker-of-
girls-back-into-the-mud. The process will
occupy six nights only. The previous record
is 25. It may be on account of the
war that there are more girls instantly
available for chucking. Or it may be that
the mud is handier.

Sydney Tivoli’s three new turns this week
are all in the superlative. Torino is the
“master juggler”; James Teddy is the
“world’s champion jumper” (and The Bul-
letin doesn’t question it J ; Miss Florence
Smithson is “The Queen of Song.” Melba,
by the way, has occasionally been accused of
this sovereignty, but the Tivoli settles that.
Personally. The Bulletin doubts whether
Miss Smithson is much more than a duchess.
Westony—the man who proves that the
piano really is a musical instrument—
Wright and Dietrich, and the Carpatii Bros,
are in their last nights; but Fatima, the
corkscrew and blanc-mange expert, and the
Four Boises are good for some time yet.

“Princess Caprice,” putting in a week at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.) to finish up the
season of the Royal Comics, strikes one as
being a good means to a weak end. The
first act has some more or less charming
music by Leo Fall —music of the modern
light German type, which is always sweet
but never distinctly original —and some
effective, grotesque comedy of J. Cannot.
The second act also is full of conventional
melody, but the comedy in this case is largely
shared by Phil Smith, whose giggle grows
gloomy. Still there is a reasonable strength
ol comic opera plot and a lot of ladies of
the Court in the second section, whereas
Act 111. is merely a poor excuse for an
interpolated dance and a shame-faced con-
fession that the plot has petered out. The
Prince-Regent of Thessalia is up against
the fact that the bailiff is in his place, and
salaries are five years in arrears. There-
fore he proposes to marry his niece to a
wealthy, elderly reigning idiot, in the adja-
cent principality, and get himself pensioned
off at great expense. The Princess loves
her music master —a crude, youthful bari-
tone with arms that seem to be growing out
of the sleeves of his little velvet jackets,
whilst the betrothed idiot loves the High
Chambermaid of his bride-elect, who is also
the Princess’s foster sister. Finally the
chambermaid's aged father, who resembles a
30-year-old cherub in a bleached wig, states
that the two young ladies were changed in
infancy; whereupon everything is vaguely
understood to come out right. Given a little
more magnificence and, in some ■ places, a
stronger cast, “Princess Caprice” should be
good for a popular revival in the future.
* * * *
J. Cannot’s joyous superficial fooling is
contrasted with the pretty sighing and sing-
ing of Miss Spain, whose voice, unfortu-
nately, is almost as small in proportion to
her character as the jackets and style of
Derek Hudson are disproportionate to his
manly baritone. Olive Godwin fills the bill as
the buxom singing chamberma'd," who •- is
rocked in the wrong cradle, and John RaL. ...
sings successfully in knee breeches throuc '
the cherubic-parent part. But the acting u
Ralston amounts to a polite negative.
Dorothy Brunton, as a saucy 'young angel
from Paris, happens providentially to be the
littlp sister of his Highness Phil Smith,
although he is supposed to be fully 40 years
her senior; and in this unaccountable char-
acter Dorothy requires nothing but a louder
singing capacity to make her a dream. Being
so very promising a soubrette she ought to
be granted a six months’ holiday, on double
salary, for the purpose of developing the
muscles of her voice. McCunn’s orchestra
did very nicely as a rule on the first night,
and the chorus gave every indication of being
likely to do better next time.
