Asche Ivayton, the sleepless detective,
appeared before a big house in “The Argyle
Case” at Melbourne Royal on Saturday.
Where Sherlock Holmes would have failed
by employing inferences and cocaine, Asche
succeeded in arresting some of the cleverest
crooks by merely keeping awake and burning
cigars. During the whole of the proceed-
ings he smoked, and his army of spies and
gentlemen helps did the same thing at every
opportunity. Between puffs, also, he blew
orders down the telephone, read countless
documents, and tore around with the activity
of a terrier hunting its tail. He took finger-
prints, tapped suspected conversation by dic-
tograph, sought direct evidence by every
cross-examining method, from sweet persua-
sion to plain bullying, and generally gave an
edifying spectacle of a unique and sagacious
Mind at work. It was all tremendous, and
when he had arrested a gang of counter-
feiters and found that the murderer of John
Argyle was a stout, discreditable Cent. One,
the audience felt that Tobacco-ash Ivayton
was sufficiently marvellous to give lessons
even to the Melbourne C.I.D.
* * * *
Charles A. Milward, as the eminent inves-
tigator, is pleasing in every phase, from the
smooth wooing of the heroine to the crema-
tion of the cigars. He is an astonishingly
convincing smoker, easily the best in the com-
petition. William Harrigan doesn’t waste
genius on the part of Joe Manning, but is
good enough; and Miss Sylvia Bremer, as
Mary Masuret, decorates the character with
all the ingenuousness of a maiden who lias
been well brought up. Miss Gertrude Bos-
well presents Mrs. Martin as a figure of
gloom and tragedy and, a poisoned past;
Robert Greig reaches stentorian heights as
James F. Hurley; and Gregan McMahon
plays a weird counterfeiter with a large,
intellectual forehead and more than a sug-
gestion of John Stofel returned from the
war. Miss Maggie Moore, Miss Ghiloni,
Miss Holloway, R. Whyte, junior, Carleton
Stewart, J. H. Nunn and others put satis-
factory work into minor parts.
Some nights last week Wirths’ put their
Circus into Melbourne Hippodrome building,
out of reach of the cold snap, nut on Satur-
day the show was back in the vast Sauca Torn,
whence the gentle perfume of the polar bears
and Copbants ascended to the star-spangled
tzure. The entertainment proceeds as
before. Albert Toulouse, the balancing
Frenchman, has not yet broken any part of
himself, neither has the dare-devil Diver met
lis death. The Hassans (late of Harry
Linder’s co.) are wire-walking in the same
old way, and Mrs. Berzac’s ponies continue
to maintain their fast and furious footing on
a platform which whizzes human intruders
into space. Also, the gymnasts, acrobats,
equestrians, clowns and beasts of prey are
doing their duty in those spheres of useful-
ness where it has pleased Wirth Bros, to
place them.

gramme is most conspicuous for “Lucille
Love” (fifth instalment) ; “Schooling Mary
Ann,” a tract on the virtues of simple
pleasures; and “Leeds of Chaos,” a frag-
ment of literature for the criminal classes.

Exit the German Band in Sydney. That
noisy institution, which has worried every
corner of the city three or four days a week,
has lately been asking for trouble so regu-
larly that the Inspector-General of Police
lias commanded it to go and get work. About
a week ago, because a harmless citizen didn’t
give it sixpence for playing “Rule Britannia”
it yelled Hoch der Kaiser” ; whereupon the
citizen ceased to be harmless and stoushed
as much of it as he could reach. Authority
of course, disapproved of that, and the citi-
zen was fined and had to pay for the damage
done to all the instruments. After that
nobody cared whether the band played “Rule
Britannia or was drowned. However, it
kept going, and one day last week it took
un its stand outside the Sydney German
Giub and blared out the Austrian National
Anthem. That was the dead-end. The
police, fearing that the next step of the
daring fools would be to fill the drum with
bombs or put a wireless station in the bass
trumpet, planted their foot down; and the
German Band, being underneath the foot at
the time, was broken up into 18 pieces
r> : see the Sydney German
Hands have been demobilised on the indict-
ment of a local barrister, named Teece, who
charged one of them with playing the Aus-
trian National Anthem in his hearing Sheer
braggadocio on Teece’s part! It is hard
enough to tell what a German Band is try-
ing to play under normal circumstances •
but when it comes to distinguishing between
Nationai Anthems (the Marseillaise and
the Chinese National uproar excepted) the
effort becomes hopeless. The odds are that
the air which Teece in his musicianly arro-
gance identified as the Austrian anthem was
some quaint old Choctaw folk-song or a new
ragtime melody. The Germans’ luck is dead
out these times.

“Fax”: Whilst St. George’s Hall (Melb.)
and its history are under discussion on
loverty Point, the new show-place on the
old site is being finally planned. The Hoyt
Co. has got a 30-years’ lease of a block of
land with the same frontage and depth as
the adjoining Theatre Royal property. On
this will be built a picture theatre, flanked
by a big shop on Bourke-street, and backed
by warehouses having their entrances in
Little Bourke. Architect William Pitt
reckons that five months will see the block
completed, times being favorable for uninter-
rupted work. Six months at the most should
suffice to fix up the theatre and warehouses
as going concerns. Meanwhile Hoyt’s has
to find £BO a week ground rent for the block
where the builders are at work, so Olympia
(over Princes Bridge) and the National (at
the top of Bourke-street) are pouring their
profits mostly down the sink, as it were.
Thusfar the Hoyt enterprise has prospered
wonderfully. On a recent Saturday the
audiences at the Olympia and the National
—matinees and night—totalled 8500. Who
said picture shows were played out?
Allen Doone, who has been strengthening
his banking account in Westralia, will take
Sydney into his confidence again on
November 7. His new play, “The Bold
Soger Boy, will be the first move.
.‘‘Melb’’: Our musical world is enraptured
With The Life of Wagner” made into a
picture. The representatives of Wagner,
Liszt, the King of Bavaria, Wagner’s wives
Minna and Cosima, Bulow, Wagner’s
banker-backer Funshel, and all the other
personages are just like we had been taught
to expect; and there is first-rate acting. Even
VV agner s three young children, by Cosima
are introduced—the two daughters and
sonny Siegfried with golden hair. The kid
is now Professor Wagner, who is over-
shadowed, like the sons of Goethe. (Deliver
us from a heavy father!) Cosima had two
daughters by Bulow. Then there was the
piebald one, fiercely claiming to be an illegi-
timate Wagner, while mother insists that she
is a legitimate Bulow. How irreconcilable
is the Artistic with the Bourgeois!
“N.L.”: Esther Mitchell, the young Aus-
tralian who plays Nang Ping in “Mr. Wu”
at Melbourne Princess’s, is likely to know
quite a lot about languages if things go on
as they have been doing lately. In “Wu”
she has several bits of genuine Chinese. In
“Who’s the Lady?” she was originally sup-
posed to be a French girl who had been
educated in Germany and had gradually
forgotten her own language. After she had!
learned the necessary German, the war-
stalked forth, and the school was judiciously-
shifted to Italy, which meant some study of
yet another language. A little earlier, in
“The Clash of Arms,” at Sydney Palace, she 1
had to speak English with French trim-
mings. Heaven only knows (unless perhaps;
Beaumont Smith shares the confidence)
what her next language will be. I can only
hope it isn’t Bowery American.
“Jeames’s” funeral oration :
If Germany had had a diplomatist as skilled and
painstaking’ as the Marquis di San Giuliano, who died
last week, its position would be a happier one
to-day. Wherever big Italian interests lay, there
San Giuliano went and looked into them on the
spot. In this way he came to investigate person-
ally, while he was Foreign Minister, the Balkans,
Tripoli, the Tr'entino, Albania and Erythrea. He
it was who inspired and did most to make a success
of the Tripolitan adventure which has meant so
much to Italy. To get an analogy to the case of
San Giuliano you have to picture Prince Lichnowsky,
the last German Ambassador in London, journeying
to Ulster and satisfying himself as to whether the
Nationalists were likely to turn against the British
Empire in the event of a European war. The aloof
Berliner preferred to rely on Tory newspaper lies,
and, as a result, his country fell in. The Germans
hated San Giuliano, and when he was first sent to
London, as Italian Ambassador, a queer plot, of
Teuton designed to discredit him morally
and socially, was undertaken. However, it misfired
amid a gale of laughter, as a similar Berlin scheme
to ruin young Freeman-Mitford (who married the
Friedlander-Fuld heiress) failed. The dead states-
man was a Sicilian aristocrat of French origin. An
ancestor in the dim past had been a Norman, and
San Giuliano bore an extraordinary resemblance to
the Due d’Orleans, who hopes one day to be King of
France.
Even in the worst times the idea of drag-
ging all Cornstalk affairs to Sydney is as
strong as ever. Premier Holman has pro-
pounded an inspiration about a canvas camp
on the outskirts of the city, where the
tammes of men who are working half-time
may be cheaply accommodated. And Jabez
Wright, ML A wro t e to “Dear Bill” at
Broken Hill (“Dear Bill” is W. B. Barnett
secretary of the A.M.A.), re the Broken Hill
unemployed, stating that “the general
opinion is that the people should be
biought to Sydney and a camp formed.”
Yet the perfunctory clamor about the neces-
sity for developing the great natural re-
sources of the country” is as strong as
ever. How can men develop the great
natural resources of the country if they are
loafing in tents around the blessed;

By “Henry Horsecollar” :

a Tho . has .decorated his fourth son, Prince
August William, with the Iron Cross as a reward for
being one of the first to enter Antwerp.— Cable.
Legion of Honor of great Napoleon and Charlemagne’s
Iron Crown!
Throw the lot in the crucible, while Actor Bill melts
’em down.
Bill is playing for movies now; and the big scene
in Reel Two
Is when he decorates August Bill
Foi the Belgian child that the Prussians kill,
And all the innocent blood they spill
From Antwerp to Waterloo.
Legion of all Dishonor is theirs, pinned on with the
Iron Cross,
For a peaceful people put to the' sword, to insult,
and flight, and loss;
Mummer and Ranter William, blunderstudying Nap.
the Great!
Pinning some Krupp on Gussy’s chest,
Repeating the while “Mein und Gott’s behest” ;
Humorless to his own ghastly jest,
Deaf to the wide world’s hate!
Bill is playing for movies now, and we’ll see him
in three reels —
At his worst at murder and breaking oaths, at his
■ best when he merely steals.
In a No. 2 company at last he’ll be, to his shame
and loss,
Decorating Gussie—at Old Junee!
Maying the tank-towns—back seats free !
And one-night stands at two bob and three!
The farce of “The Iron Cross” !

SOCIETY
Melbourne has been officially oi jercd to
go slow with its water. And this in October !
The large, flat city on the Yarra suffers from
the fact that its Board of Works has slept
while everything else has progressed. Mel-
bourne now “consumes” 65,000,000 gallons
a day in place of the 45,000,000 of 10 years
ago. The O’Shannassy scheme is calculated
to bring in 20,000,000 gallons a day, but
it isn’t in full going order yet, and, by
reason of the dry season and the fact that
everybody along the route of the aqueduct
wants to tap it, it won’t bring in more than
half that quantity when it is going. The
big Yan Yean reservoir usually starts the
summer with 24ft. of fluid, and when it gets
down to 14ft. the big city gets the shivers.
It is starting this summer with a depth of
just lift. So the Board stirs uneasily in its
sleep, and the Fire Brigade wonders whether
it is a fair thing to take any sleep at all.

“F.W.C.”: A few days ago the dailies
recorded the alleged utterance of an alleged
warrior to this effect: "The cowardly tier-
mans are afraid of the bayonet, and sham
death when we charge them: but we stick it
into them, anyhow, and enjoy hearing them
squeal.” Now. the blithering press which
prints an atrocious statement like that is a
deadly peril to the soldier at the front. The
story is sure -to come to the ears of Hans
YV'urst, and will inspire him to stick his
lance or assegai or whatever it may he into
the fallen Britisher; and thus many a good
lighter will suffer for what is almost cer-
tainly the lie of a braggart, admiringly
recorded by a senseless press.
K * * *
“Milmeridien” : Suffering eclipse by the
war, that pushing little Hebrew, ida
Goldstein, appeared at South Melbourne
the other night, and. with another Miss—
a Miss Pankhurst, who came to Australia
to tell women that they must insist on get-
ting the vote they have already—did a
double song and dance entitled “Peace.”
The ladies —who belong to the militant
sisterhood which stuck hatpins into police-
men until war cut off the limelight —decor-
ated the platform with such legends as
“Thou shalt not kill” and “Peace on earth,
goodwill toward men.” (Of course they
misquoted, the real thing being “Peace on
earth to men of goodwill.” which is quite
another matter.) Vida related with indig-
nation that she had tried to organise a peace
demonstration with “60 clergymen.” but of
the 60 only three supported her. The other
57 apparently knew that there is no adver-
tisement for peace demonstrators just now.
Undeterred by this failure, Miss Pankhurst
moved to the effect that the workers of all
nations he called upon to demand the imme-
diate cessation of hostilities, and the sub-
mission of all claims involved to arbitra-
tion. She “believed” quite a lot of things,
and seemed to think that that ought to be
enough for anybody. She believed, for in-
stance, that Germany would not conquer
England because “it would not pay.” Or
wonders how it paid the blatant Pankhursts
and their lunatic followers to" slash pictures,
or to set fire to letter-boxes, probably with
the remittances of the poor therein —letters
whose non-delivery meant tragedy to some
woman or other. Happily, Vida and Ghns-
tabel didn’t get much of an audience. Just
now the suffragette could scarcely have a
smaller audience, even if she entered the
solemn institution of matrimony, which
would supply her with one pair of ears—
until they ran awav.

SHEARER (just arrived with his cheque and a big thirst): “Here, Jacky! Keep an eye

“Coot” : Another instance of the deplorable
degeneracy of Australians conies from the
Northcote (Vic.) Cricket Ground. The
ball was thrown, and seemed certain to strike
the head of a bystander, whose attention was
otherwise eneaeed. One of the cricketers,
named Wass, seeing the danger, jumped to
catch the ball just as it was on the point of
striking. The man whose head was in
danger was busy cutting up tobacco, and the
knife gashed Wass’s arm so badly that a
doctor had to insert five stitches. Later
on. when feeling a bit better, Wass returned
to the cricket ground. Just as he arrived
the ninth wicket of his team fell, and the club
still wanted 11 runs to win. He buckled on
the pads, or rather others did it for him,
and went in to try to keep his end up while
his batting partner knocked up the runs.
His team won, and Wass, with one hand,
scored nine.
“Rob”: A swarm of pugnacious ship’s
fiiemen, stirred by the tightableness of the
times, have been raising Tophet in Auck-
land. When the Pakeha came in from Lon-
don, which place she left before war broke
out, she had six shivering Germans and a
scared Austrian in her engine-room crew.
These aliens promptly fled to the police, and
indicated! that they would rather be pris-
oners of war for five years than spend
another day in the stokehold with the patri-
otic Britishers. The police, however, could
do nothing. The shipping people said that
ill the foreigners were discharged from the
ship they would have to engage men in
their places at “colonial” rates of pay, which
would be a calamity. So the miserable
enemies of Britain were again sent to the
fervent patriots below. The latter imme-
diately came on deck and informed the wide
world that they were going to work with no
blessed squareheads, and formally declared a
strike. The ship’s agents then swore out
warrants for the arrest of the patriots for
refusing duty, and next morning 23 beer-
stained subjects of the King were lined up
in court. They seemed comically resigned
to a month hard. But 'the magistrate
declared he would not convict Britishers of
such a charge under such circumstances.
The Germans and the Austrian were, there-
fore, blasphemously discharged, and, hasten-
ing ashore, barricaded themselves in a room
of the Sailors’ Rest. The firemen, on their
part, went off on a jamboree that will live
for ever in the memory of the waterside.
A couple of nights later. 40 firemen from the
Corinthic, full of patriotism (at 3d. a pint),
fell foul of the police. It was a fight in
which skin and hair, baton and bottle, wmre
inextricably mixed, and it raged over the
Queen-street wharf for an hour. Once more
there was a sweat-rag parade in the police
court, and this time the revenue of the
country benefited. Incidentally, deep regret
was expressed by two or three firemen; but
they had lost consciousness early in the
proceedings and had missed the greater part
ot the gorgeous spree. The rest seemed quite
satisfied.
* * * *
Look into this as a sample of the “war
news” we are receiving:—
London, Sunday.—The Official War Bureau
describes a former “knut,” who is a chauffeur with
the General Staff, as performing his toilet in the
morning in the open, washing his face in a saucepan
balanced upon a petrol can, brushing his hair with
an old nailbrush, and using the window of the car
in which he had slept as a looking-glass.
In short, he washed himself; and we at this
end of creation are informed of it in nine
lines and three startled scare-heads. There
is not even any novelty in his manner ot
washing himself. Thousands of Australians
wash themselves in precisely the same way
daily, year in and year out, without a ghost
or a hope ;of their ablutions getting into the
papers as interesting natural history.
“The German soldiers are in many cases
compelled to eat grass.”— Cable.
On grass the tortured Germans sup ;
But Providence soon intervenes.
Behold the kindly foe comes up,
And generously give them beans!
• “Billy Blue” : A double land tax on estates
within 15 miles of a railway was too much
for the A. A. Co., which’ accordingly
announces the subdivision and sale of
38,000 acres of the Warrali Estate, near
Quirindi (N.S.W.). This estate was one of
the grabs allowed by the British Govern-
ment in the Crown Colony days of N. S.
Wales. The A. A. Co. in 1826 selected,
after inquiry, 1,000,000 acres of land as a
grant on the northern shores of Port
Stephens. By 1830 the directors had dis-
covered that the land wasn’t what they
thought it was, so influence was brought to
bear, and Governor Darling, in May, 1830,
was instructed to allow them to exchange it
fox other land. Sir Edward Parry, the
co.’s representative, then selected a large
block at Warrah and another at Peel River,
the former 249.600 acres and the latter
313,298 acres. Meanwhile Governor Bourke
had assumed office, and he vetoed the selec-
tion, as other settlers had already squatted
on the land. Sir Edward Parry—the man
who did the Arctic exploration —referred the
matter “Home,” and Lord Goodridge, the
Secretary of State for the Colonies, over-
ruled Bourke. So the fat Pharisee got the
exchange. The Great Northern road and
railway now runs right through the estate,
and the A. A. Co. will reap no end of un-
earned increment.
THE CHEAPER WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first the bright young lodger came
The women dogged him night and day.
The lodger liked the pretty game
And, laughing, let them have their way.
From sparrow-chirp to vesper hymn
The ‘ dear girls darned his socks for him.
The novice lodger liked it well.
At home they’d never make a fuss ;
buttons he’d been forced to yell,
And darning orders made them cuss.
The lady lodgers loved to do
His work —and they were pretty, too.
The old, case-hardened lodger saw
The worsted nets the girls had spun.
Unto the boy he wagged his jaw,
Telling him harshly to have done.
“When socks are worn, pitch ’em away !
Buy new socks! Buy ’em ev’ry day !”

The war has vividly illustrated the British
dread of death by drowning. It is the
dread of a race of non-swimmers for an
element that is always handy. Thus it
comes about that, though the British tar will
gladly blow his enemies into fragments, the
moment he sees them struggling in the sea
the old water-dread is too strong for him.
and at tremendous personal risk to himself
he will “stand by” to save them from that
fate.. The German seems to be more logical.
He is there to kill, and the manner doesn’t
greatly affect him. He murmurs into his
moustache the German equivalent of “Good
enough for the Cows!” and lets it go at
that.

The revelation of a neat scheme of opium-
smuggling has won acquittal for a man
charged in Sydney with misappropriating
money. lie told the Court that he had been
engaged to go to Java to bring back snakes,
monkeys, and other live-ware and that the
box to carry the serpents had a false bottom,
which was designed to transport tins of
opium. The idea that
Customs officials were
unlikely to carry investi-
gations for opium past a
layer of snakes was pro-
bably correct. The box
wasn’t produced, the
accused explaining that
he had left it behind him
when fie learnt its pur-
pose, and threw up the
job. The opium business,
being highly profitable,
gives a good opening for
the ingenious swindler.
Lately a man in Sydney
negotiated with a Chow
for the sale of a quantity
of the drug. Deals
of this sort have
to be made swiftly
and secretly, and
there is every
reason why cash
should be de-
manded. In this
instance, when
the opium-trader
had delivered his
tins, and opened
one of them to
show the goods',
he collected his
money and got. It
was only after the
police had seized
the whole con-
signment that it
was discovered
that only one of
the tins contained
opium. Further,
there was a strong
suspicion that the man who reaped the coin
also “blew the gaff.” Even Ah Sin can
sometimes be instructed in ways that are
dark.
“Pontius Potter” looks ahead :—-
lie the passing of the mafficking habit (B.
1/10/T4). It is not dead, but sleeping. Some day,
and pray Heaven it may be soon, authentic news
will come to hand that General Someone-or-otherski
has entered Berlin and booted the Kaiser into the
Spree. At that precise moment, as I chuck my
pen at the ceiling and spill ink on the ledger, in
pure joy, a wild crash will tell me that a patriot has
pushed his, hilarious head through our plate-glass.
Out in the street the dying shrieks of a man caught
buying blut-wurst will mingle with the sweet music
of an elderly draper playing upon a penny trumpet.
Grandmothers will tango-on the tramline to the tune
of the cathedral chimes, and patriarchal males will
explode crackers in the ears of utter strangers.
Youths of a mechanical turn of mind will loop the
loop in motor-lorries, and Jack ashore will be
dragged to bits by females. The local hard-borrow-
ing- Treasurer will be seen juggling three golden balls,
whilst smoking two pipes and a cheroot simultane-
ously, and then the fun will begin properly. I
can’t really tell what will happen next, but the
feelings of the inhabitants that the censor is doing
so much to dam will burst with a fine report. The
world will seem a dull place ever after.

“P. J.”: Re the Ministerial decision to
allow the expeditionaries two pints of beer
apiece, per diem, while at sea. Some of the
larger vessels will carry (say) £OOO men,
and the 2000 men will carry 4000 pints.
Multiply that by 40 (the boats won’t do the
run in less than 40 days), and you get
160,000 pints, which is equal to 20,000 gal-
lons. It seems a lot. But possibly the
authorities intend to charter a fleet of beer-
tank steamers to accompany the troopships.

“504”: Some, years ago the Maoriland
Government took over the whole of Rotorua
and leased it out in sections to anyone who
wanted to build a shop or a
home or a boarding-house. As
there was no freehold gambling
in land was down and out.
But Premier Massey, who got
into power partly because he is
in favor of the freehold and
the unearned increment, is now
talking of giving the lessees
the fee simple of their holdings
on the basis of the original
valuation, or something mad
and incompre-
hensible like
that. Anyhow,
he appointed
another Com-
mission (the
faith of Massey
in a commission
is perfectly am-
azing) and sent
it to Rotorua to
ask the lessees
if they would
like to have the
freehold at a
price based on
the value 15
years ago! Naturally
the howls in the negative
were like the howls from
the grave. Massey has
already given a lot of
the Crown tenants the
freehold of their land at
the ancient valuation,
and if Maoriland doesn't
hurry up and get a change of Gov-
ernment the rest of the territory will
be thrown to Private Enterprise, where-
upon P.E. will be entitled to hang on
to it to the end of things without being
ever again any more enterprising than a
bunch of turnips.
The attempt to make war pay its way, by
levying huge contributions on towns that are
captured, is an old device of the remorseless
Prussian thief. He has been an heroic
looter since he first appeared in history.
The levying of an indemnity on a conquered
country at the end of a war is common
enough, and plundering is common enough,
but the habit of holding each captured town
to ransom separately, in order that it may
supply funds for the capture of the next
town, is mainly characteristic of the Prus-
sian nowadays. When the Junker comes
out on top he makes four separate profits:
(1) The formal indemnity.; (2) the cession
of territory?!- (3) the separate indemnities
exacted from captured towns while the war
is in progress; (4) the loot. Unfortunately
a very large, percentage of portable loot con-
sists of liquor. Jewellery may be stolen
to a certain extent, but even it accumulates
and becomes heavy. The private soldier on
the march can hardly take with him on his
back the library and picture gallery and
brass bedstead of his enemy. But he can
take his enemy’s rum away with him in his
stomach, and thus he puts' a hostile force
into his bowels to steal away his gun. And
a sober foe, who might, under happier cir-
cumstances, have consumed that stomach-
furnishing himself, gets on his tracks. Loot-
ing is a dangerous luxury.

A batch of 28 ex-Government officials from
Rabaul landed in Sydney last week, per the
Berrima. The German Club secured accom-
modation for 14 of them at the Hotel Metro-
pole, where the visitors’ book was «isrned with
much pomp and circumstance, the address
being registered as “German New Guinea.”
The lady who presides over the cash and
lOU’s naively inquired “Wherever’s that ?”
and sundry captious visitors who have
assisted in rectifying the entry have embel-
lished the page with inscriptions in multi-
colored inks and pencils and in many hand-
writings.

THE GIRLS OF SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It s just great to walk about and see the girls.
Aren t there some bonzer girls in Sydney ! —Letter
of Territorian, written from Sydney, under the
mftuence of Spring.
Summer is a-comin’ in—clothes a-comin’ off!
Ky’ry young, unshackled male cultivates a cough ;
Pink arms glow in muslin sleeves, bosoms blush in
lacc.
I!7iaf a lot of loveliness scattered round the place!
When these muslin maids of Spring decorate the
streets,
Lv ry man who’s got a heart cannot counts its beats.
AVho could still be serious ’midst these little dears?
Ijord, 1 wish that I could live twenty-thousand years !
Think of all the merry maids of old history!
Think of all the Future’s girls—girls you’ll never
see!
Man who flirts a little space, and by cares is cowed,
Simply cannot hope to know half the Springtime
crowd.
’Twixt the Future and the Past, undecided, I
Gaze beyond the Present maid as she saunters by ;
What a lot we’ve got to miss in this jolly hive!
Still, for what we have, it is good to be alive.
Surely Beauty cannot die! Somewhere, out of
sight,
All nice girls that ever were, since the birth of
light,
B ait to welcome those who pass in the central blue;
So the ones wo see are just angels passing through.
N.s.w.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

THE WEEPING CONQUEROR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The drought is broken.... General rain is reported
from Queensland and the northern parts of N.S.W.
and S. Australia.
General Rain has come again ;
His legions grey we see ;
With mist and spray he weeps his way
To sodden victory.
He is an old, old man and cold,
With red and wretched eyes;
’Mid storms of cheers he sheds his tears
And straight the crops arise.
O'er fields and slums his stormy drums
Beat valiantly on high ;
Wind pipers shrill, by flat and hill,
Before his legions fly.
But though glad shouts of lords and louts
Float clearly to the skies
Where, black and frail, his war-clouds sail,
Uncomforted he cries.
General Rain yet once again
Has come to our relief,
With streaming eyes and sobs and sighs,
God bless his ancient grief!
N.S.W.

McPHERSON'S VIEW OF A
SANGUINARY WAR.
THE MEENISTER: “It is a verra terrible war,
Mr. McPherson; such awfu' losses .”
THE BUSINESS McPHERSON: “Mon, ye have

figures is appalling .”
An amateur “handcuff king” struck trouble
in Maoriland recently. He had received some
tuition in the art from an alleged expert at New-
castle (N.S.W.). Then he acquired a pair of hand-
cuffs and rushed over to Loan Land in pursuit of
fame and sundries. The first audience he struck con-
sisted of one lone railway porter at a place called
Tuakau, but even with the energetic assistance of the
audience he couldn’t get more than one hand free,
and, as the key of the handcuffs was lost the king
was in trouble. The porter sent him to Mercer by
train, and there he called at a sawmill and sought
the loan of a file. Someone saw him using it on
the handcuff on his wrist and ’phoned the local cop,
and the local cop sprang along and grabbed the
unfortunate artist. A sympathetic beak got the rail-
way porter to confirm by ’phone the accused’s state-
ment about the performance at Tuakau, and then
banished him with a caution.

There was an invigorating law suit at
Gisborne (M.L.) the other day when a taxi driver
sought £44 for ear hire from a gentleman with the
Hebrew-Maori name of Abraham Te Whiro. Abe,
before leaving on a trip to Napier, had told the taxi
man that if his two stepdaughters wished to go for
a run in the car while he was away they were to be
taken on request, and the bill would be paid by him
(Abe) on his return. The girls had a perfectly lovely
time, and when father arrived home a fortnight later
he was handed a bill for £44. The Maori refused to
pay, and was promptly ushered into court, where he
put up the defence that the driver had taken advan-
tage of his instructions, and had systematically lured
the girls out in the cab. Also, it was alleged that
he had supplied a couple of young men to hold the
girls’ hands and keep them from getting lonely. The
beak said that whenever business was slack the driver
may have rushed round to Abe’s house and tooted
the horn to remind the girls that he was still on earth ;
but, if so, it had nothing to do with the case. The
Maori had said he would pay any hires incurred by
the girls, and the Court must take him at his word,
as the motor man did. Abraham was, then formally
smitten with costs, and departed after having con-
vulsions in two languages.

Proclamation read to the local Binghis at
Rabaul (New Britain) after the Australian force
had finished its argument with the Kaiser’s dele-
gates :
All Boys belongina all place, you savvy big fellow
Master he come now; he new fellow Master, he
strong fellow too much. You look him all ship
stop place; he small feller ship belongina him;
plenty more big feller he stop place belongina him.
Now he come here he take him all place; he look
out good you feller; now he like you feller you
look out good along him. Supposing other feller
Master he been speak you, “You no work along new
feller Master,” he gammon. Supposing you work-
good with this new feller Master, lie look out good
along with you, he look out good you get plenty
good feller kaikai (food) ; he no fighting black
feller boy along nothing. You look him new feller
flag; you savvy him, he belong British. He more
better than other feller.
Supposing you been making paper before this
new feller Master come, you finish time belongina
him first; after you finish time belongina him you
like make him new feller paper belongina new
feller Master, he look out good along with you. He
give you more money and more good feller kaikai.
Suppose you no look out good along him he cross
too much.
'British new feller Master he like him black feller
man too much. He like you all the. same you
piccanin along him. You got black feller Master
belonginu you, you look out along with him. lie
all the same Police' Master. He look out place along'
. with you, you no fight other feller black man other
feller place, you no kajkai. man, you ho steal Marv
belonging other feller -man.
Me finish talk along with you now ; by-an-bye ship
belongina new feller Master he come and look out
place along with you. You look him man beloilgina
place belongina you, you speak him: all the same.
Me been talk along with you now ; now. you give
three feller cheers belongina new feller Master.
It was a wonderful bit of literature; but Binghi
seemed to catch on to the main points, and sent
along the cheers as requested.

“E.J.M.”: Shearing cut out at Glengame,
Narromine (N.S.W.), yesterday. A barber named
B. H. Harris, who comes from Sydneyside, I think, put
up, on the last day, the respectable total of 304.
The cove who runs the shed is rather a particular
sort and doesn’t allow slumming, so you may take it
that the sheep were well shorn. Does any Abo-
writer know of a better tally?
THE CURSE OF TINCANBERRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne, Thursday.—lt has been decided by
the Federal Government that the competition for
designs for the proposed Parliament buildings at
Canberra shall be suspended for the present. This
action has been taken as the result of the disturbed
conditions produced by the war.
Yass, Saturday.—Several fresh cases of small-pox
have occurred at the railway camps in the vicinity
of Coolalie. —News items, NOT frgm the war.
List to me, 0 camerados!
List to me with close attention.
Yea, with earnestness and patience,
Like to Job—who had to hearken
All the chestnuts and back-answers
Of the ancients who protested
They were friends —the Talking Trio,
Bildad, Eliphaz and Zophar !. .. .
But you are not boil-afflicted,
And, I trust, I shall not bore you.
Hear the tale of Tincanberra,
All the swindle that enwrapt it;
All the jobs and lies that made it;
And the Curse that fell upon it;
All the load it staggers under—
Dry, deserted, dead-broke, dusty,
Dreary and drought-stricken hamlet
By the rabbit-poisoned “rivers.”
And the waterholes fast failing;
Where the peanut politicians
Strangled young Australia’s city,
Slew and buried Public Honor
With their rotten reputations!
Years ago they told the people
They would rear a stately city
In a fair and smiling district
By an ever-running river—
Such a city as no nation
Ever owned or dreamed of building!
Such a capital resplendent—
Such a cynosure of Wisdom,
Wealth and Art and peerless beauty,
Commerce, pleasure and all features
Rome and Athens vainly sighed for—■
London, Paris, Brussels, Moscow,
Agra, Stockholm, Copenhagen,
Madrid, Munich, and all cities
Noted for their size or beauty,
Lacked—and ever will be lacking.
“We,” they said, “are Big Australians,
And our views are like our country—
Large, expansive, Continental!”....
After years of cogitation,
Disputation and excursion,
And survey through champagne-glasses.
“Here,” they said, “we’ll build our city—
Here Australia’s highest glory,
Concreted in stone and marble,
Shall appear to envying nations.
Set among the eternal mountains,
Bv the swift, blue-rushing river,
Capital of a whole continent!”
Camerados, I assure you
The applause was quite tremendous!
All the people shouted Viva! —■
Or the Australian word for Viva !—-
While the envying outside nations
Made remarks of flattering import....
Hold ! Did I say all the people ?
Pardon, my good camerados !
I forgot the Sydney people— •
I forgot the big Importers—
I forgot the little Geebungs—
I forgot the Ma-State-Frighters !
These, although their faith was plighted
(Faith! Excuse me, camerados!),
Rose up swiftly in their anguish,
Crying: “Very great is Sydney
Of the Importers and the Geebungs!
What is this that they would give us ?
In Carruthers all our hope is—
Yea, in Wade and Hughes and Fuller,
Conroy, Cook and Willie Kelly!
To your tents, O S. M. ’Erald,
Daily Telegraph and others!
Blow Australia, blow our brothers,
Blow the Future! Blow the city! ”
Straight the peanut politicians
Curved their spines in meek abasement;
On their bellies fell prostrated,
Wailing: “Oh, dear—Hark at Sydney!”
Then they clasped the knees of Sydney,
Weeping at the sound and fury
From the cellar of the ’Erald ;
From the clubs of the importers;
From the attic of the Geebungs ;
From the whiskers of the Telegraph.
Then they moaned: “Oh, be not angry!
We don’t care about the city;
We thought it was for Australia !
Since you raise this loud objection
We will change the site to-morrow;
You can put it where you want it—
Certainly!... .Oh, blow the others!
No one else will fuss or bother—
Anything for peace and quietness!”
After some more years of quarrel,
Cogitation and excursion,
And inspection through large bottles,
All the peanut politicians—
Experts all at grinding axes,
And most skilful at log-rolling—
Engineered a grand bouleversement
(That’s the French for overturning,
Or polite for blanky swindle!).
llow they managed it were better
Left to chronicles hereafter.
Meantime, mark, good camerados.
How they rushed to help—er—Australia !
First,, they dropped the useful measure
Which secured a site superb for
Our great Continental City!
Then, by cunning combination,
They excluded sites inferior,
Though not altogether hopeless.
Finally, they chose Canberra !
Dreary, rainless, brown Canberra-
Windswept, desolate Canberra....
Why?... .The question needs no answer !
Canberra is near to Sydney—
Everything must be near to Sydney!—
So, of course, they chose Canberra,
Now renowned as Tin-Can-berra!
But the Curse fell on Canberra !....
From the day on which they chose it
Nothing prospered, nothing flourished ;
But the soil grew even drier,
And the rabbit-poisoned rivers
Shrank and shrank, and kept on shrinking;
Water there was none for drinking,
Save the drops that fell from Heaven
(Usually one year in seven!),
And the west wind howled in winter,
Blowing half the landscape skyward.
And the sun for eight months, yearly,
Burnt and blistered plain and hillside—
Blasted grass and vegetation ;
Scorched the paddocks where the rabbits
Languished for the lukewarm water
Of the Cotter and Molonglo—
Rivers now retired from business!
Very strong and highly active
Is the Curse of Tin-Can-berra !....
All the plans and parchment jimjams
Of the Ivnock-the-World-Stiff City
Have been brought to naught—and laughter !
Though the peanut politicians,
And the put-on-dog officials
Have for several years spent money—
Yea, three hundred golden thousands
Of good Uncle Cohen’s sovereigns—
Though the plans are simply gorgeous,
And the paper lakes and fountains
Flood the rock-scape with their moisture;
Though the buildings—which outrival
Rome and Athens, and the castles
Spain and Ether boast so justly—
Are of breath-depriving splendor.
Still the Curse will not be shifted!
Still the rain avoids Canberra —
Still the “rivers” go on shrinking.
And the “public works” are ailing ;
And the revenue is failing ;
And the loans —just like the “rivers”—
Have stopped running; and the small-pox
Is beginning to get busy in that dry, despondent
terra
K nown to fame as Tin-Can-berra!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

“J.M.M.”: No brush could paint and no
pen picture the sufferings now being endured by
starving stock on the southward tracks from the
drought-cursed Riverina and northern parts of Vic-
toria ; but supreme in the midst of many miseries
are the sufferings of the drovers’ dogs. These tireless
creatures work all day in choking dust and heat, with
little water and less food, and lie chained on guard
all night. Then, at the first sign of a move on the
part of their masters, they tug at their chains, and
yelp delightedly at the thought of liberty, though
liberty to them means another day of hard roads on
sore and bleeding feet. Still they never quit. It
makes my heart sick to see drovers treat such noble
animals so heartlessly. I admit that some drovers do
the fair thing; but the great majority reward their
dogs with kicks and curses and semi-starvation.
“Lagani-Nam'o”: The natives of the
Gazelle Islands (near New Britain) know quite a lot
about surgery. Broken limbs are reset and kept in
position with splints of bamboo or tortoise-shell. For
headaches and painful bruises the proprietors resort
to phlebotomy. A miniature bow with a tiny flint-
tipped arrow is used for blood letting. But it is their
peculiar skill in trepanning that excites most admira-
tion. Rows, in which stone clubs whirl energeti-
cally, are very frequent thereabouts, and many frac-
tured skulls result. This is how they mend them.
Before operating the surgeon laves his hands and the
patient’s cranium with the milk of a green cocoanut
and shaves the hair off the top of the patient. He
then takes his splinter of shell or obsidian and makes
the requisite incision. The scalp is now drawn care-
fully back, and the crushed bone is removed with
pincers of springy bamboo, until the brain is visible.
The surgeon can tell by the slight movement of the
thinking mecha ism whether anything foreign, such
as a splinter from the skull has’got into it. This will
be diligently searched for until the operator is
satisfied that the works are in good going order.
Then a specially bevelled plate of cocoanut, tortoise-
shell or wood is dovetailed into the aperture, the
scalp is replaced, washed again with cocoanut milk,
and the cranium encased in a cap of finely-plaited
cane. After that, all is well.

“Snell” : The war fever takes quaint shapes.
There is a wide stretch of open land between St.
K i 1 d a and Elwood
(Melb.). Passing through
on the tram the other
day I noticed an elderly
man stripped to flannel
and pants furiously at
work. Two days later
the same man was still
on the job, and it sur-
prised me to see that
his detached raiment con-
sisted of a white shirt, a
black frock-coat and an
ancient belltopper. The
worker must have been
60 ; he went at it with
a zeal amounting almost
to passion, tearing up
one particular weed with
an instrument like a
diminutive pickaxe. The
growth to which the in-
dustrious moiler was de-
voting his energies was
the cape weed, a fair
hog of a plant, the bane
of the district. My
native and professional
curiosity was aroused.
“Working for the coun-
cil?” I asked. “No,”
answered the man, belt-
ing into another weed.
“For whom, then?” He
attacked a weed
saVagely. “No-
body!” he said.
“Excuse me,” I
continued
urgently, “but are
you paid for
this?” He looked
at me with a wild,
disconcerting eye.
“No,” he said,
said, and swiped fiercely at a cape weed. . “I do it
because I hate ’em!” The elderly gent is still out
there in his flannel and pants swiping at his enemy.
AS ADVERTISING.
POET: “How much for this little effusion?"
EDITOR: "H'm! How many times do you wish it inserted!"
“Hypo”: My farmer friend’s best horse
died the other day. The animal was in horrible pain
prior to its death, but, though there was no chance of
saving its life, the owner would not shoot the poor
brute, as it was insured. Death eventually ended the
torture to the beast, as well as to those who watched
it. The insurance co.’s agent viewed the body, saw
the torn-up earth caused by the death agonies, and
acknowledged that the four-footed creature was dead ;
also, that it died according to the policy regulations.
So the farmer saved his money, and the co. saved its
rules and regulations, and whether the horse saved
its soul, I can’t tell.

“Marloo”: While wheat farmers and
sheep-raisers all over the country were recently pray-
ing for rain, or cursing because it didn’t come, cane
growers on the Clarence Itiver (N.S.W.) were vig-
orously blackguarding an over-supply of moisture.
During wet spells a disease known as “arrowing”
makes its appearance in the cane. It is allegedly
due to over-production of sugar in the stalk conse-
quent on excessive rainfall. In order to allow the
surplus sugar to escape, the stalk develops an arrow-
shaped growth at the top, and later on a tassel
appears. The. ailment in a very mild form has
always been present, attacking mostly the cane when
fit to be cut, and little harm has resulted. Now,
however, it has assumed rather serious proportions
and infests the year-old plants, which in 12 months’
time will not Contain sufficient sugar to pay the
cutter, let alone supplying tucker for the grower and
a bloated divvy for the C.S.R. Co.
“Bill Bowang” : Niue islands, which have
been pushing themselves into the war news, had
quite a respectable hat-making industry once. The
islanders plait the pandanus leaf very skilfully,
and there used to be always a good market in
Maoriland for the millinery. The work is done
by the women in their own homes, and the finished
article sold to traders at so much per dozen. In
recent years, however, Maoriland has been flooded
with a cheap machine-made Java hat; and though
for appearance and durability it cannot be compared
with the Niuean lid, the export of the latter has
fallen off considerably. The Niue trader can’t now
get more than about 10s.' per dozen for his goods;
and the native’s cut is about 9s. per dozen, which
he says isn’t enough to make the job worth while.
There is little doubt that unless the Niuean hats are
protected in the Maoriland market from the com-
petition of shoddy machine-made articles, the in-
dustry will perish and plaiting will become a lost
art in the group. An attempt was made a while
ago to produce Panama hats there. A trader
imported the straw and put it into the hands of
the more skilful natives, but they either could or
would do nothing with the new-fangled material.
’Gators’ Euchre.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FAIR evening—an evening of the
North.
The tall, tropical palms were swish-
ing. The locusts, whose eternal screech,
heard throughout the day, had seemed to
intensify the heat with its dreariness and
monotony, were silent at last. The sun
had set; and across the level scrub-lands
of the Burdekin there lingered a wonderful
glow. It tinged the tops of ash and blood-
wood, of pandanus and ti-tree ; and it made
the seared grass lands golden. Quietness
reigned and the North seemed young.
On a beautiful deep lagoon, a mile long,
a half-mile wide, and thick with ducks,
geese and swans, the sky—a touch of glory
—was mirrored. Near the steep banks all
was deep shadow, and high above the thick
mangroves the ti-trees stood, gaunt.
There was a clear space of water near
one end of the lagoon—a still, clear space.
Towards it, calling quickly and warily, flew
a flock of geese. Round they circled, round
twice more, lessening the distance at each
turn. Then they ducked right down —and
suddenly up again, and away to the farthest
end of the water.
High above, and eager for the common
camping-place—like many other animals
ducks like much company in the camp —-
appeared a flock of whistling ducks. With
whirring wings and vigorous whistling they
descended to the clear space in one grand
corkscrew swoop. Then they, too, rose pre-
cipitately and fled.
The bird of calmness and courage—the
black swan —called in the distance, and was
answered by two others flying higher up.
He circled down to a similar clear space
in a neighboring lagoon, divided from the
first by a narrow neck of land. He settled
with a light splash and swam about slowly,
his head inclined now slightly to the left
and now r to right. A quick call from the
two circling higher above, and they began
to descend. A steady note from the one
swimming. The two soared upward. Our
swan, uttering a note of sadness, rose from
the water and joined his wife and son.
In the clear patch on the right lagoon
there appeared a smali piece of bark. It
must have floated there, for it wasn’t there
a minute ago. A moment later a small piece
of bark was noticeable in the centre of the
clear space on the lagoon to the left.
Thus the two pieces of bark remai n
motionless. A water-lien flew out to P e
on one of them, but turned quickly when
the point of settling, and flew back to
bank. Then they went—they floated out or
view as unostentatiously as they had ap-
peared. The two patches of water continue
strangely deserted.
From the farther scrub emerged a horse-
breaker, driving a colt in a buckboard. i- ■
was 15 miles from the homestead. As ne
bowled along towards the narrow neck ot
land he noticed how naturally his horse kept
to a faintly-beaten pad. Three weeks ago,
before he had come to the station and had
begun to exercise his horses in that part, the
pad had not been in existence.
He reflected that in 10 minutes he would
be at the neck. As usual, at seven o’clock he
would make his horse trot across, it. In
20 minutes be would be two miles from
it. He would then, as usual, turn and walk
his horse back, and so get him cool for a
drink at a spring just past the. lagoons. It
was part of his system of training horses to
show them that, firstly, they had to bend their
desires to his; and, secondly, that he was,
while making them do this, mindful of their
welfare, after all.
A steep fall led down to the neck. . He
rattled down recklessly. He had whisked
acx-oss 20 times; this would be his twenty-
first ; and not once, though there wasn t
sufficient room for a vehicle to turn round
on it, had he been capsized into the water.
He had gone across with seven different
horses, but none bad behaved like this colt-
For instance, when had one suddenly pulled
up at the neck, with a jerk, and planted its
feet firmly down like a stubborn donkey?
“Anyhow,” he told the colt with a grin,
“we’ll go over the neck, my boy, or over the
neck, my boy, curse you”—meaning they
w r ould cross the neck or fall off it into one
of the lagoons.
He swung his whip fiercely and hard.
“Hell,” he said grimly. “It’ll either be rump
steak and grass or ’gator meat to-night.
Please yourself and blazes take you. I’m not
particular.”
The colt, the white of its eyes showing with
fear, made a rear and a plunge, and swerved
to the right. Before it began to descend,
however, the breaker jerked the left rein
hard. The animal was righted while in the
air. It hit the ground safely, and dashed
madly ahead. The buckboard reeled from side
to side, and the driver’s laugh floated back
on the air as it disappeared into the scrub
at the brow of the rise. The two pieces of
bark had drifted back.
The bush was calm ; but the thousands of
waterfowl on both lagoons seemed restless,
for little flocks constantly rose and departed.
Fewer flocks took their places. There was
rather less than the ordinary amount of
quacking and calling. Conversation seemed
constrained.
The pieces of bark remained. Larger
armies of ducks departed. Darkness settled.
Only the sing-song of the mosquitoes re-
mained unhushed. The tall palm tops,
silhouetted against the sky, swayed rhythmi-
cally.
The two pieces of bark, owing to the
darkness, were no longer discernible when
the breaker returned with his horse at a
walk, and reached the slope that led down
to the neck.
A black swan circled overhead, sounded a
quiet honk of warning, and flew. away. The
breaker began to have trouble with his horse
again.
“You rotten cow!” he yelled.
Horsemanship will win out. He went
down the slope, and at a walk. He was
determined to go at that pace right back to
the spring. Colts had to learn that man
was boss.
In the centre of the neck the colt sud-
denly stood stock still, and shivered. The
black swan returned, swept round overhead,
again uttered its pregnant note, and again
flew away.
“To Hell”
The breaker was about to swear. Some-
thing indefinable checked him.
With pricked ears, wide nostrils and wider
eyes his snorting horse stared ahead. He
bent forward and listened.
A sound reached him from a little distance
away—a crawling, rustling, stealthy sound
which came in little jerks.
His horse’s ears went back. Its head
turned. It trembled more than ever. He
looked round —a slow, crawling, stealthy
sound reached him from behind.
“Cod!” he muttered, and his face was
white now. “The Euchre. The ’Gators
Euchre.”
A clumsy crashing rush out of the dark-
ness ahead, another from behind. Two
shrieks —one from the man and another from
the horse, and a great rattle and splash as
the maddened colt sprang to one side.
But hardly an extra ripple appeared on
the water as the two alligators left the neck
find entered the lagoon. The black swan
circled higher and higher above, _ calling
despairingly. Then it flew away into the
night, returned, and left again.
And the tropical night was full of peace.
A. L. BREWER.

Seaman Kiordan, who went down with
Australia’s submarine AEI, was a native of
Kaikoura (M.L.), and was one of the first
draft of Maoriland boys trained on H.M.S.
Pioneer. David Jones, Esq., made an at-
tempt to get him three months ago, when a
tug in Sydney harbor ran down and sank
the pinnace of the Pioneer, in which he was
returning to the ship. He dodged death
that time through being a good swimmer,
but the cowardly old Enemy squared the
account when it got its quarry carefully
lccked up in a submarine.
SENA TOR
A.J. GOULD
(N.S.W.).
One at least of Nelson’s officers was
buried in N. S. Wales. At St. John’s, Cam-
den, there is the grave of Alexander Martin,
Commander R.N., who died September 7,
1868, aged 84 years. He joined the navy in
1795, and was an officer under Duncan at
Camperdown and Nelson at Trafalgar.
A yarn of Jabez Wright, M.L.A. for Will-
yama, who has spent half his life in business
as an undertaker at Broken Hill. He was
on his way to the Chamber when a horse
swerved from the road, and struck the wall
of 1 arliament House. In doing so it col-
lided with a pedestrian, slightly injuring him.
Jabez bustled up.and asked if anybody was
killed. "Only the horse,” said somebody.
"My luck !” retorted Jabez. "I don’t burv
horses.”
Some women have poor luck. It is cabled
that two kindly Yankee spinsters, possibly
with straight hair and spectacles, have
established a private hospital for wounded
soldiers at Ostend (Belgium). The name
of one is given as Maunder and the other
as Tinker. No angel should be named
Maunder —or Tinker. An angel’s name
should be carefully selected. Elizabeth Fry
was probably a greater benefactress than
Florence Nightingale. But Fry suggests a
bit of bacon in a pan in the back kitchen,
while Nightingale is remindful of a song-
stress in a tree bn a moonlit night in sum-
mer. So Betty' Fry never got quite the
credit that was due to her. And neither
will the ministering sisters Maunder and
Tinker. They have just as much show as a
deathless hero named William Slush.

Bishop Golding-Bird, who goes to the
wars with the Westralian cohort, has been
telling a story of his experience in S’Africa
concerning individual courage. For him-
self, he said _ that during action he w r as
“horribly afraid,” and one day mentioned to
a rough-and-ready officer that he would give
a lot to be like those men wdio never felt
fear in battle. Said the officer: “Would
you like me to tell you what those men are
who say that?” “Very much, indeed,” re-
plied the parson. “Well, they’re damned
liars,” snapped the warrior.
Passed to Abraham’s bosom the other day,
at Melbourne, Isaac Jacobs, one of the
pioneers of the Australian tobacco indus-
try and a man full of charity. About GO
years ago he settled in Australia and became
associated with the struggling concerns
which have grown into the States Tobacco
Co. and the big British-Australasian To-
bacco Co. Seven sons and two daughters
(including the wife of Justice Isaacs and
Mrs. Harry Levy, of Sydney) share the
loss.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant” :
Apropos Australian-born Bishops (B. 15/10/T4).
An Australian gained a bishopric in England, and an
Australian-born rector in England won an Australian
bishopric. The former was Edward Parry, son of the
Arctic explorer, born at Government House, Parra-
matta, in 1830, his father being then in charge of
the A. A. Co.’s affairs at Port Stephens. He was
appointed Bishop of Dover in 1870. The second was
Dr. Samuel E. Marsden, Bishop of Bathurst, a grand-
son of Sam Marsden, the blacksmith-parson of the
very early days. Young Marsden, who was at Cam-
bridge in the ’so’s, was a diocesan inspector of
schools when he got the Bathurst job in 1869. He
was probably the worst preacher who ever wore an
apron in Australia—and that’s saying a good deal.
“M.McD.F.”: Two portraits were unveiled
in Ballarat the other day. One was of
Father “Tom” Guilfoyle, a fine man. a good
priest, and one of Ballarat’s best citizens
in the days before he died. He has been
dead just long enough to have been forgotten
if he hadn’t been the kind of man that
refuses to be forgotten. The other portrait
was of the present Catholic Bishop of the
Garden City, Dr. Higgins, who used to bish
at Rockhampton (Q.). Both were painted
by Bruno Geppe, of Rome, there being no
capable portrait painters in Australia. Had
Father Tom been about, to voice his opinion
on the matter, those responsible for the un-
patriotic act would have had a foretaste of
what’s waiting for them. But, as mentioned
before, he is dead.
Westmore Stephens, founder of one of
Melbourne’s biggest fluff and staylace shops,
died last week. He stood up alongside Rob-
ertson and Moffat and Buckley and Nunn.
It is a subject of bitter and enduring regret
with the Post Office that it failed to secure
the Stephens property when an offer of sale
was made. The offer having been turned
down, the energetic owner went ahead and
established so big a business that next time
money was mentioned the figure was sky-
high. Though formerly a City Councillor,
Stephens was best known as the chief lay-
man of the wealthy Baptist church in Vic-
toria. He was twice President of the Bap-
tist Union, was a vice-president (in Phila-
delphia. U.S.) of the World’s Baptist Con-
gress, and had been nominated as vice-presi-
dent of another congress fixed to be held
next year in Berlin.
‘ Vic”: There is fury in Melbourne over
the announcement that a male and female
baboon just added to the Melbourne Zoo are
named Mr. and Mrs. Maguire. I don’t be-
lieve the gentle director Be Souef was re-
sponsible for such a silly thing. Anyhow,
no matter what folly of this kind there may
be, Maguire will remain one of the most
honored names in Ireland. The Maguire
princes ruled for centuries as Lords of Fer-
managh in the ancient kingdom of Ulster,
and various members of the family were dis-
tinguished military commanders in France
during the reigns of Louis XIV. and Louis
XY. ;n Spain, and with Hungarian and
Bohemian regiments in the Austrian ser-
vice. They were Barons of Enniskillen
under the British Crown; and the Ennis-
killing dragoons and fusiliers come largely
nom that clan and its kindred. Incidentally,
isn’t it paltry that, while State School chil-
dren are admitted free to the Melbourne Zoo,
Roman Catholic school children have to pay?
Bishop Torres, Abbot of New Norcia
(W.A.), dead at Subiaco, had a brief record.
It was his predecessor, Dr. Salvado, who
gave the aborigines of the Nor’-West their
first good impression of the white man. He
and his company of Spanish clerics became,
in a sense, abos., who mixed hunting with
building and planting. “I even came to like
lizards and earth worms,” records Don Sal-
vado ; but there wasn’t enough even of these,
so he went back to Perth to raise money
to buy provisions. With that in view he
sang and played the piano, facing his audi-
ence in a tunic to the knees, his trousers in
tatters, his socks of different colors, on his
face a six-months’ untrimmed beard, and
wearing hands that washing didn’t improve.
But he raised the wind, loaded up his dray
and started back to Norcia. (The place was
called Nursia at the beginning, after the
Spanish birthplace of St. Benedict, the
founder of the mission’s Order.) It was
Salvado’s way with the aborigines which
made the monastery the success it has be-
come—a success unique in Australia. The
abbot who has just died had an easier
time. He came from the Philippines 10
years ago, and has passed out at 53. Sal-
vado returned to Rome when over 80, and
enjoyed good health for some years.
Thomas Fergus, who died at Dunedin
(M.L.) recently, reached Melbourne from
‘Scotland in the early 00’s of last century,
and.in 1869 pushed south-to Dunedin. Two
years later he became District Engineer of
Goldfields under the Provincial Government.
After four years of this he started business
with a partner named Henderson, and
carried out many big construction contracts.
In Maoriland the firm built the Mosgiel-
Outram railway, the Patea railway, the
New Plymouth waterworks, the Bluff rail-
way wharf and many big bridges; and it
constructed the Deloraine-Mersey railway in
Tasmania and the Alphington-Heidelberg
and Koroit-Warrnambool lines in Victoria.
In 1881 Fergus won a seat in the M.L.
House of Reps., where he remained until.
1893. In that year he definitely retired,
after being twice elected unopposed. At
different times he held the portfolios of
Justice, Defence, Works and Mines in
various Governments, and was looked
upon as an efficient Minister. As a
keen business man he was in request as a
company director; he was one of the Govern-
ment nominees on the Board of the Bank
of New Zealand and earned his fees from
the Standard Insurance., the Mosgiel Wool-
len and the Westport Coal cos.

Another book on the vicissitudes of life
in N. S. Wales is threatened. Joshua Vickery
is to do it. As the Vickerys have,
as a rule, done well, there will probably be
more ups than downs in the story. The
original Vickery started shoemaking in
George-street, Sydney, in 1833; and Joshua,
the present book-maker, was born on the
premises six years later.
Kitchener being now 64, and married only
to War, all hope of a mortal direct heir has
apparently been abandoned. Special provi-
sion has therefore been made for the succes-
sion of his titles —he is now Earl K. of K.,
\ iscount Broome of Broome, and Baron
Denton of Denton. His brother, Colonel
11. E. Kitchener, who is named as his heir,
is something of a mining engineer. The last
time he visited Australia it was to report
on the Cloncurry copper field —in the inter-
ests, it was understood, of the Coats cotton
people chiefly. He has a son and a
daughter.
Jensen, the new Federal Minister of
Marine, has been most things except a
mariner. He left school when he was 41,
and got a job cleaning currants at 3s. a
week. When he had mastered that branch
of commerce lie went into a jam factory at
ss. a week. His next promotion was to the
stables, and afterwards he. worked in a
pottery. This was in Victoria. Then he
went across to Tasmania and had a week or
so in. turn at mining, undertaking and pub-
keeping. Finally he settled down as an
orchardist, and still sticks to that as a side-
line.
“The Oldest Inhabitant’s” note:—
Apropos “Tom” Pidgeon (B. 8/10/’l4). Ilis
father, “Nat” Pidgeon, was not an ordained clergy-
man at any time ; he was in the Wexford Yeomanry
in the time of the Irish rebellion of 1798. Nat was
apprenticed to a cabinet-maker in Enniscorthy. As
he, his wife and child arrived in Port Jackson on
May 20, 1841, he could scarcely have seen six men
hanged in Wynyard-square in the ’3o’s. Any-
how, no hangings ever took place in Wyn-
yard-square. He got employment with E. Hunt,
in Jamison-street. He says in his journal
that he was horror-struck at the wickedness of both
men and boys; while in Hunt’s workshop he felt as
if he “had been landed on the borders of Hell.” Which
is a cruel injustice to the memory of Hunt, who
was a highly respectable man, in whose warehouse
Irancis Forbes (Chief Justice), Edward Parry
and other leading men of the time founded the first
temperance society in Sydney. Nat Pidgeon attached
himself to the City Mission, and became an open-air
preacher. One of his greatest aids at reforming the
“drunks” was a large bullock-bell, with which he
armed himself on Sabbath mornings, and in front
of each Sussex-street pubbery he bawled: “Come out
and be saved, ye rum drinkers.” Many were called
but few came out.
ABLE SEAMAN JONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The naval authorities announce that letters for
persons serving in H.M.A. ships should be addressed
in the following manner:—
Able Seaman Jones,
11.M.A.5. Encounter,
' C/o G.P.0., Sydney.
Jack Tar and Tommy Atkins, those immortal men
we‘ll see
Wherever Britain’s trumpets sound the call of
history ;
They’ll march and fight, though vultures sight and
pick their shattered bones ;
And now to help the deathless pair conies Able
Seaman Jores.
Full-grown, equipped for land or sea, with battle-flag
unfurled,
lie takes his station in the line that winds around
the world.
No shot or shell will stay his march, no steel will
bar his way !
He is immortal, and he knows that he has come to
stay!
A year ago they knew him not. To-day he is a kingM
We only live because he waits where questing liners
swing;
Below the blue horizon rim lift watches day and
night
The interests of the lonely land behind and out of
sight.
While Jack and Tommy in the North serve out the
nickelled cones,
They’re leaving all the jobs round here to Able
Seaman Jones ;
And .Tones is cleaning up the South and ordering
the seas,
Collecting German colonies, and never savin”-
“Please!”
Next arm-in-arm we’ll see them roll around the smil-
ing world,
When all the little jobs are done and battle-flags
are furled ;
And Capital will wave its hat and Labor lift a cheer,
And all will sounder sleep at night because our
Jones is near.
Thus cobwebs spun of dread and doubt in days of
slothful ease
Are melted by the blaze of war, and on Pacific seas
We see him ride, his gun beside, and Lord! how it
atones
For all the toil and agony! Hail, Able Seaman
Jones!
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
A Sydney politician whom it is always
consoling to find on the premises at a time
of trouble.—provided it is a Labor Govern-
ment which is in trouble —is Henley. He
suggests the affable cat which sits and purrs
on the rug when there is a death in the
family. The latest proposition of this Job’s
comforter is that the N.S.W. Government
should raise the rate of interest on Savings
Hank deposits to 5 p.c. At present, the rate
is 34 p.c. for money which can be with-
drawn at the shortest possible notice. For
deposits at call the private banks allow no
interest whatever, and they even charge a
trifle for keeping the account. So the Sav-
ings Bank lives precariously on a very
narrow margin. If the present war trouble
lasts long, the withdrawals will probably be
heavy, and it will be difficult, in the hurry
to realise securities, to earn 34 p.c. To pro-
mise 5 p.c. on the pound which is here to-day
and gone to-morrow would be suicide. Sup-
posing Premier Holman had struck Henley
with a club at this crisis it would have been
a noble and fitting act. But with his usual
affability lie said that the matter would be
considered. Holman is the affable kind of
man who would consider a proposal for dig-
ging up the Murray River, and making it
into soup for the unemployed.
* * * . -X-
Marcus Cuark and Co., Ltd., who carry
on a furnishing and drapery business in
Sydney and elsewhere, and do much of it on
a time-payment basis :

The co. was formed 12 years ago, but the
Public didn’t come into it for three or four
years afterwards. To begin with there was
a loading of £72,.‘>77 for goodwill. By main-
taining a modest dividend in times of afflu-
ence the management has been able to
exchange that deficit for a very substantial
reserve; in other words, instead of having
tangible assets £72.277 less than the capital,
it has now £78,453 more, which means that
in 12 years the net profits have exceeded the
dividends by £150,830 —a performance most
creditable to the directors. Nominally there
is £15.000 of goodwill still among the assets ;
but that could all be wiped out. even by the
unappropriated P. and L. balance. ’ And
besides that unappropriated balance there
are two formal reserve funds totalling
£75.024.
The accumulation of reserves has certainly
been simplified by the fact that of the pre-
sent capital of £350,000, no less than
£150,000 was got in when money was cheap,
and carries only 6 p.c. Still there’s more
than that much —£157,091, to be exact —
represented by freehold and leasehold pro-
perties. nearly all of them in Sydney; and
it isn’t reasonable to expect Sydney proper-
ties, purchased or built during the past few
years, to return any more than 0 p.c. It
boils down to this: a trading co. with so
much in bricks and mortar couldn’t show
•such results as this one if it weren’t helped
on its way by a lot of cheap preference
capital.
But even 6 p.c. on money sunk in big
business premises may take a lot of earning
in bad times, when half the accommodation
might be sufficient. And it looks as though
this is the sort of business that bad times
would affect seriously. To a great extent
it is a furnishing house; and thoughts of
extra, furniture are about the first thing you
shut down on when prosperity evaporates.
A further source of trouble lies in the book
debts, mostly balances outstanding on time-
payment purchases. The corpulence of this
item is shown among other things in the next
table: —
1906. 1911. 1913. 1914.
Stock . . . . £ 96.965 £ 209,271 £ 211.175 ,C 1 55.434
Book debts .. 124,289 175,791 182,477 198.078
Freeholds .. 77,093 92,451 82.159 86 643
leaseholds .. 14,093 26,751 64,220 70’148
Plant, etc. .. 17,354 18,428 19,033 21’215

That makes a total of £551,491 of assets;
and as capital and reserves are only
£428,000-odd. the co. lias to lean on the
bank and other creditors pretty heavily:-—-
_ 1906. 1912. 1913. 1914.
Mortgages .. £40,800 £42,000 £40,000 £38,000
Other debts.. 05,262 111,189 107,910 72,538
Total .. £106,062 £153,189 £147,910 £110,538
However, though the liabilities are still
large, the co. is in a much better position to
meet this war and drought trouble than it
easily might have been. By a fortunate re-
duction of stock it has been able to wipe
£37,000 off its own liabilities during the
year. Further, probably very few of its t.p.
customers have so far been really unable to
keep up their instalments. Thus, no doubt,
the outstanding book debts have been solidly
reduced during the last 24 months, and that
should have meant further shrinkage of lia-
bilities. The average Australian trailer has
had a lot of luck in one way: though war
and drought results combined may be very
serious presently, they didn’t get serious
suddenly—except in a few mining centres
like Broken Hill. So the retail trader, while
getting the straight tip to restrict credit,
has had the luck to be able to collect a lot
ot the money which a sudden catastrophe
would have made hopelessly bad. And the
luck of the retailer in this respect has of
course improved things for the wholesaler,
and for the banker who stands behind both
of them.

There are other ways of putting it. In the
first place, note that the reserves are de-
scribed as nominal throughout. Originally
£125,000 was paid by what was then called
the Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Co. for the
Dunlop patent rights for Australasia. In
the ordinary course this would have been
treated in The Bulletin as a hypothetical
asset, and a deficit of £125,000 would have
been indicated. That is to say the tangible
assets were £125,000 short of the capital.
Soon, however, things got comulicated by
the purchase of other rights, leases, etc.; and
as some of these may have been tangible
assets, the practice was adopted of including
the goodwill as an asset and showing the
reserves as nominal. Later on, when the co.
was reconstructed in 1911 and £172,500 of
bonus capital was issued, goodwill was
apparently written up. Any wav, that item
now stands alone at £180,000; so that, after
allowing for profits accumulated, there is
still £115,115 of the capital not represented
by tangible assets. This paper doesn’t for
a moment suggest that the intangible assets
are worth nothing. The right to make a
tyre and brand it “Dunlop” is certainly
worth money. But it isn’t anything you caii
measure; and it isn’t an item that should
remain on a balance-sheet a year longer than
necessary.
I he capital is now £520.000. made up as
follows :
Cumulative preference .. .. £ 200,000
Deferred non-cum. pref.. . .. 90 000
Ordinaries 230,000
In the old co. there were prefs. and ordin-
aries, the latter (mostly issued in the first
Place for goodwill) limited to 9 p.c. On the
reconstruction of the co. the ordinaries were
turned into second prefs., and a new lot of
ordinaries were issued —172.500 as bonus
shares, the balance at par. The position of
the shares is now' something like this: (1)
Cum. prefs. are entitled to a first 7 p.c., and
they also rank first in the event of liquid-
ation. (2) Second prefs. are next entitled to
• > p.c. (3) The ordinaries then come in for
10 p.c. (4) After the second prefs. have had
•’ P-c. and the ordinaries 10 p.c., the first
Prefs. are entitled to whatever else it is
thought proper to divide. Thus far it hasn’t
»een thought proper to give them more
Ilian 10 p.c. The position of these first
prefs. is unusually fine, On the other hand,
it is to be noted that, quite contrary to the
usual practice, it is the ordinaries whose
dividends are limited.
Though the goodwill item is large and
heavy stocks have to be maintained, the co.
manages to do very well without large
liabilities. The total is £93,812, partly mort-
gage. partly overdrafts and deposits.' partly
trade debts-—the last-named unexpectedly
... . 4 comparative smallness of the
liabilities—considerably smaller than in re-
cent years is due mainly to a reduction of
stocks and the wise accumulation of surplus
profits. In three years nearly £65.000 has
been available from the latter source for the
strengthening of the business; and that is
an achievement for which the business and
Hie management are mutually entitled to
give one another three cheers and loud
applause. There are not many businesses
that can pay an average of nearly 10 p.c. on
watered capital and then lay 7 by 7 an annual
t p.c.; and there are not so verv many
managements that would make that lav-bv
even if they could. However, the wisdom of
the policy will be made evident in the strenu-
ous times ahead of us; for the less borrowed
money a concern now has locked up and de-
manding interest, the less its worries are
likely to he.

Besides having the usual post-office branches
of the Commonwealth Savings Bank, S
Australia has a State Bank and also this
Savings Bank guaranteed by the Govern-
ment. Moreover, it not only has these two
State Banks, one of which is quite unneces-
sary. but both of them are gradually extend-
ing their overlapping organisations. This
Savings Bank has already 28 branches and
2(53 agencies, and it is dotting the- State with
new premises whose principal object, of
course, is to compete with the Common-
wealth Bank. That is to say, Brown as a
citizen of S. Aus. is permitting his money
to be employed in fighting himself as a citi-
zen of the Commonwealth. Brown’s leg is
being pulled in this manner, and his money
is being wasted, for two reasons; (1) If
there was only one bank instead of two (or
three, as a matter of fact), some now-wasted
salaries would be saved. Naturally the
people who are drawing the salaries would
rather see the waste than get the sack.
(2) If the Commonwealth Bank collected the
money the State Government might not be
so free to peck at it as it is at present.
That, however, is a matter which could and
should be arranged. There never has been a
time, indeed, when the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment hasn’t been willing to allow a most
liberal feed to the State cormorants. But
nothing has been arranged so far. and there
is consequent waste; and waste means weak-
ness.
In eight years the aggregate amount at
depositors’ credit has very nearly doubled :
1906 .. £4,750,192
1907 . . 5,304.704
1908 . . 5,803,301
1909 . . 6,328,870
1910 .. 6,771,150

1912.. 5,223,260

The accumulation hasn’t been so swift in
the last couple of years, though bigger in-
terest earnings should, of course, have made
it swifter. The truth is S. Aus. hasn’t been
feeling so well lately. Its wheat' yield last
year was under ~i bushels per acre ; and
this year it is going to be still less. How-
ever, while things were good the central
State maintained its old reputation for
being frugal and thrifty, and of every 100
people 56 have a trifle in this Savings Bank
—an extraordinary proportion, unmatched
perhaps anywhere else in the world. For
the whole Commonwealth the proportion is
only 40 per 100. The average amount per
depositor is not as great as in some of the
States ; but S. Aus. is the only part of the
Commonwealth where, if the whole of the
deposits were divided up, there would be
more than £2O a head for all the people of
the State. As might be judged by the vigor
with which the State supports the bank,
most of its funds are borrowed by the Gov-
ernment. Local public securities represent
£0,051,645, or 54 p.c. of all the assets, and
another 2J p.c. is on deposit at the Treasury.
There is 23 p.c. out on mortgage: and 71
p.c. is on fixed deposit with other banks. To
its exceedingly low expense rate and its
substantial reserve fund, this bank thus adds
the virtue of being energetic enough to
mostly find investments for itself, instead of
passing the job on to other banks.
