Of the profit shown for the past year £646
has fallen into the Contingency Reserve
without mentioning where it came from;
but it is pretty certain that it was the net
surplus over book values made on the sale
of properties in hand. Practically all these
properties have now been either got rid of
or written down to nothing or thereabouts.
Bor some years to 1012 the co. thought that
the less it said about such assets the better,
and they were consequently lumped with
advances on mortgage, the halt and the
blind being thus hidden among the sound
and the seeing. In 1012 a clean breast was
made of the whole matter ; but by that time
there were only £7112 of freeholds to con-
fess to. This year the total has been re-
duced to £1782. However, the co. has
£252,976 out on mortgage, and there’s plenty
of room for trouble there, more particularly
as no less than £88,225 has been advanced
during the past year. This means, not only
that the money lias been advanced at a time
when high values have ruled, but that the
co. hasn't been repaid much of the original
advances. The question now is: How many
of these borrowers are likely to throw the
securities on the co.’s hands? It depends,
of course, mostly upon the sort of time that
is ahead of Queensland. So far as one can
see, it shouldn’t lie a shockingly bad time.
For one thing. Queensland is pretty well
free from drought; in fact, the Darling
Downs are having a better-than-average
year. The wool market is as disorganised for
Queensland as tor the rest of Australia; but
the drought in the South won’t make Queens-
land s fat bullocks any less valuable. And
finally for its great sugar crop there is the
Australian waiting with the money in Ip’s
hand —a fine object-lesson in the value of a
home market. Altogether, then, though the
Northern State must suffer, inasmuch as it
bad many men employed on loan works
which must stop, and many others engaged
nr mining which lias stopped, its immediate
prospects are distinctly less dolorous than
those of any other mainland State.

From done to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council ( Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. pel cun.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ,
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. od.
per cub. yard, 'this result was obtained by using a
Bigelow ioin. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* I-

Extract from The City Review, London, January
17 1914 re The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society Ltd.“ This led to the appointment by the
Lieutenant-Governor of Victoria of certain distin-
guished actuaries to investigate the affairs otthe
Society, with the result that we anticipated. those
gentlemen reported that at the date of their investiga-
tion (December 31, 1911) the Society was sound and
solvent, and able to meet all its liabilities, immedi-
ate and prospective, and they expressed the emphatic
opinion that the charges of fraud, dishonesty, mis-
management and incapacity made against the dnee-
tors and managers were wholly unjustified. * LA

Idie object of the limitation is to increase
the competition. But obviously that couldn t
be done effectively if private sales were still
permitted; so the selling brokets
decided that, as from about a week ago,
there were to be no more sales by innate
treaty. This of course means that the •"1
ply is to be purely artificial, with the one
plain object of making purely
quotations. The growers thus find them
selves in this position : as only tins hand u
of wool in the aggregate is to be sold in
Sydnev at auction, and as no wool at au
is to be sold privately, they have precious
little chance of selling their clip in Aus-
tialia; and many of them are therefore
talking of sending it to London tor sale
which, for one thing, means a certain amount
of loss to Australia. The Selling Brokers As-
sociation may possibly have good reasons toi
prohibiting private sales after so severely
restricting auction sales; but at present it
looks just as bad a piece of union tyranny
as ever emanated from any trades hall.

Mount Baring, or “Dad's Millions, ’ about
eight miles from Oberon (N.S.NV.), is b ; * v ~
ing attention prior to a systematic test. Ihe
show is a large low-grade one, and if the
gold contents are sufficiently substantial
there is a very large supply of ore to operate
on. Most of the gold being fine, cyamding
will be an essential part of the process, and
at first an attempt will be made to obtain
payable results by dry-crushing and direct
cyanidation. L. O. Wilkinson, formerly ot
Stawell (Vie.), is in charge of the treat-
ment part of the venture. A good deal of
money has been spent on Mount Baring,
but it has never yet been tried on a large
scale, although a few small trial crushmgs,
bagged and carted to other works, have
yielded satisfactory results. As usual with
such surface deposits (no great depth has
been attained in the workings), assay values
are erratic, rich gossany. veins and poorer
lode matter being intermixed, but all tests
point to the wisdom of giving the proposi-
tion a trial. If it pans out satisfactorily,
Dad’s Millions will be a very attractive
proposition indeed, quantity being already
assured, and an improvement in quality
with further development is probable.

Negotiations have been in progress
between the N.S.W. Government and cer-
tain tin-buyers, with the object of inducing
the latter to resume business —anyway, for
•six months, as an experiment. The buyers,
however, don’t want to carry any of the real
risk. In other words, they propose simply
to make advances guaranteed by the _ Gov-
ernment. An apparently-hopeless. difficulty
has arisen with regard to the price which
should be offered. They say £BO for a TO
p.c. concentrate is as much as any sane per-
son should pay ; and the man who is inter-
ested in a tin show scoffs at the figure. Still,
the buyers easily remember the time when
stream tin sold at 15s. per unit, which means
£49 10s. per ton for a 70 p.c. concentrate ; so
they are not likely to agree that £BO is a
preposterously inadequate basis for the pro-
posed advance.
* * * *
“Derwent” : Sulman, a metallurgist, _ of
London, is “emphatic in the view that zinc-
smelting works could be erected in W ales
to cope with the full output of zinc concen-
trates from Broken Hill,” and thus permit
“England regaining the substantial portion
of the smelting industry which she lost to
German works during the past decade.” The
same Sulman “argued that the possession of
Welsh coal (which is exported to the Con-
tinent for the express purpose of dealing
with this product) and skilled labor made it
every way reasonable.” Queer how these
sleepers-awakened never consider the possi-
bility of Australia working no its own ores
into metal! The Freetrade lunatic who
believes in keeping Australia as a mere wood-
and-water joey for the Old World of. cheap
labor, will quote that same cheap labor as
proof against Australian spelter manufac-
ture on a big scale. But Australian labor,
if relatively dear, is proportionately good;
and, with the right use of water for power,
we can beat Swansea, anyway. Roughly,
it takes lh.p. per annum to decom-
pose a ton of zinc, and if move-
ment from coal costs £25 or so per
h.p. per annum, naturally working costs seem
prohibitive. But the Hydro Metallurgical
Works recently taken over by Tas. Govern-
ment can produce energy for £4 or £5 per
h.p. per annum, and therefore Tasmania is
the place for the Australian manufacture of
spelter. As to the comatose nature of the
English metallurgist, we have Sulman’s state-
ment that the British possession of Welsh
coal—“which is exported to the Continent
for the special purpose of dealing with this
product”—constitutes a reason why Broken
Hill concentrates should go to Wales. But
they’d best import first a few hard-headed
men who will be horrified at the spectacle of
a country so fat-headed in its devotion to
Freetrade that it lost a smelting trade to
Germany, and then supplied Germany with
coal to help it keep the trade.
Though the Commonwealth Oil Corpora-
tion’s shale-mines and works are still u e•
the British-Australian Co. has got thiough
its troubles, and its works at Murrurundi
and Newcastle (N.S.W.) are in operation.
Considerable supplies have been made to
the navy; and, generally, while the financial
results are not yet what they should be. a
thoroughly-satisfied market is being steadi.y
established for the various products.

Chaffers (W.A.) has resumed ore treat-
ment. In 1913, when the remodelling of the
mill and residue works was decided on, ore
in sight was said to be 105,000 tons, of a
recoverable value of 255. Od. Costs were
estimated at 235. 3d., with development
thrown in, leaving a possible profit of 2s. bd.
per ton. On a promised 6000 tons a
month this meant £750. But what share-
holders will get out of it won't keep them in
tobacco, and if the stock is to be worth hav-
ing the co. must lift the grade. Since tne
co.’s reconstruction a lot of fresh ground has
been broken ; and if values come up to any-
thing like the average indicated by official
assays, the lifting of the grade should be
possible. Unfortunately, the show has
always been spotted; hence little dependence
shouid be put on it.

Edna May Central (W.A.) has plenty of
ore if all that is reported is there; but the
yarns about continuous ounce crusliings are
nonsense. It won't manage much more than
half an ounce, unless it gets a turn-up in
depth, or a windfall from some other source.
The latest from the West is that the
Edna May and Central reefs are two dis-
tinct bodies which come together near the
boundary of the two shows and form a oO to
40 feet bulge.

Speakman’s, on Hampton Plains (W.A.),
is installing a 10-head mill and gas-producer
plant. This co. was formed in Melbourne
in June, to acquire the holdings of the Hamp-
ton Boulder and Hampton Properties, Ltd.
The amalgamation was recommended by J.

good chance of a quarter of a million or

From the Sheepshead run of stone. Bendigo,
Ironbark has paid £19,834 in dtvs.,_and still
has about 400 ft. to open up before it gets to
the Hercules and Energetic boundary. Work
is proceeding on the east leg and at a depth
of from 500 ft. down, the intention being to
open up the saddle on its pitch to the north.
South of the shaft the turnover of the saddle
gives a block of fair crushing dirt, but the
legs are not very fat. Victoria Consols,
south of Ironbark, is now getting the bencht
of the shoot in the cap of the same saddle,
hut the stone must carry more colors if it is
to live up to the present newspaper pulls.
The co. has-winzed down on the Central Blue
run of stone, and finds a few colors on which
to feed the imagination of the faithful who
have made the claim their sheet anchor.

matter wants probing. The present is no
time for cos. to play dog-in-the-manger.

In the art of writing reports to excuse
failures there isn’t a man in Australia wiui
more experience than J. S. Reid Etheridge
Gold Mines. Ltd., formed in 1911 under the
wing of Chillagoe. came a cropper within
two years. In 1913 another co. was formed,
and it got to work on the remodelling of the
almost-new plant. Says Reid as ’
“The alterations were completed and the
mill started on August 3. For the fortnight
during which the run continued the null
worked well, with good results, the recovery
being good, and the residues practically
valueless. Just then, however, the meta
markets of the world were closed through
the war. The concentrates were thus ren-
dered unsaleable, and the directors were
most reluctantly forced to suspend opera-
tions.” Of course you don’t get a word about
the financial results. In fact, though Ile
manager gives a report dated September 1.
not a word is said there about the plant oi
the run it had. except for a remark in a
footnote that it is “proved that the Queens-
lander ore can be treated most satistactonly
by the M.S. process.” The ore reserves are
still wretchedly small —16,000 tons in the
four mines—and the value, from 15 to -1
dwt., is nothing to talk about. As usual
with these Chillagoe and allied concerns,
the co. is in a bad financial way: as far back
as June 30, counting in estimated value ot
ore on hand, it had less than £2500 of liquic.
assets, and owed £2979.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, October 19, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
(Jbief Justice Cullen, who steps iuside the
glittering chancellor’s robe, in which the late
Dr. Normand MacLaurin looked like a Grand
Llama or something equally imposing, is
the first Australian-born citizen to fill the
recesses and suburbs of the garment. As
Chancellor of Sydney Uni. the new man
isn’t likely to peer into official details that
the Registrar-General’s clerk can see to, as
the late chief did. Dr. MacLaurin had
plenty of time to make a hobby of the
Senate and its frills, but C.J. Cullen will
preside amicably over its peace and storms
without dislocating his own duties, and may-
be the ’Varsity will be none the worse.
Apparently, by the way, the election of
C.J. Cullen wasn’t compassed without some
heartburnings. Backhouse, a District Court
Judge, has been the hardworking vice-
chancellor for many years; and after the
Chancellorship had been refilled, one of the
first jobs of the Senate was to accept the
V.-C.’s resignation. It seems to be a rather
serious thing to be a mere District Court
Judge.
The great recall to arms tears Aide Wake-
man from these shores. This affluent young
man was wandering round the globe for
pleasure when he strayed this road. There
happened to be a vacant niche in the sub-
regal Help Department, so the traveller
put down his swag at Government House.
As a matter of fact, no Governor in Aus-
tralia will be able to appear in dejected
Society with even the tamest species of
whiskerless warrior clanking at his heels
if the British War Office goes on drag-
netting all our best young men. Mr. Wake-
man is a lieutenant in the Grenadier Guards
and is curtly mentioned as being on the
reserve of officers. He has shed the title
“Captain,” which falls on every member of
a gubernatorial staff, and hies home to arm
for the fray.
The rain pattered down on the rubber-
tyred and the merely rubber-shod as they
fled through the wet streets to the Town
Hall, where a concert had been organised for
the French Red Cross by Mrs. Charvin
(pianist Yvonne Leverrier). Up the cold
stone steps panted the andience, and down
the same bleak address dashed the lady
pirates, clad in French and Belgian peasant
garb, to relieve the newcomers of their super-
fluous cash. Programmes, flags and flowers
were sold for the cause. The vigorous at-
tacking party included Miss Isabel Ramsay,
who fell upon the dazed pedestrians before
they had time to shake the rain out of their
front hair.
The programme gave every promise of
going on from Saturday eve till Sabbath
morn. When a band, mentioned in the list
of items as the “State Military Band.”
settled down to deal out patriotic ditties, it
did so in an apparently unshakable belief
that time was made for slaves. Mrs. Charvin,
Malcolm McEacharn, Bryce Carter, Mabel
Batchelor, Airs. Alex. Gordon, John Lem-
mone and W. J. Coad did their little bit to
push the musical coach along.
The interval brought the most popular
event of this raid on Cash—the auction sale
of some fine bunting. British and Belgian,
Servian and French colors brought fancy
prices. So did a Jap. flag. Wild cheering
greeted the Belgian flag, and the uproar
became frantic when the price ran up to
£ll5. The buyer was Mr. Field. Mrs.
Macarthur Onslow’s voice downed opposi-
tion when a bit of British bunting was
offered. The bidder secured it for £llO. Mr.
Marieli, whose strong right hand had given
the French Red Cross concert a vigorous
push, bought a picture of George Rex for
£lO, and gallantly handed it to a lady. All
told, the flag-selling must have added about
£7OO to the receipts. After that it didn’t
matter if half the rest of the proceedings had
to be lopped off, so that the audience might
get home before the Sunday morning milk-
man banged at the back gate along with the
belated cat.
The national costume dress parade had a
beautifully-garbed French peasant set.
Britannia, rather wobbly in her headgear,
led her forces on the French heels. Little
Servia and Belgium had their evening out,
and Maori (and was represented by a maiden
with as fine a head of hair hanging down her
back as ever you saw in a hairdresser’s
advertisement.
The Free Kindergarten babes demonstrate
the joyous effects of belonging to the cult of
the Unsmacked Child in the Town Hall on
October 24.
The Battle of Flowers is .still in the offing.
Owing to the downpour last Saturday the
organisers pushed the carnival forward
another week. Damsels who are beautilnl
and damsels who only think they are, and
damsels that other people think they are
(this sentence is mixed, but I am in haste)
will struggle to be crowned Oueen on Octo-
ber 24. in the Agricultural Society’s arena,
Moore Park. The Bed Cross Fund is to get
the profits.
Mrs. Joe Cook, some time ago, wrote a
letter to the daily press, giving in detail all
the convolutions of “knit round, purl round
and cast off loosely.” This hint was for
the benefit of well-meaning enthusiasts whose
knitting is full, of large holes and draughts
and lumpy knots. Some of the alleged
knitters might look up the instructions to
purl before they dash forward to disaster.
To cast off loosely, with infuriated language,
will be the job of Tommy Cornstalk when
he puts his toes or his head through some of
the home comforts supplied by half-baked
knitters.
A Fiji friend tells me that the tropic isles
are having an anxious time, and plate, jewels
and other valuables have already been re-
moved, so far as possible, from Suva to a
place where the enemy’s cruisers can’t do
them any damage.
Mrs. Watteeuw, the Belgian Consul’s
wife, lias had the bad luck to be captured
by a scarlet-fever germ, which has made her
prisoner in sick bay for a while. This sug-
gests a doubt as to the wisdom of sending
second-hand clothes to the destitute babes in
Belgium. There’s quite a reasonable chance
of forwarding, with the best intentions, a
generous supply of measles and scarlet fever
along with the petti, and the pinny. You
can’t look a child’s gift frock in the seams
and tell the story of its career from what
you see there. So the safest present is the
stuff which the myriads of women workers
make up for nothing.
Captain Macintosh, who goes south later
on to interview the Emperor Penguin and
his court, left Sydney for Hobart the other
day. He steers the Aurora into this port,
war permitting, next month. In these stir-
ring times it -seems rather a dull exploit to
bury yourself alive in Antarctic solitudes.
But the frozen glamor of the Pole can drag
strong men like a grappling hook.
The Condominium Court in the New
Hebrides has recently shed its Acting-
Prosecutor, Mr. Bengel, who goes to Europe
on leave of absence. Europe isn’t much of
a place for a cheerful holiday just now; on
the other hand, the New Hebrides is pro-
bably a good set of islands to get away from.
It seems only a short time since Count Some-
thing von Other, the gentleman with the
imposing profile who is Judge of the Condo-
minium Court, passed this way for a trip to
France.
Did you notice that King Carol of Rou-
mania has left a beautiful obituary notice of
himself in his will? He meekly forgives
those who doubted his good intentions, and
hopes his successor will follow his noble
example—all for country, nothing for self.
(I'.S. —The patriot’s personal property ex-
ceeds £4,000.000.)
After the enemy’s recent visit to Papeete,
the big stores of Donald and Edenborough
were amongst the ruins. They went to
blazes in the general conflagration. Senior
partner Donald, of this Auckland firm,
hasn’t been much in Maoriland of late years.
He and his wife spend most of their time
in England with a married daughter. She
was the well-dowered Agnes Donald, who
said “I will” to Lieutenant George Steer,
R.N., a few years ago. When the old
liingarooma (now on the scrap-heap, most
likely) used to wallow round these shores,
Mr. Steer’s large and cheerful presence was
favorably known in local Society. The
Donalds are wealthy enough to stand the
Papeete racket without being very hard hit,
though it knocks a temporary hole in their
fruit trade to and from the tropic isles.
Willy Wagtail and co. took a back bough
this year. Bird Day was only a mild
chirrup amongst the school children, though
the Gould Bird Lovers’ fraternity fore-
gathered as usual. The large Turner Hall
at the Technical College was the meeting-
place ; and there small boys listened meekly
to Mr. Dawson’s advice rc the respectful
admiration they w-ere to offer to the
feathered warblers of the bush, instead of
chasing them with traps, guns, clods and
clubs.
Dora Christoe, whose papa is in charge
of the E. S. and A. Bank in this city, has
plighted her troth (there’s a fine Family
Herald flavor about this statement) to JUr.
William Donald, of Townsville. Dora is
a tall and comely damsel, but I don’t know
whether she takes one or two lumps of sugar
in her tea, or plays goff as persistently as
her father.
Walter Bentley will—bar accidents —have
given a continuous lantern-slide picture of
the Oberammergau Passion Play before this
reaches you. The funds go to the Alex-
andra Hospital for sick babes, which is
suffering from a hungry feeling in its
treasury.
The unpatriotic rain is getting a confirmed
habit of falling in buekets-full on outdoor
sports for patriotic funds. While the
parched country is fishing hard in its creeks,
with the hope that a pint of water will
bite at the worm on the end of the
line, Sydney is getting more or less web-
footed. ' Some day, perhaps, science may
evolve a new kind of meteorologist who will
be able to adjust the badly-arranged shower-
baths from aloft. At present the incur-
able trouble is that the rain always falls
in the wet regions, whereas it should fall
in the dry ones.
The State Schools’ big display on the
Sydney Cricket Ground was splashed with
rain before the youngsters had finished all
the events. The rush for shelter was unani-
mous. A few hearty young persons made a
futile effort to crawl under the sub-regal
motor, but the vehicle was not designed as
a shelter shed, and presently they fled to
the stands. The Strickland lady camped in
the car with two young daughters, while his
Ex. poked round to see the inwards of the
show. The living Union Jack was con-
fused by the tossing of small flags, which
blurred the sharp red, white and blue. Other-
wise it was a brilliant spectacle. The
sports and dances would have been well
arranged if the weather had left them alone.
Professor Tearne- —in his new spring mil-
linery—was in charge of the infantine efforts
to split the welkin with the Marseillaise and
similar topical songs. The youngsters had
been so well drilled that a second helping of
the display ought to help the Alexandra
Hospital with a fat cheque.
Mollie Piper, a great-grand-daughter of
the late Captain John Piper, of the old
X.S.W. (or Rum-selling) Corps is engaged
to Hugh Wolrige, a son of Dr. Wolrige, of
Manly. The girl’s great-grandpa Piper was
Commandant at Norfolk Island and Col-
lector of Customs (Sydney) during the ex-
cited old days. He owned the Point Piper
estate, which afterwards passed into the
keeping of Daniel Cooper. Captain Piper
bought Vaucluse from Sir Henry Hayes,
and sold it again to William Wentworth.
He went over to the innumerable caravan of
the dead in the ’3o’s at Bathurst, after dab-
bling in the dairy business at Alloway Bank.
Lieut. Graham, R.A.N., was wed to Miss
Isobel Chalon, of Woollahra, the other even-
ing.
Another naval sprig belonging to one of
our own warships. Lieut. Douglas Cooper,
brought his new bride to this port the other
day. The ’groom is now" afloat on the rolling
main, though I’m oledged, in the interests of
the State, not to mention even to you, my
dear, exactly where he is rolling. Besides,
I don’t know.
The naval and military dance in the Salon
de Luxe last week was a well-intended effort
to collect cash to add to the AEI submarine
disaster fund. But the naval and military
persons present had a gloomy air of having
been hurriedly scratched together, and didn’t
amount to anything very numerous or beau-
tiful. A Patriotic concert w r here flags can be
sold at fabulous prices seems to be the one
sure method of dragging the citizen from his
domestic hob these nights. Mrs. Charlie
Wade, supported by the twins, arrived in the
early part of the evening. The Brownlow
girls—as fashionably lean as greyhounds—-
and the Macneill Simpsons trod the Salon
floor in the more or less mazy hops of the
moment. Some youngsters in sea-going out-
fit piped a patriotic lay, and were understood
to hurl dreadful threats, at a long range,
at the enemy. The Belgian Consul, hugging
his opera hat under one arm, looked in for
five minutes. Manager Michael Joseph
wrestled valiantly with the generally flat
feeling in the atmosphere. Nothing but a
bucketful of cream of tartar (which is
undergoing a crisis and can’t be flung away)
would have made the affair rise to a light
froth.
Citizens in search of information have
been making serious pilgrimages to the
Trade Schools’ Exhibits, now showing in
Anthony Hordern’s new" Fine Arts’ Gallery.
The show" closes on October 24. Notable
specimens of Australian w T ood in the raw,
and the same timber after it has been vigor-
ously attacked with French polish, incite
the person w T ho has a turn for carpentry to
go forth and slay a living cedar that he may
turn it into a handsome dead bureau. Tas-
manian stringy-bark, heavily disguised as
Eucalyptus obliqua, looks like English oak
when a good craftsman has wrnrked it into
a cute little cabinet. Local marble, in slabs
of many-colored patterns, includes a fairly
venerable relic. This is a mantelpiece, made
in 1834, of the first marble quarried in Aus-
tralia. It was chipped off at Marulan, where
nowadays the clattering milk cans meet the
morning train.
An exhibit from the Technical College
Museum which chains the imagination of
persons who eat heartily off mere earthen-
ware, is a facsimile of an American mil-
lionaire’s Cauldon China. It consists ot
one plate, with a rainbow trout in applied
art as the design. This specimen belonged
to a gorgeous dinner service, which helped
the jaded appetite of a dollar king. As each
course passed before his fastidious glance,
the china that served it was painted with
representations of fish, fowl or beef in their
native lairs.
From rainbow trout in applied art to the
plumbing department is only a slep or two.
But women are cold to the misapplied art
of the plumber who does the wash-house taps,
after frantic entreaties, and undoes the kit-
chen floor. So the Trade School tea parties
oi: last week removed their millinery to a
more congenial alcove. The Cullen dame
was in charge of one of those mild dissipa-
tions. On another day Mrs. Felix Booth
was generalissimo of the teapot.
( Enid Sly, daughter of Dr. J. D. Sly, of
Cordon, has taken a favorable view of
a proposition laid before her by Dr.
Boyle. _ The proposition was of the kind
which is supposed to be made in a garden of
roses on a moonlit night in summer, or in a
shaded corner of the lady’s father’s drawing-
room if the season is winter. It involves a
becoming blush, a fervent kiss, a large pur-
chase of clothes, and a parson of some kind
or another. Then all parties concerned pro-
ceed instantly to live happily ever after-
wards. The subject is one about which a
vast amount of poetry has failed to get into
print at different times.
The Rubber-tyre Edwardses (of Mel-
bourne when they’re at home) are motor-
ing around this city on a holiday jaunt.
There has been a good deal of rain to rubber
through, by the way, but it is so good for
the country that the city must go goloshed
in a spirit of resignation. Unfortunately,
the country that wanted it most has got least
of it—“least” tailing off to none at all.
Mr. Edwards and his fair-coiffured lady
propose to splash round in Sydney for
about a fortnight.
In spite of the revival of the flounce, it
is amazing how lean the fashionable figure
still looks. Banting must be the favorite
recreation of our more expensive suburbs,
for feminine Society assiduously aims at
the appearance of a flat deal board. In
its evening undress the board may be a
trifle bony, but the lady who could, with a
squeeze, glide through a keyhole is trium-
phant by day. She is the only person the
modern dress builders recognise in desisn-
ing expensive rags. Woe, woe unto the
comfortably fat.
“The Glad Eye,” at the Palace Theatre,
is a mirthful farce, French and frothy, and
full of laughter and nice frocks. Ethel Dane,
as the fascinating Paris shopgirl who rejoices
in the pet name “Kiki,” is an alluring minx.
With a shadow-like form that looks as if
the girl inside the demure black gown lived
on meringues and morning dew, Kiki fills
the best part of one act with riotous mirth.
The Chateau de Vercottes, where the scenes
are laid, is the usual light-comedy country
house, where the inhabitants chase each other
round chairs and tables, and fall up-stairs
and down-stairs with the ease of trained
acrobats. Lucienne (Alice Hamilton), the
mercenary young matron who is always on
the trail of new clothes, takes her husband's,
eccentricities more calmly than the iveepful
Suzanne. Lucienne has to be calm. When
she dazzles the company in an evening gown
whose corsage is built, so far as one can tell,
of a flower, a half-incli band of fur, and a
wisp of tulle, much agitation would burst
these frail bonds asunder. In that case, the
butler would have to render first aid by fling-
ing a tablecloth over the ruins. When no
tragedy happens, yon realise that nothing but
the calmness of Lucienne could have saved
the situation —and the frock.
Miss Tait, recently matron of the Darwin
Hospital, left Sydney to return to the North-
ern Territory in the Dutch boat Tasman.
After a whirling rush to gather up her
trousseau in this shopping centre she flees
with great joy to the warm, comfortable
North. The wedding takes place the end of
this month. Captain Lewis, formerly of the
13th Hussars, and now of Administrator
Gilruth’s staff, is the lucky man.
The new kind of shiny waterproof stuff,
in lugubrious black millinery, is having a
vogue hereabouts. The only thing to be
said about this hearse-like plumage is its
gloomy resemblance to some kind of marine
weed which had been dragged through a
slimy duck-pond, and then hurriedly dyed
black. Stiff little posies, with hairy faces
oit their futurist blooms, are stuck into the
crevices of the long, lop-eared black bows.
Millinery is a weird thing when you come
to think of it.
Shoes made of material that forms part of
the wearer’s anoarel are not calculated to
raise spasms ol envy in the leather-shod.
There was a bedroom-slipper appearance
about race-week footwear of this kind, and
the feet inside them have to be very small to
be presentable in buff brochg covering.
yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 30, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Caulfield Cup celebrations transpired
on Saturday in shiversome weather. Half-
way through the proceedings there was not a
warm nose on the lawn. There was a fairly
big crowd, but on close scrutiny I found it
to be mostly composed of mere People. Only
the faintest tinge of Blue Blood was observ-
able. One explanation of this calamity was
file unanimous defection of the Munro Fergu-
son clan. They were at Ballarat listening
to bagpipe contests!
Governor Stanley—who again had fo leave
his pretty little wife at home ill—was on
hand, and so was Norman Hodges. The
lonely potentate grouped himself in an ex-
treme corner of his blue plush coop, and pro-
ceeded to look as forlorn as a 305.-a-week
bank clerk, who, having backed a winning
outsider out of his moruing’s embezzlings,
discovers that he has had the ticket snatched.
In' sheer motherly good-nature the Richard
Casey dame, resplendent in check taffeta and
osprey and furs, bustled in and saved his
Ex. with racing tips and other rousing ver-
bal material.
The Cup Race itself was a thrill from the
start. Uncle Sam left the barrier a dead
last, and he pranced, full of victory, past
Ihe stand and winning-post in a frantic bl ur
of chestnut and green and scarlet. His Ex.
at this stage dried his tears, figuratively
speaking, and began to take an interest, in
life. lie is strongly suspected of having
backed the winner, but it may be mere
rumor, and, anyhow, we all know that vice-
regalities are too good and wise to gamble.
The Lawn and Stand were mostly navy blue
—the color at this stage of the sea-
son. Also, there was an absolute litter of
capes. They flapped gustily round their
purple-lipped wearers and gave spiteful slaps
in the eye to inoffensive strangers who were
frying to look round them at the racing. Mrs.
llennessy wore a four-decked erection of
shot green and purple, held down with
'possum furs, and a hat forested with black
heron tufts. Mrs. Herbert Power featured
white heron feathers, and Mrs. Cumming wore
fluffy white feathers, pink roses and black
main-work. The Lachlan McKinnon dame
supported quantities of sables, and Hilary
McKinnon loomed large and pleasant in
greeny-grey, and a black hat littered with
white feathers.
Three Baillieux were in the Stewards’
Pe*?v -one all brown satin, feather and
ninon (Mrs. Clive) ; a navy coat and skirt
edition (Mrs. Arthur) ; and a third (Yere)
in black tt'Ppe and blue bows. Irene Browne
was in the creme coat and skirt she wore in
“Man and Superman,” and near her was
Mrs. Stewart Brunton, in olue crepe de
chine. Mrs. Jas. Grice was upholstered in
bright blue feather-toppings and a blue coat
and skirt, and Ivy Clarke peered forth from
a wilderness of musquash and skunk.
Mrs. George Armstrong looked smart and
taking in navy blue fitted with cherry edg-
ings, and a white hat. She was with Miss
Mitchell, who affected black crSpe de chine
hangings. Mrs. Donald McKinnon was in
purple. The Fred Fairbairn lady sported
black, with a yellow punctuation. Jess
Fairbairn wore white cloth.
Henley will be a really important affair
this year, if only because, practically every
other festivity is cancelled. There are to
be ever so many house-boats or art-muslined
rafts, and as ail the takings (barring about
half a button, which is to be reserved
for charity) goes to the Red Cross
money-box, there will be nothing _to
prevent the Best People from attending
except deliberate pro-Germanism. The
Geo. Fairbairns have the use of the Gover-
nor’s boat. They have decided to feed starv-
ing vice-royalty aboard it. The Australian
Club has handed over its 100 ft. craft to the
Foundling Hospital and some sort of Home
in East Melbourne. The good old G.P. will
be enabled, accordingly, to plant its tired
feet on this sacred hulk, and Mrs. O’Hara
and Mrs. Fred Knight will see that they pay
their footing. The Overseas Club will be
imperialistieally in evidence all over the
place beneath many layers of the only three
colors that count these days.
A stupendous thing happened last week,
my dear. The Carthage of masculine jealousy
was destroyed. A sort of male Sadowa tran-
spired. I know you dislike this poetically
figurative style of mine, so I will simply men-
tion, with an affectation of calm which is all
too palpably forced and unreal, that Parlia-
ment has given women the right to be alder-
persons in Victoria ! Alderwomen, female
-mayors—we shall have them both shortly.
Oh! the glory of it 1 Our fearless champion,
E. J. Cotter, clinched the matter in the
House last Thursday; and when the final,
fateful words were spoken on the subject.
.Mrs. (Professor) Osborne, who was sitting
in the Speaker’s gallery, broke into masses
of tearful smiles or smileful tears, while Vida
Goldstein rushed in and emitted a war-whoop
of girlish delight that fairly woke the drowsy
old Chamber up. Now for au agitation in
favor of female archdeacons!
The Cafe Chantant in aid of the Belgian
Relief Fund over-ran the Oriental Hotel last
Thursday, from lunch- till dinner-time, and
would still be going strong if some of the
crowd had had their wilful way. Disaster
was averted by a small but fearless man,
who repelled a series of desperate attempts
4o penetrate the Saloon Bar-Lounge lines
with the dogged valor of a Joffre. In a position
n.n.w. of his left wing were grouped a social
army corps of privileged creatures in snowy
uniforms topped with black hats. The Bel-
gian colors were everywhere. And still this
public-house Horatius held the bridge, and
still the serried hordes of Society hurled them-
selves against him in vain. The uniformed
ones turned out later to be Bandits —of the
charitable variety. They sold programmes at
ss. each, bunches of lily of the valley at
4s. Gd. per spray, and sweets at about £lO an
ounce. Just as the people were beginning
to make inquiries for insolvency forms, with
the object of filing their schedules, the front-
row seats were put up at auction. Mrs.
Sanderson took three at 10s. each, but there
was nothing else doing in this line, so the
lonely defender, above-mentioned, was ordered
to fall back in good order, destroying all
drunks, spies, kleptomaniacs, amateur reci-
ters and other nuisances as he went, and the
crowd immediately rushed the seats in the
green beyond.
Epoch-making scenes followed. Mrs.
Alex. Landale, in black, and Mrs.
Winifred Forbes, hatted in something that
reeked of Paris, were everywhere at once.
Lesley Madden was prominent among the
programme sellers, and so was Dorothy
Merritt, the blooming young daughter of
M.L.C. Merritt, of Kew. This maiden scorned
the white-muslin conventionality, and did her
commendable blackmailing in smoke blue. An
orchestra, which was part professional,
part amateur, broke into the Belgian,
French and British National Anthems,
one after another, just as everyone was
comfortably seated. This enabled everyone
to get up again, and stay up till the melo-
dies ended. The star amateur was Mrs.
Stuart Murray, who draped herself in front
of the grand piano in a dream of lilac silk
panniers, buttoned up at each side with
naughty little French bows and lace, and a
black hat decorated with black ospreys 10
yards long, and pointing N.E. and S.W.
for all they were worth. I have said so much
about this lady's clothes that I have no
room for a detailed criticism of her turn;
but it was great. Irene Browne reached the
piano with a kind of running-jump step,
and committed a prelude with an appalling
name as though she did such things for a
living. A Belgian flag, hand-painted by
artist Fischer and autographed by the G.-G.,
was auctioned by Daisy Jerome. Ellen
Terry bid £5O for it, but she had no chance
against Edward Solomon, who appears to
be determined to save the Empire with his
cheque-book or perish in the adventure. He
got the emblem for £lOO. Programmes signed
“Ronald M.F.” were next disposed of. Then
Ellen Terry snatched the Belgian-colored
ribbon trimmings from her bouquet and put
them up. All the “profession” shrieked bids
at one another for this line, and it was
knocked down to Irene Browne at £l2. The
Belgian Consul was discovered in the crowd
and dragged on to the platform, where in
ruinous English he tried to express his grate-
ful feelings ; and just as everyone was yearn-
ing for tea, Mrs. I. H. Moss, in mole and a
blue-ribboned spring hat, sprang to the front
and spoke impassioned words about Belgium,
the artists, the keeper of the Oriental Pub
and other things till the tired and the
hungry were very tired and very hungry.
Altogether it was a great and festive occa-
sion.
The atmosphere at the final meeting of
the Patriotic Flower Rally at the Town Hall
was distinctly electric. When one devoted
patriot got pn her feet, a sort of horrified
hush settled on all present, for had she not.
accused a rival worker in the cause of being
“no lady,” impelled another to bathe the civic
furniture with her tears, and caused yet
another to swoon from sheer rage and stress
of emotion? However, she got through with-
out an explosion. There was another period
when the rally threatened to develop into a
plain melee or free fight, and Mrs. Ilen-
nessy’s tact and Miss Guthrie’s persistence
were needed to darn the jagged rents in
Charity’s mantle. But all simmered
down in the end, and the £3SO-odd, collected
from the public, was duly allocated. Besides
the money that went to the three Patriotic
Funds, the Girls’ Training Home and the
Talbot Colony for Epileptics each got a
cheque for £37.
In the very flower of his usefulness, vice-
regal sec. Blackstoek lias been stricken down
by the measles. Mrs. Lorna Price Mills is
another victim of the nursery ailment; also
a Oliomely girl, and her pal, Colomba Back.
Just now measles are quite the thing. In
fact, they are It. Mumps, which were the
correct affliction a couple of years ago, have,
for gome reason, become absolutely dowdy
of late.
The A.D.C. famine still rages, and now, tu
make things even more *£h a
Luttrel. of the Federal staff, is down with a
feverish cold. Panic-stricken, moic 01 les..
the G.-G. has cabled for help to the one and
only Lord Richard. But he has but fa nt hope
that the famous social expeit will be
able at a time like this.
Norman Hodges, who is. alLthe Gentleman
Help that the harassed Vic. State Goveinoi
possesses, nearly blew himself oft the map
last week, lie made the mistake of smoking
a cigarette while filling the petrol tank of his
car, or throwing a lighted lamp into the
lubricating apparatus, or something equ. y
casual. You wfl be relieved to leam that
his beauty has not suffered permanently
One cheek might be classed as damaged, but
the rest of him is intact, thank Heaven.
Colonel F. T. Hughes has dropped aiding
the G.-G., and taken command of a Light
Horse Brigade. Lieut.-Col. Gwynn, duet
tor of the Duntroon R. M. College, and
('apt IL L. Waller, R.E.. of the same
dusty spot, are also off to look for trouble.
The G.-G. and Lady Helen stayed for a
couple of days with Admiral Bridges, at
Trewalla, last week. Miss Mamleville aul
I-leli) Luttrell were with them. Nothing
unusual occurred. They just put m4<
hours with an elderly ex-naval man and
then came home again. Still 1 thought 1
might as well mention it.
Amber Reeves, the pretty daughter ot
Maori land’s ex-lligh Commissioner in Lon-
don, has been mentioned in despatches, so
to speak, for a fine book entitled ‘ A Lady
and Her Husband.” This is her second
effort, and it is so crowded with Thought
that the bookbinders had a job to get the
cover on. She is a Maorilander by
but was educated at Kensington High
School, Newnham. and other places where
they drive in learning with a siege gun.
Ruby Ward, daughter of H. S. K. Ward,
who is in the grain trade, was tied irrevoca-
bly (so far as you and I are concerned) to
one Arthur Richards on October 12. The
feat of legerdemain was performed by a cleri-
cal prestidigitatepr, of acknowledged merit,
at the Congregational Church. St. lvilda, and
the lookers-on were afterwards given nourish-
ment at the Grand Hotel. The latter or
honeymoon part of the show is being put on
in Tasmania.
Minnie Graut-Bruee, the writer, who
married a cousin, and so kept her own name,
is off to Europe by the Nestor next week,
ller Major husband has been ordered to re-
turn and hurl himself at the embattled, might
of Germany, and she insists on going to
watch him do it; She only got back here
last May, and feels a bit blue at leaving her
people again, but duty must be done. There
is no doubt, by the way, as to the Major
being constructed of the real old hero stuff.
He faced a tea-party of the Women Writers’
Club, given chiefly in his honor, with the
cool, debonair nerve of a Soult or Ne.v or
whoever it was that Napoleon called “the
bravest of the brave.”
The amiable and good-looking Walter
Withers, who died suddenly last week, was a
pleasing artist, though it would be inaccurate
to style him a Michael Angelo, or anything of
that sort. “Tranquil Winter” and “The
Silent Gums,” two of his best, are hung in
the Melbourne National Gallery, and most
of the other States house some of his works.
He was a trustee of the National Picture
shop at the time of liis death.
The Robert Harpers struck England just
in time for the war, and when last heard of
they were mitigating the horror of the situa-
tion by motoring in Wales. They hope to be
back here before this summer has joined the
great majority. Meantime young William
Harper, he of the niany motor break-ups, is
tearing about the U.S. accompanied by a
good motor and bad health.
Ellen Terry is off to the U.S. towards the
end of this month. After a season of hard
labor in ’Frisco and N’Yark she will return
to her little Kentish farm, or whatever it is.
That is, if the Germans haven’t ruined it.
Now that Antwerp has succumbed, the ruth-
less foe could easily swim over and devastate
her hencoop—provided always the British
navy didn’t interfere.
From my Adelaide correspondent-
The weather for the Morphettville races was awful,
and the track arose at short intervals and distri-
buted itself over the wilting farms in the neighbor-
hood. Otherwise the meeting was a success—that is,
when a fair discount is allowed for the drought and
the war. The dressing w T as slightly mixed. There
were winter coats and skirts and furs, and there
were thin, wispy garments of muslin and such-like,
the latter in some eases very low of neck and skimpy
of skirt. The women who scored were those who
happened on a judicious something that suggested
spring and defied wjnter—notably an attractive black
taffeta worn by Lady Galway, a smartly-tailored
white cloth coat and skirt which bedecked Mrs. A.
M. Cudmore, and Mrs. T. E. Barr Smith’s blue crepe
de chine.
The opening of the Adelaide Rowing' Club occurred
on Saturday. Sounds of revelry were heard on the
Torrens, which, by the way, is turning an art shade
of greenish yellow, and they were traced to boat-
races, a string band and afternoon tea. Nothing
thrilling transpired—lt never does these drab times.
Father Le Maitre and the necessities of the Belgian
Belief Fund packed the Town Hall on Tuesday even-
ing. The lecturer is a rotund, rubicund person with
' nice sense of humor and an amazing grip of the
vagaries of the English language. His accent especi-
ally when he warms to his subject, is un-English ana
broken and so it came as an added surprise to heai
from him a choice of words and a rounding of
phrases which would have done credit to Deakin him-
self He gives the impression of an abiding good
nature but for all that he dealt it out to W flhelm
of * Genua nv with no niggard tongue. The Governor
Is in the chair, and the votes of thanks were
attended to by Belgian-Consul W. 11. Phillips, Japan-
ese-Consul Herbert Parsons, Coadjutor-Archbishop
Spence and Register editor M. J. Sowden. The
SSL was mostly R.C., and gave the show the
atmosphere of a McCormick concert.
The latest idea is Patriotic Bridge. Jhe procediue
varies. Sometimes stray shillings are gathered in by
a collection; sometimes there is a levy on the stakes.
I can clearly see Patriotic Poker and Imperial C ub
nights coming for the men, as a measure of retal.a-
tion. It will be a great excuse for the dissolute male
who blunders home at about 4 gun. to be able, to
point out that he has been assisting to down Ger-
many, and that anyone who dares to chide him is an
enemy of the Empire. .
From Pinaroo, which is in the mallee scrub in the
direction of the Victorian border, comes news of two
aeroplanes that were seen cruising over Murray ville.
Also doubtless, from Oodnadatta and llergott and
Tarcoola and Charlotte Waters. The capacity some
folks have for detecting aeroplanes in war-time is
astonishing.
“Billv Khaki,” words by Edward Dyson,
music by Miss Dorothy Boyes, is an Allan
nublication that gives Australian singers of
soldier songs just about all they ask for—
rousing verse that tramps on its own teet
and good, simple, lilting music. It has
caught the ear of the Billy Khaki boys,
and they should have the eight verses and
chorus bv heart long before Kaiser W illiam
gets cold feet. But they’ll have to hurry up.
Pen. Boyd is showing 80 landscapes in
the Athenaeum picture shelter, and intends
to go on doing it till Saturday, Oct. -4.
The work is of a high order, and the prices
are printed in plain figures in the catalogue.
Ilis “liiver Study,” a large, tuneful oil,
is marked £2OO, and a couple of
Warrandyte views bear the legend,
£l5O. There is a fine strain of op-
timism about the man who is capable of
mentioning such figures at such a time as
this. He ought to take a job as an hon-
orary Cossack and ride on the road to
Berlin. His Titian-headed wife moves
alluringly among the pictures in a series of
smart French hats, and the perpetrator ot
the works is nearly always on hand himself.
The ladies of the Overseas Club have at
length found suitable rooms. They are on
the second floor of a building, which, for all
that it is in the naughty old Block, is as
respectable as an archbishop’s umbrella. One
of the rooms is done in art sage green, with
oak accompaniments, and is fitted up with
band-painted screens (pastels by artist May
George), and a secretary. It is one of the
most ideal spots for nose-powdering purposes
that I have seen for many a long day.
Mrs. I. 11. Moss has deliberately ceased
to be vice-president of the Tie. Wimmin’s
League,. and, as an expression of regret, or,
mayhap, because they temporarily mistook
her, iu their agony, for a departing trooper,
her colleagues gave her a diamond-studded
wristlet watch and wept bitterly upon her
shoulder. All this at the Club rooms last
week. She has been a vice-president for
five years, and has been a positive Terror
to Socialism during all that period.
The new civil appointments in Samoa
have been filled by M.L. expeditionaries.
The Commissioner of Customs is a strap-
ping young sergeant, the District Judge is
a corporal, and the Collector of Customs
and the Governor of the Gaol are both de-
serving privates. There weren’t enough
captains and lieutenants to go round, and
that Avas where the rank and file came in.
Miss Lenore Eddington has become en-
gaged to Surgeon Barr, of the Melbourne.
He was a Bananalander until his father
came over to Victoria and became a natural-
ised Toorakian.
The Army Nurses have been chosen. There
are four of them. Think of it, girls, with a
man-drought raging, and the prospect ot
worse to come —-think, I beg of you, of being
one of four females aboard a troopship
containing hundreds of delicious soldiers, and
no Mrs. Grundy! That is, if thinking on
such subjects doesn’t render you liable to
explode from envy. 'J'he only black-beetle in
the consomm4 is the uniform. _ It has been
designed apparently to matrimonially pro-
tect the Empire’s defenders. A grey dress,
with a little red cape, leads up to a dreadful
cap or bonnet, which perches upon the
brow like a Dimple on a grandstand. Kits
are limited to regulation size, so the poor
things can’t take race hats to counteract the
effect of the working togs. Those who want
to bring an extra pair of stockings must leave
out a toothbrush, and so on. Grand-pianos
are absolutely barred.
Yours, till Johanna’s return,
ANNE SMITH.

of the memory. In Dewar’s Whisky there is
something creamy and yet not cloying;

something rich and yet not obtrusive; some-

THIN AND PUNY CHILD
PASSED THREE LARGE STOMACH
WORMS AFTER TWO DOSES OF
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM
PELLETS. HEALTH AND STRENGTH
NOW GREATLY IMPROVED.
The following voluntary statement re-
ceived from Mrs. Peter McGrath, 78 Hor-
dern Street, Newtown, Sydney, N.S.W.,
describing the anxiety which her little girl
caused her, and the rapid improvement in
health after three stomach worms were
expelled by Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets, should be valuable information for
parents whose children are suffering in a
similar way. One of the worms referred to
has been preserved, and may be seen at the
office of this Company.
“Since 12 months of age my child was
thin and puny, and backward in walking,
and I was unable to discover the cause of
her suffering,” states Mrs. McGrath, “but
attributed it to teething, and I was very
worried regarding her health, fearing that
I would lose her. Thinking that she might
possibly have worms, at different times dur-
ing this period I tried various remedies,
but without any result. About three weeks
ago. acting on the advice of a neighbor, I
decided to try Comstock’s “Dead Shot”
Worm Pellets, so obtained a packet at the
chemist’s. After the first dose she passed
a stomach worm 13 inches long, and
after the second dose, a few days later, she
passed two more stomach worms, one
18 and the other eight inches long.
Although the time that has elapsed since
then is very short my little girl has picked
up wonderfully and gained weight. The
child is now two years of age, and it is
only since passing the worms that she has
been? able to walk properly. I am only too
pleased to allow you to ,publish this state-
ment, and would confirm same to anyone
calling on me.”
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation
for the eradication of worms in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers. price 2s. 9d. per packet; or will be
sent post paid at the same price by The
W. H. Comstock Co.. Ltd., 28 Lang Street,
Church Hill, Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Pity Holland, between the Devil and the
deep sea fleet! If the Dutchman permits
Germany to use Antwerp as a naval base,
or as a port through which it may stock
up, the Allies will fall upon him —and then
what will become of the apple of his eye,
lii.s East Indian treasures, from Sumatra
through Java and Celebes to Papua?
And if he refuses right-of-way to the gentle
German, there is the case of Belgium to
remind him of what the gentle German may
do to his home, which is the apple of his
oilier eye.
WAR FINANCE.
N.S.W. TREASURE HOLMAN: “’ S’oright!
They wont explode. I’ll get ’nother
bottle, pour it over ’em, put ’em out."
Last Tuesday a meeting of 219 N.S.W.
civil servants gathered in Sydney, and
decided by a majority of three —lll to 108
—that the service should life registered as a
Trades Union. The registration is a com-
mendable idea, but there are over 14,000
civil servants in the State, and the assump-
tion thfit nobody need have a voice in the
matter unless he lives in Sydney and is
aide to attend a meeting held in Sydney is
calculated to make many of them feel angry.
Some of them think there is too much Syd-
ney.
Twenty years ago Maoriland had two fac-
tories turning out glassware. One was in
Auckland and the other close to Christ-
church. The quality of the goods was un-
questioned. and the supply of raw material
more unquestioned still. But cheap foreign
labor, added to low freights by subsidised
German steamers, and supplemented by a
rotten tariff, killed the local article, and
for some years Maoriland has been dragging
its glass half-way round the world. Now
that importing has suddenly become difficult
there is a howl for the revival of the in-
dustry; but. although the sand is still there,
no one is likely to touch it unless the tariff
is wrestled with first of all. And there
doesn’t seem much chance of that while
Farmer Massey, the friend of the Big Im-
porter. is at the helm.
BILL’S TRUST IN HIS ALLY.
The Kaiser is well defended by troops, aeroplanes,
etc., and his armored train is decorated with the
Red Cross. — Cable.
BERLIN B ILL: “Is our Royal and Imperial
person well guarded?”
THE L ACKEY: “Brave and Imperial master,
your person is defended by all the effective
means at our disposal, and you are also
under the protection of the Red Cross, at
which the enemy do not fire.”
BILL: “It is good. Commend our brave per-
son to the care of God.”
Where are all the unbusinesslike acts of
the Fisher Government of 1912-13, as dis-
covered by J. Cook and W. Kelly during
their time of brief authority? The earlier
Fisher Government decided on a. floating dock
at Cockburn Sound (W.A.), having well sat-
isfied itself that a graving-dock was impracti-
cable. When Cook came to power, in 1913,
the floating dock work was scoffed at and
'stopped, principally because the reptile con-
temporary had started it, and expert Maurice
Fitzmauri.ee was asked to return to Aus-
tralia and report. lie didn’t return, but
cabled that the “difficulties attending grav-
ing dock are prohibitive; I therefore recom-
mend floating dock.” So Pearce’s Depart-
ment resumes the work which the Nasty
Little Men interrupted.
The papers are still discussing the Ger-
man capture of Antwerp and asking why
the Kaiser was such a fool as to waste so
much effort on it. Napoleon said: “Ant-
werp is a pistol directed at England’s heart.”
Germany, no doubt, expects to win—and
that’s when it expects to reap the benefit of
the capture of Antwerp. “The punishment
of England will be to see a German naval
base at Antwerp,” a German wrote nearly
three months ago. Certainly there’s Holland
in the way. But Belgium was in the way
too.
“Uloola” is scornful: —
Where is the sense in The Bulletin going on
with its absurd untruths about the aridity ot Can-
berra? We on the spot know differently. The water
from the Murrumbidgee, Cotter, Queanbeyan,
Molonglo, Yass and Goodradigbee rivers passes
through the Burrinjuck Dam. There are four lines
of pipes through the wall, each provided with a
valve which can be closed altogether, or opened from
jin. to 4ft. In addition, there are two sluices each
10ft. high by 6ft. wide, fitted with ,iron doors, which
also can be adjusted to whatever opening is re-
quired. At the present moment all four valves are
open lin. ; one sluice is closed ; and the other is
open 14in. The water in the dam is stationary, so
the combined flow of the six rivers above-mentioned
is actually percolating through these pinholes. 1
will admit that the winter rains have had something
to do with this splendid performance. Last summer
two pipes, each open Sin., failed to drain the reser-
voir of all the water that the six rivers were pouring
in. The Bulletin may argue that the Snowy River
would have had Burrinjuck full to overflowing long
ago. No doubt. My point is that even from the
mighty rivers that flow through Canberra, aided by
others, we can get a certain amount of water into
the dam. And, after all, you can’t expect every-
thing from a capital site. Look how much nearer
Canberra is to Sydney than Dalgety is.
The Melbourne Harbor Trust is building,
by contract, a pier at Port Melbourne. n
the pier there is to be a steel shed,
course the Trust didn’t dream ot getti. »
the shed made here. Nor yet in Lritani-
It arranged that the needed materials—tnc
columns, beams, joists, etc. should a
according to the German-Aniencan stan-
dards. and in those circumstances a German
firm naturally got the contract. At presen
the Trust’s chairman is busy explaining
matters, with poor success; but what the
public--would like to know is—who is the
individual so obsessed with the idea ot Gei-
•nan superiority that he' imposes its stan-
dards on public bodies in distant British
dominions? Whoever lie is, he is wasted in
his present position : he should be assisting
Yon Kluck or advising the Kaiser.
There are signs that the
U.S. mean to start a sort <)f
commercial Armageddon. The
Trusts consider that the time
is ripe to make a complete cap-
ture of the foreign trade of
.South America, S'Africa, Asia.
Australasia and the Dutch East
Indies. and to do it so
thoroughly that, when the war
is over, it shall not be lost. A
U.S. trade paper, the Philadel-
phia Commercial Museum,
puts United States exports to
Australia for 193.2 down at
$45,900,900; to Maoriland,
$10.000,000: and Dutch East
Indies, $2,200,000, and points
out that Australia imported
about eight times that value
from countries which are now
at war. And it sees no reason
why the United States should
not secure all their business.
Australia, on the other hand,
sees no reason why it shouldn’t start to
manufacture a lot of this merchandise itself;
and the clamor for an effective tariff grows in
volume accordingly.
The days are passed when the king on
this side of the creek threw a stone hammer
at the queen on the other side and there was
war within half an hour. From the day
Austria sent the first demand to Servia till
war was declared between Germany and
England, the official correspondence totalled
1(50 portentous letters and telegrams, reckon-
ing only the correspondence in which
Britain had a hand. Sir Edward Grey, the
Foreign Minister, sent 02, nearly every one
of them a part of a brief but desperate
struggle to push the inevitable war a little
further into the distance.

THE MINE.
Our United States brother is something
of a weariness. It was cabled one day last
week that President Wilson didn’t think his
Uncle Sam country should supply a little
food gratis to the people of Brussels —mot to
the combatants, for there were no com-
batants left in Brussels; but to the im-
poverished civilians —without the consent of
the German authorities, because the grand
old Belgian capital had become German terri-
tory. A man with an exaggerated, conscience
might take Wilson’s view honestly. enough,
lmt the view ought at least to be consistent.
The United States are now raving over
schemes to annex German trade. They want
to supply goods to all sorts of combatants
for cash, without asking any permission from
German authorities. Even in the Australian
papers one sees the advertisement of the
neutral Yankee urging this country to buy
his output instead of that of the. German.
When it is a matter of trading off Tor dollars
the Standard Oil Trust’s grease, or the In-
ternational harvester, or the Huckleberry
piano, or the pig that is killea atiChicago
to one of the Allies, there seems'rto be no
difficulty whatever. But diplomatic compli-
cations stand in the way of giving” a crust
to the Allies’ widow.
Britain has started a belated:-, effort to
“capture German trade.” Even: tie Free-
traders are realising that many of the millions
which their country paid
annually for German goods
went in wages of Krupp gun-
builders. Yet, though Britain
wants to collar the Fatherland's
trade now, there is no hint of
any intention to protect its
manufacturers from- German
competition- after the war is
over. Collaring the” enemy’s
trade, and putting no tariff wall
round it. is like capturing an
eagle and turning it loose in
the backyard, with definite in-
structions that it isn’t to go
past the ash-barrel by the gate.
There will be some sharp
howls of pain if ever the amend-
ing Local Government Bill
reaches the N. S. Wales Leg.
Council —assuming that it goes
through the Assembly in some-
thing like its drafted . form. It
says that women may be local
councillors, and that the muni-
cipal franchise shall be on a
one-adult-one-vote basis, .with
the addition that absentee prop-
puty may also have : a vote.
Under it authority. will be
vested in shires or municipali-
ties to supply gas or electricity,
conduct the undertakery busi-
ness, run light railways and
tramways, erect buildings for
sale or letting, register dogs,
establish milk depQts and
restaurants, manufacture and
supply bricks and tiles, hawk
fuel, meat, dairy produce, forage, cooked
provisions, clothing, building materials,
bread, cakes and biscuits; control public
amusements and games and lodging-houses.
■ and go, in fact, the whole socialistic tiger-
hog. ;
Adelaide Chamber of Manufactures post-
pones its 1915 exhibition till better times.
1> holds one every three years, and claims
to have introduced the idea, now so popular,
of showing what can be locally made, and
appealing to the puh'ic not to jfeed the
foreigner. For its next show it had-Planned
to work not on State but on Commonwealth
lines, and to include all Australian-made
goods ; but war and drought together" make
the prospect too uninviting to face.-
THE CYCLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some fifty years ago the Prussian rent
A tiny land, a gallant land, in twain,
And Britain’s ear was stopped when skyward went
The wronged one's call in vain.
Aye, though we stood full pledged to the hilt,
We raised no sword, wo lent no helping hand ;
And Fate, forgetful ne’er of blood and guilt,
Comes now in Gallic land, •
And pits us, staunch of heart and stern of face,
Against the Teuton warriors stark and grim,
And bids us wipe away that story base,
And spare not life nor limb,
Driving us onward with her deadly scourge
To meet the foe on bloody Hill and plain,
Until in expiation full we purge
Our sin against the Dane.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
