SPORTING
NOTIONS
When St. Carwyne won the Spring Stakes
on the first day of the recent ltandwick meeting, and
at once jumped to the position of “moral” for the-
Metropolitan. a lot of people suddenly remembered
the ease of Positano 17 years ago. “PoSi” won the
same race unexpectedly, and his ■ Met. chance was
a lovely thing—on paper. Cautious old sports who
didn’t risk a wager once in six months came crawling
out of their shells to put their fivers and tenners on
him for the Metropolitan., lie let them Sn. Jle hail
an erratic temper, and was already more rpf a stallion
than a racer in disposition : and lie rawer repeated
his brilliant performance in the Spring Stakes. The
4-year-old St. Carwyne did go near to proving him-
self the good thing he appeared to be ; and had the
chestnut with the white markings actually won the
Metrop., a number of old sports would have kicked
themselves for remembering tlie Positano ease.
* * . * *
The punters were not the only people who
thought Royal Laddie a cert, for the Epsom Handi-
cap. Trainer McGrath reckoned the Ayr Laddie horse
the best thing he had ever sent to the post; and
McLaughlin, who had the mount on Malt Mark, told
his friends that McGrath's favorite couldn’t be beaten.
Probably a lot lpore money is going to be lost over
Royal Laddie. fie carried a sack of quids in the
Toorak Handicap, a week after the Epsom, and
finished fifth. HB is one of the born deceivers, judged
by his record up"to date.
* » * *
Remarked that Victorian horses, as a
rule, get less benefit from a trip to Sydney in the
spring than Sydney gees get from a change to the
Melbourne air. Adelaide, in the early winter, is the
best health resort, for Victorians. Still a week’s
galloping at Randwick seems to improve some of
them. For instance, Rathlea, who disappointed his
friends in the Epsom and Metrop., scored a quite
brilliant win for the Toorak Handicap after being
sent back to Melbourne in disgrace.
* * * *
lownit (from S.A.). winner of the New-
market, had been in training at Melbourne for many
weeks with a Caulfield Cup victory in view ; but this
time the perky little gee didn’t thrive during his
preparation. He ran badly once or twice before he
was backed for the Toorak Handicap, although, to
the writer's eves, he looked very different from the
lownit who did the big trick last autumn.- Again
he ran badly ; also, he got a nasty bump or two, and
when the Caulfield Cup acceptances appeared there
was no lownit on the list, liis hopeful backers had
dropped their coin, into the grave which lkey digs
for early fancies. Handicapped at 7st. 131 b., the
same weight he carried in the Newmarket, the South
Australian seemed to have a second-to-none chance
for the C.C. if he could stay 12 furlough. But could
he stay? The question is held over.
* * * *
What are the odds against the Melbourne
Cup going to an imported horse, for the second time
in its history? Comedy King, imported as a foal,
won the Cup four years ago. Voyou and Hallowmas
were runners-up who came from England ; but with
these three exceptions foreigners have failed in the
famous two-miles race. Even Almissa, who subse-
quently won the Australian” Cup from Oadohia and
Piastre, could get no nearer than fifth. This year the
chances of St. Spasa and Grey Spear, both from
England, are particularly bright. St. Spasa seemed
to be tiring ere he got home for the Metropolitan,
but the extra month may count in his favor, and it
has to be noted that lie ran his best race as a two-
years-old over seven furlongs and a mile. Grey Spear
whipped in tlie field for the Met. what time his com-
patriot St. Spasa was passing the judge’s box. They
were the Alpha and Omega of the race,. and Grey
Spear will meet the winner on only 21b. better terms
at Flemington. But meanwhile 11. R. Denison’s horse
has proved his liking for a distance by capturing the
Waverlev Handicap (14 furlongs) in handsome style.
The Melbourne Cup is Mr. Denison’s particular objec-
tive, and all going well with Carbine's grandson, lie
should carry his Bst. 31b. near to the front.
Seeing that Uncle Sam won the Caulfield
Cup in open defiance of this paper’s suggestions, a
few words of apology are called for. Briefly then,
the writer, who tipped Uncle Samuel for the Toorak
Handicap and Caulfield Cup, which he won two
years ago, and for the Melbourne Cup, in which he
ran third to Piastre and Hallowmas, had become pre-
judiced against the big chestnut horse for reasons too
indelicate to mention. The evidence of his own eyes
when he watched Uncle Sam in the saddling paddock,
not once or twice, but several times, convinced him
(hat ihe nervous racer, in spite of his long, free stride
and natural pluck, would never again win a great
race over a longer distance than (say) 10 furlongs.
The prejudice held good last season when Uncle Sam
scored a victory in his first race (nine furlongs) and
five subsequent defeats over longer distances. This
season, as a six-year-old, he started not so well as
before, and though he didn’t look quite so nervous he
certainly appeared no stronger. A burst of hot
weather would probably have put him back into his
evil courses; but the week or two before Caulfield
Cup happened to be exceptionally cold, and so Uncle
Sam came to the scratch in a cool and composed con-
dition. Carrying 151 b. more than he won with in
THE PUZZLER PUZZLER.
Kay is unable to understand where Griffiths'
left comes from. (Sydney Stadium.)
1912 he ran almost as brilliantly. To a horse of his
size tlie extra weight would make little or no differ-
ence. Still, it is a fact that, however good the stal-
wart son of United States may have been last Satur-
day, lie wasn't the mighty champion he might 'have
been. Ilis chance in the Melbourne Cup race, for
which a 101 b. penalty would not stop him, depends
mainly on the weather.
* * * *
Twenty horses started for Caulfield Cup,
the 21st acceptor, Sylvanmore, being scratched (as
ihe result of the knocking about he got whilst pushing
his way to win (lie Coongy Handicap on the previous
Wednesday). Anna Carlovna (3 to 1), St. Carwyne
(7 to 2), and Uncle Sam (9 to 2) were the sinfplly
short-priced favorites. The betting otherwise was
“10 to 1 bar three,” and “20 to 1 bar six.” Aurifer,
obviously above himself in condition, assisted Garlin,
First Principle, Mintstone, Anna Carlovna and St.
Carwyne to make most of the running for a some-
what bunched field. It was not a very strung-out
field, and Uncle Sam, at the extreme tail end, never
looked hopelessly out of it. A quarter of a mile from
home St. Carwyne was leading from Anna Carlovna,
Garlin and Uncle Sam, with Rathlea coming after.
It looked as though the mare could dart ahead when-
ever she wanted to ; but in a moment she was beaten.
Tier foreleg, which looked dicky before tlie race,
practically broke down, and she finished nowhere —-
lame. Garlin also dropped out, and, whilst Uncle
Sam continued to stride along, St. Carwyne must have
tired or “chucked it up.” Anyhow, Rathlea was the
only one that challenged Uncle at the finish, and even
then the ex-sprinter finished more than two
lengths from tlie winner. St. Carwyne was
a length and a half farther back. Rathlea
will meet Uncle Sam on 111 b. better terms in the
Melbourne Cup, and had he not been penalised 71b.
for winning the Toorak Handicap he would look
very dangerous for the Melbourne Cup. For two
years Rathlea has been treated as a miler, whereas
he was probably a two-miler in disguise.
# * # *
No previous winner of the Caulfield Cup
got quite so big a reception as Uncle Sam. It was
the money that talked for the most. part, for U.S. was
the adored of the small punters. But behind the cash
consideration there was the sudden feeling that jockey
Ettershank had been wrongly accused of not knowing
how to ride the horse.
* * * •
The success of jockey ,T. Ettershank in the
Caulfield Cup lifts him to the diamond ridge hitherto
occupied only by IV. 11. McLachlan, Mick O’Brien
and Tom Clayton—the latter two now dead. They
four are the only riders who have twice won tlie
gilded utensil since it was inaugurated 34 weary,
dreary years ago. R. Lewis, a good, careful rider,
has had 15 mounts and lias yet to ride a winner. The
hick of the game!
* * * *
Third in a Melbourne Cup is certainly a
better performance than first in many other handi-
caps. Yet Ulva's Isle, who ran third to Posinatus
and Belove in the big two-miler last year, ''asabe
to claim 71b. allowance in a mere Trial Stakes at the
recent Randwiok Spring meeting, and that while ie
topped the market for ihe coming Melbourne Uup.
He won the Trial Stakes, naturally, just as Piastre
did before getting home in the Melbourne Uup. But
the eases are not as similar as some scribes make
out. Piastre, prior to his Trial Stakes, was not a
Melbourne Cup placed horse, nor was he a Cup lator-
ite at any time. Still, Ulva’s Isle may win the Cup
—-favorites have scored occasionally.
* * * *
Consider the waxing of pony racing
around Svdnev. Last week Kensington Spring Uup,
worth £785, drew a world’s record of 84 starters,
and had to be run off in five heats and a final, the
day’s racing slopped over five solid hours, during
which there was hardly time to get a bite or a sup.
The harvest reaped by Brian McShortodds was
tremendous, as instead of six he had sixteen races to
bet on —-and only two favorites won. lalk about tne
green bav tree and Jonah’s gourd! An unpleasan
feature of the day was the smash in the first division
of the Cup, which resulted in the death of top-
weight Yarrum. Jockey Gaynor, assessed as guiltj
of the spill, was disqualified for life. What a day .
And they call it Sport!
There was a curious jostling of names ■ in
the Glenelg (S.A.) Handicap recently, when
Epistle beat Pistol King for first place ,and the
latter was chased for second place by Pistolier. lh e )’
were all sons, of course, of the sire Pistol. The first
two paid £5 11s. and £3 2s. respectively, and the
third one had the consolation of explaining that the
other two had a year more experience and strength
than he.
* * * *
Bill Murray, Vic.’s amateur champion
walker, put up a great performance at the Richmond
racecourse on Saturday, when lie trod a fair 25
miles in 3 hours 58min. lasec. His most formidable
opponent was W. Pitt, an ex-Birmingham walker,
but two others did remarkably well—Drayton and
Dooley. Given a good surface road, such as that on
which Butler, the London policeman, did the distance
in 3 hours 1 Oinin. 20sec., Murray is a dead certainty
to clip lumps off his Saturday figures.
Port Adelaide football club is now happy.
It has done everything that a club could do in the
Australian game. (1) It went through the whole
season unbeaten. (2) It beat the premiers of the
local Amateur League. (3) It beat Carlton, premiers
of Melbourne. (4) It has now beaten, handsomely,
a team from all the other Adelaide clubs put together.
There doesn’t seem to be any other test in sight.
On Saturday afternoon the windows of
Heaven were opened over Sydney, and the flood swept
away all sports. Cricket was off, bowls were post-
poned, cycling stopped, athletics did not start. So,
as far as Sydney was concerned, there was no sport.
But no one complained—the drought had broken;
anyway it had broken where there was no drought
to break, and in a few other places it was slightly
cracked. The one bright exception in the grey
atmosphere was the fight in the Stadium. This raged
at night, as usual; and in spite of horrible
weather, drew over 5000 of an audience. The
sailing races on the Harbor were not bright excep-
tions, lint mighty dreary experiences for those who
followed them.
* * * *
The second round of First-Grade Cricket
was declared an all-round draw. Hard luck for
University, which, under the circumstances, was
almost sure to inflict a drubbing on North Sydney,
with only 52 to get and six wickets in hand. Red-
fern also had a rosy outlook in its struggle with
Western Suburbs, with eight wickets standing and
123 to get. Glebe, with. 217 in hand, had laid out
four Balmain wickets for 14. Central Cumberland
had 316 in hand against Middle Harbor, and would
have taken some beating. The result of the all-round
draw is to leave Central Cumberland and North
Sydney level in the front rank with one win and one
drawn game each. University and Gordon have one
first-innings victory and one draw each. So has
Western Suburbs. There the list of winners ceases.
#** 1 *
The annual meeting of the Australian
Board of Cricket Control was held in Melbourne on
Thursday, under the presidency of Queensland dele-
gate J. Allen, and resulted in the election of 11.
Blinman (S.A.) as chairman for the ensuing year.
The selection is a good one, the appointee being a
sound cricketer and a highly-placed civil servant
with a profound sense of responsibility. A South
Australian edition of Syd Gregory in size anil merit,
lie played for his State in home matches, but was
rarely able to visit Melbourne or Sydney. In his day
he was a mighty fieldsman, with a swift and deadly
return, and a sound batsman who narrowly escaped
brilliancy. Originally associated with the Norwood
Club, the electoral system pushed him into East
Adelaide, and lie took an active part in the develop-
ment of his new club, which included Clem Hill, Joe
Travers and Gehrs.
The Board is disinclined to go on with ai inter-
national campaign during a period of national stress,
and is leaving it to Marylebone Club lo say .vlier,her
an English team should come to Australia . yhe
1915-16 season, and also if Australia should s- • d a
team to South Africa. This paper is of the opinion
the whole international programme should be sus-
pended while the war is on. The money is wanted
for other objects. For the rest, the Board decided
to further communicate with Marylebone on the sub-
ject of giving guarantees for international tours. It
agreed to the Marylebone • proposal that matches
between the English and State Xl’s should be limited
to four and a half days, hut declined to endorse the
idea that test matches should be confined to five and
a half days. Its idea is that they should be played
to a finish. Marylebone had asked that 200 be the
limit for a follow-on in Australia, and the Board
acquiesced, but it declined to agree to the English
proposal that the wicket be not swept and rolled
during a match, holding that the conditions in Aus-
tralia make the attention absolutely necessary. Mary-
lebone should have no difficulty in changing its mind
on this point.
* * * *
Saturday was a horrible day for a regatta ;
nevertheless N.S.W. Rowing Association made a game
effort to open its season with the usual pomp and
frill. But it was foredoomed, and when the Maiden
Eights and Champion Fours had been disposed of the
function fell overboard. Both the races held were
good, and showed that the crews had trained unusually
well. Five started in the Champion Fours, which
was won by Enterprise No. 1 crew, after a great
struggle with the same club’s No. 2 boat, while
Balmain always hovered dangerously near. For a
mile a decent-sized sail would have covered the fleet,
but with half a mile to go the leaders made an error
in steering, and No. 1 Enterprise dashed to the
front, followed by Balmain, after which Leichhardt
splashed into third place. The Maiden Eights was .
a good struggle for three parts of the way. Then
most of the crews went to pieces, and Leichhardt
paddled home an easy winner.
* * * *
Sydney surf-bathers seem to be unusually
unlucky or careless of late. At Manly, in one day
recently, burlv beach life-saver Tom Gunning rescued
no less than 22 surfers from positions in which they
got glimpses of the pearly gates. If that rate is con-
tinued the life-savers will die of exhaustion, and then
the Devil will make a fine haul.
Evidently news of the idiocy of Sydney
surfers has reached the sharks, and the brutes ai e
making an early visit to the beaches. A couple ot
lift, grey nurses were landed at Coogee and Bondi.
Thev were not the only ones around ; but the others
were looking for human shark-bait, and declined the
hook. Unless some official restrictions are placed on
the bait, there are going to be tragedies'shortly.
Brisbane is consumed witli a desire to
make its patriotic carnival, organised by Reg Baker,
bigger and more successful than the one staged in
Svdnev. The Major supplied Mr. Baker with an
office in the Town Hall; the Overland Motors gave
the use of three cars for organisation work; in fact,
everybody is offering all kinds of assistance, and that
the carnival will be a roaring success is now assured.
* * * *
Duke Ivalianamoku, the Hawaiian swim-
ming marvel, is as inconsistent as a girl with tawnj
hair and golden eves, lie lias been threatening to
visit Australasia on and off for months now, and
changing his mind every week. Recently the bust -
ness was definitely fixed up*,, the money arranged for,
and everything in the garden was so lovely that the
various swimming associations were proceeding to
alter their constitutions to allow their members to
race with the brown marvel. Now it i$ all, appar-
ently, off. But this time it is not the Duke’s fault.
The trouble is the money. Bast week Victoria threw
in the sponge owing to shallowness of the purse.
This week Maoriland shows signs of collapse. The
V S W bodv, however, swears, like Britain over the
war, that “it will see the thing through!” If it
does it will find the Duke a millstone where it usually
wears its necktie. t ,
N.S.W. Bowling Association has thrown
out the district scheme and reverted to its sectional
system for the 1911 -’.15 competitions. Altogether
there are 21 metropolitan clubs in the three sections,
and a committee of four —Shadforth, Whitehouso,
Laurence and Hillman —shoulders the unthankful job
of dissecting tlie clubs.
* * * *
Les Darcy’s blacksmitliing indentures
have been cancelled, and he has definitely committed
himself to a pugilistic career. If he continues to
improve in his boxing as he has done of late, and
can acquire a cool control of himself, he has a big
future before him. In fact, he may win to world-
championship honors. But, first, he must find his
head.
Arrived from Vankeeland. by. the Ventura
last Monday Jeff Smith, Pat Bradley and three new
U.S.A. boxers, J. Coffey, Loughrev and “Young Otto."
Boxers in the U.S.A. are having a cold time, and the
Stadium has been inundated with telegraphic offers
to make the Australian trip. To the average Yankee
boxer shivering in the cold wind of the war-depres-
sion, Australia seems just now a land of gold.
* * * * . 4%:
If the roofing of the Melbourne Stadium
can be completed in time, Cup week will see a couple
of interesting scraps to mark the re-opening: .left'
Smith will meet Dave of that ilk, and Hughic
Mohegan and Fred Kay will battle for the light-
weight championship of Australia. Jeff and Dave
Smith should make a very interesting pair, as both
are excellent boxers. The fact that Jeff has beaten
McGoorty, who defeated Dave with such perfect ease,
does not' affect the problem. Paper form is not worth
a tinker’s howl or old boots as regards boxing. Writer
suspects that David lias the heavier punch of the two,
although more than once Jeff has demonstrated that
he can hit. The beauty of the fight between
Kay and Mehegan all depends on what sort of fight
Kay puts up. He will certainly have to either
hit more frequently than he did in his bitterness with
Johnny Griffiths last Saturday night, or gallop for
his lii'e. On the other hand, Mehegan, in his fight
with McOov, demonstrated that he had picked up a
new lease of speed somewhere, and was hitting harder
than ever, and that is saying a good deal, for Hughic
always was a mighty walloper.
* * * *
Australian lightweight boxer Fred Kay
had a strenuous time last week —underwent a slight
local operation on Tuesday, cot married on Friday,
and fought 20 rounds on Saturday. Under more
favorable conditions he would have -walloped U.S.
citizen Johnny Griffiths. In fact, even under the
.adverse conditions of the meeting lie might have done
the trick if lie had hit 15 times as often and hall
as quick again. No man can win a modern fight by
simply -evading punishment—lie must hit the other
fellow more or less frequently. Ivay had Griffiths
completely disconcerted by his unconventional style,
leading with the right and crossing with the left, lie
had a considerable advantage of reach, and could
always outrange Griffiths, and when he did fight, which
was seldom, he sent Johnny staggering. But he was
dog-tired. He bent bis head back out of reach till,
to get to it, Johnny had to walk half-way up his
stomach. Griffiths bored in in bulldog, style, and
occasionally be would get Kay on the ropes, where
the Australian twined his arms three times round his
head and covered his jaws and allowed Johnny to
belabor liis arms and gloves till be backed off in
disgust and helplessness. But Kay would not hit,
or did it so slowly that the. target always escaped.
The only time he scored was when he stood up
and met the American with straight rights.
While lie did that Johnny could not get near him.
Early in the fight Kay decided to go for a knock-out.
and smote with a force no one dreamed he possessed.
But his heavy shooting was always too slow, and at
last the affair, in which Griffiths hunted Kay round
the ring, and could do nothing with him when he
caught him, became wearisome. At the end of the
20 rounds Harald Baker gave the only possible
decision, and Kay remarked that a hot bath struck
him as the sweetest thing on earth. All the same,
this writer, who rarely prophesies, ventures to pre-
dict that next time they meet the young U.S. citizen
is in for a mighty bad time.
FIVE LITTLE SKINNERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out-of 16 races only three favorites won, and two
of these were in deciding heats. So much had many
of the bookmakers won after the fifth race that they
closed up their bags and retired to secluded corners
to count their winnings. —Account of a suburban race
meeting during the ,weel:.
“The others may follow their luck if they choose,”
Said the bookies who fled from the ling ;
“But I’m of opinion il fellow won’t lose
lVho knows liow to hold a good thing.
My comrades may bet on a coming event
To pay for their supper and wine,
But I’m going home with a spirit content,
For five little skinners are mine.
“You never can tell when the times will he bad,
And you'll want a few shillings in siore,
For the squatters and farmers are growling like
mad,
While the Germans continue to score.
The fates have but little in bounty to give
To the ‘book’ on his perilous way,
So the struggle is hard, Init a fellow can live
If they grant him five skinners a day.
“The punter comes down like a wolf on the fold
To rob me of all I possess;
So once I have flayed him his skin I shall hold
With my odds growing prudently less.
If seven to one is the price he requires
I shall scare him with seven to two;
Though at times 1 may fail of my utmost
desires,
Yet five little skinners will do.
“It may be that famine won’t happen to
tweak '•
The tummies of those I support,
If five little skinners occur once a week
And the oddsj are consistently short.
If a three-to-one risk is put out at odijs on
The punter must pay for his chance,
And with five pots boiled over with five races gone,
It is time for a Miriam's dance.”
It was thus that the bookie sang deep in his
throat
As lie packed up his hag for retreat,
But when 1 came near him and murmured “The
Tote,”
He called me a rogue and a cheat.
He convincingly, proved with a paper and pen
That the Tote meant the Devil to pay,
And he swore he was faced with insolvency, when
He’d had five little skinners that, day!
N.S.W.
M.

When the Vic. Protectionists’ Association,
which suspended animation before the death
of David Syme, came to light again
the other night, some misguided maffickers
talked of giving as much as 50 per
cent. preference to British goods—as
if the cheap labor of England were
only half as bad as the cheap labor of any
other country. Barring (say) Carty Salmon
and Theodore Fink, the committee elected
was such a marvellous collection of Ilas-
Beens and Never-Weres that the interesting
question arises: If Mauger and Edgar had
been elected as Fusionist senators, and
b reetrader Cook, being in power, had dropped
the Tariff “owing to the war.” would anv-
thing have been heard of the resurrection of
the Protectionists’ Association?
The whirling merry-go-round of History:
On December 31,. 1663, Mr. Samuel Pepys
wrote in his diary:
. - T , he J ’ urk ? jery far entered into Germany, and all
that part of the world at a loss what to expect from
hie proceedings. Myself, blessed be God! in a
8 °?- C L way ’ an(1 design sticking to my business to get
a little money with, doing the best service I can to
the King also; which God continue! So ends the
old year.
Poor, old-fashioned Pepys! If he were
alive to-day and in Australia, he would be
tiding to raise a, loan, or kicking up a row
because the enemy's ship with the imported
goods aboard hadn’t arrived. When war
strikes a low-tariff country very few of its
inhabitants can make money by sticking
to business. Business mostly falls down
dead and looks untidy.

SYSTEM IS ESSENTIAL IN
MODERN BUSINESS.
w
w
m
sx^
<6
44 90 per cent, of the failures in retail
shops are due to lack of system.”
The large commercial agency making the above statement
has had many years’ experience of retail shops and knows the
facts concerning it.
If yon cannot be sure that your method of handling your
cash will tell you to the penny how much you should have
in your cash drawer, your system must be weak, because it per-
mits of losses by mistakes and carelessness.
All losses must come out of what should
be your net profit. Without a positive system for
checking your takings' you do not know— you cannot even
guess —how much you arc losing.
Why not investigate the merits of National Cash Registers?
It will cost you nothing to learn about the most perfect and
effective system in the world. Over 1,350,000 "National”
systems arc in use, covering every kind of business —large or
small. •
The National Cash Register Co. of Australasia, ltd.

About, the heroes who lingered on: —
(1) When Bill Smith heard his country’s call, and
announced that he was going to enlist, his family
wept tumultuously on his bosom, and his work-mates
tendered him a smoke-concert and presented him with
a p a i r 0 f field-glasses to watch the enemy through.
They also gave him a wristlet watch so that he
would know the time to expect the enemy.
(2) A few days later Bill got his fighting clothes
and went into camp. As he expected to leave next
morning all the women wept over him and the men
wrung his hand.
(3) A couple of nights later Bill arrived home,
wet through, and said his camp had been flooded out.
The regiment had been told to go home and say
good-bye. The women made an effort, and wept over
him again. , . . , .
(4) Bill took part in a route march, which had
been arranged f or the benefit of the public. All his
women waved and wept from windows. Friends and
the papers called him “a fine body of men.”
(5) a. reverend gentleman wrote to ihe papers on
behalf of Bill, pointing out that the evenings were
long in camp, and would kindly disposed persons
supply Bill with harmless parlor games so that lie
would not he tempted to go into town and drink,
lie had to do-something while he was waiting to go
to the war.
(C) When he was Invited for the fourth time to go
home and say good u.. e- to his people, Bill almost
got himself court-martialled for retorting that “he’d
b e d—d if he would!” However, in consideration of
his previous good character, the oHieer commanding
said he would overlook the offence and allow Bill to
go to the war.
(7) Bill almost became a misanthrope when his
most devoted visitors started asking him when he
was likely to start for the war. On such occasions
he would go sadly away and watch the tower of a
distant pub through his presentation field-glasses.
Time hung so heavy on his hands that lie thought the
wristlet watch must be chronically slow, and gave it
away.
(8) The day after Kitchener announced that the war
couldn’t last much longer, someone surprised Bill by-
telling him that he was going grey. Bill thereupon
looked closely at his camp-mates and discovered that
they, too, were ageing. They had very few visitors
now, and the papers hardly ever referred to them.
(9) Bill’s heart broke the day lie read in a
borrowed paper that a pompous but ill-informed
Liberal politician had formally asked the l’remier of
the day if be bad any official knowledge regarding
the bands of old men in fancy dress who had infested
the city so long.
“Harrison 0.”: When is Melbourne Re-
pertory Theatre going to give the Australian
dramatist an opportunity on the stage of a
theatre V If Arthur Adams’s play, “Mrs. Pretty
and the Premier.” was good enough for two
nights at the Athcmeum it. justified at least
one performance at, a regular theatre, where
director McMahon periodically offers the
work of foreign highbrows. Also, sonic Re-
pertory patrons are beginning to ask if it
is pure coincidence that local authors almost
invariably get the weakest of the Repertory
players. In “Mrs. Pretty” only two minor
characters in a. cast of 1.3 were done any-
thing like justice. Most of the others wore
not even forgivably amateurish.
“Ilorunui” : “Midcliannel s description of
Shaw’s “Mrs. Warren’s Profession” as
“slabs of lewdness” is irritating. Personally.
I think it one of the most chaste plays ever
written. Probably “Midcliannel” would pre-
fer “The Merry Widow.” with its heap of
sensual suggestion set forth in a fascinating
and tempting manner. Shaw’s white slave
traffic exposure is given with a grim and
repulsive hideousness, but it is more effica-
cious than a gospel lesson. Where Shaw
has given offence to so many is in placing
the finger on the key to the whole vital ques-
tion. viz., our competitive system of econo-
mics, which breeds the creature of the
underworld as inevitably as it breeds the
social butterfly.
“Record Room”: T. K. Tilton, who plays
Wu’s major-domo in “Mr. Wu,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, is a young Maorilander with a.
jrood deal of Australian experience. In
M.L. he acted professionally and coached the
gentle amateur. He also committed sev-
eral plays of his own. With Ethel Buck-
ley’s touring company, in N.S.W. and
Queensland, he did good work as a rapid
producer: and with Beaumont Smith, m
Sydnev. he has shown himself a capable
stage 'manager. Tilton recently married
Miss Pearl Hellmrich. whose dad’s name,
as Mayor, is inscribed on the Paddington
(Sydney) Town Hall. Mrs. Tilton, who
has had experience in both drama and
vaudeville, plays the English flapper. Hilda
Gregory, in the Melbourne production of
“Wu.”' '
“Harrison O.”: One of the merits of the
Melbourne Repertory Theatre's productions
i= that the voung are played by the young.
In Galsworthy’s “Joy” the boy and girl lovers
were a couple of crude amateurs ; yet they
were fairly convincing because they were
youngsters." whereas, in a professional pro-
duction, they would probably have been mis-
represented by middle-aged mimes. Amateur
artlessness comes as a pleasant change alter
professional artistry, raid youth, on account
of its rarity, becomes almost a virtue.
THE ELUSIVE NAME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Russians have begun the siege of
It's only a Russian could get his tongue
Round a word like that!
To the average Britisher —old or young
Whether he’s sober or whether lie's ‘ sprung
The resources of language go bung .
With a word like that!
Przemysl !
May I frizzle
If I can pronounce you!
All the same,
Despite your name,
Ivan's going to trounce you.
It’s only a Russian who'd have the heart
To tackle a place named that!
A chap who, like Ivan, is slow to start
And not very sudden or showily smart:
But soon it won’t be on the Austrian chart
You can bet on that!
Przemysl
Will frizzle.
A reprisal
Waits for you, as stated ;
Be resigned,
Make up your mind
To be obliterated!

PETRONIOUS EARBITER.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Peri Winkle” : The (Try. spell in the north
of Victoria should mean, a goldmine for every
settler who can boast a/ decent irrigated
lucerne patch. As much as fid an acre is
being paid for a five-months' grazing .ease
at Bamawm, and £2OO a month has keen
secured by a settler for the use of his 14b-
acre block. For all that, it may pay the
settlers better to cut and stack tlieir lucerne,
whereby they may earn up to £2O an acre
during the summer. Besides, when a lease
H let. the sheep kings see that their nocks
get their money’s worth when on the land,
and the vegetable is ■ eaten so close the
miracle is it ever grows again. The Hunter
Tliver variety is a miraculous kind ot
fodder when watered and cut scientifically.
You can see it growing in the warm weather,
and the more often it is cut at the right
time tiie better it seems to like it. As much
as £7 a toil Will be realised for the fodder,
trussed, during the coming hot weather: ana
this may*help to banish the eternal _ tend-
ency of the average irrigalionist to grizzle.
* • * '* *
-diKattgarooster”: “Ivuseobin” is off the
track in prophesying (B. 17/9/T4) that
Yacca gum will go up in price owing
t > the war. Unfortunately, the market for
it is Hamburg; and, as that port is closed,
the flourishing little industry is settled, un-
less Senator Pearce gets to work with
another ammunition factory. Besides being
of value for its picric acid, this- gum is used
in making varnish, in clarifying wine, and
in manufacturing linoleum. Why cant we
do all these things in Australia? Kangaroo
Island is the only place where there is
much first-class gum; that from the grass-
trees on the mainland is much poorer. As
for prices; a German ring in Hamburg has
been controlling them, and the shippers here
have as usual been the pigeons.
*' * * *
There is easy money for the local horse-
breaker these days. The last Australian
Government, when it set about mounting the
different State Expeditionary Forms, made
a beginning by buying up all the vanner s
and baker’s, butcher’s and milkmans horses
it could get. These animals —young, active,
solid and not too big—represent the exact
type which the military authorities require.
Then the Government buyers began to
operate out back. There has been no sys-
tematic breeding on the part of any horse-
owner of the military type of prad. buen
as appeared before the Government agents
were pure flukes. Still n fair number
e> entuated at the combined call of patriot-
ism and ready cash. There must be a lot
more of the same type, though in an un-
broken state, in the remotenesses of this
great land —more particularly in Queens-
land. And every one of them will be
wanted before the second Expeditionary
Force Leaves these shores. .Che skilled
horse-master and breaker who bogs m at
once, snaps up all the likely-looking animals
that are available, and reduces them to a
peaceful frame of mind, will make a ittb
fortune,

A wanderer from soil to soil
The hapless farmer gees,
With back all aching from his toil
And sweat upon his nose.
In vain he works with hardihood
All round the weary earth,
For no one pets him as lie should
Be petted for his worth.
Australia is a fertile land,
With sunshine every day;
But, oil, lie cannot understand
The taxes he must pay.
He fain would find a Paradise
From all taxation free,
So packs his bag, and in a trice
Is steaming to Fiji.
Full many a fellow’s uncle made
A fortune over there,
But latterly, I'm much afraid,
There’s little left to share.
The ancient Burden presses down
The shoulders of the whites,
While magistrates with awful frown
Uphold the nigger’s rights.
“It’s me to go across the wave
And seek the Argentine,
Where niggers have no souls to save—
Ho, Buenos Ayres for mine!”
Alas, too soon he drinks the cup
Of sorrow; he is bust;
And all his hopes are jiggered up
When he confronts the Trust.
He wants so little here below,
This farmer lone and sad !
Only ten thousand roods or so
Of soil that’s not so bad ;
Only exemption from the tax
That all the world must bear;
And niggers humping on their backs
The crop they mustn't share.
He cannot settle down in France,
For France is full of gore,
And taxes aren’t a circumstance
To losses made in war.
At Merrie England's gate he knocks
But cannot find content,
For there the farmer pawns his socks
To pay his monthly rent.
I'm sorry for the fanner who,
With modest tastes like that,
Can never find a country to
Become his habitat.
He’ll wander low and wander high,
A man of wrath and pain,
Until I think he’ll have to try
Australia once again.
N.B.W.
A. M.

IN
BANANA LAND
“W.”: A recent statement that the
Queensland banana industry is passing from
Chinese to white growers is misleading. Pro-
duction has increased in the South, where the
Chow was never a factor; in the North Ah
Fat has run it to a standstill, exports from
Cairns and Innisfail having fallen over SO
per cent. The explanation of the disaster is
that the Chow grower is not concerned about
the condition of the land, and exercises no
discrimination in selection, cultivation or
marketing, whilst his orchards contaiii and
distribute all available pests. By preponder-
ance of production he reduces the value of
white growers’ superior goods to his own
level. This is the cgse with maize as well as
vegetables. The Chow influence on Northern
agriculture and horticulture is supreme,
which may account for the readiness with
which the Alien Leases Act can be, and is,
evaded; and empty North Queensland will
never be much less empty until this, misuse
of land by Asiatics is prevented.
“Meona,” on a well-preserved Old master:
Jack Hamilton, one of the most picturesque figures
of Queensland’s picturesque past, and for 28 years
in its Parliament, has been lately in the Stanthorpe
district with his keen grey eyes on molybdenite.
Age does not wither nor custom stale his infinite
variety; but to the present generation “Jack” is
remembered chiefly for his amazing accuracy with
the revolver, for which he holds the world’s record.
His life has been packed with more incident than a
score of ordinary ones. On the Palmer diggings he
was the doctor—in a way he was forced into it —and
he had the largest, if not the most profitable, prac-
tice in Australia, 5000 polyglot patients passing
through his hands yearly. At that time there were
19,000 Chinese and 16,000 whites on the field.
Hamilton was a demon with his fists, and it was
more than once his ironic lot to repair in his own
hospital damages which he himself had inflicted. He
was the only Justice of the Peace on the diggings,
and on one occasion committed a man to take his
trial at Cooktown for a diabolioal murder. A Ger-
man witness was called and described the state of the
body when he saw it. Both arms and the head had
been cut off. The Prosecutor said to the witness:
“And from all these circumstances what conclusion
did you form?” The witness paused for a moment.
Then the illumination came and he said solemnly:
“Veil, ze conclusion I vas come to vas that she vas
dead 1”
The tobacco-workers of Victoria recently
sent an emissary to find out the conditions
under which the trade worked in Baiiana-
land. He discovered that men were receiv-
ing only 325. a week, as compared with 545.
in Victoria, and that girls were paid from
40 to 50 per cent. less. The Brisbane
Courier is much annoyed that the report
of this rude and inquisitive tobacco-twister
should be published, and refers to his
audacious doings under two indignant
headings: “VICTORIAN IMPERTIN-
ENCE” and “FOMENTING STRIFE IN
QUEENSLAND.”
The Brisbane Courier has started in to
help educate the natives. Its specialty is
geography. Gather round
There is not, however, reason to suppose that it
Holland were drawn into the struggle she would be
against the Allies of the Entente. She would have
a lot to lose in any event, for her Hattie shores could
be harassed by the German ships of tear.
Now, what mischievous wretch has been
pouring the Baltic into the North Sea? Out
with it!

If meat stores energy in . the boiler,
Queenslanders should be almost bursting the
receptacle. On the latest figures they eat
262.351 b. bn an average per annum, whereas
the allegedly beef-eating Briton scores only
1121 b., and Italy’s little lot is a mere 261 b
very little more in the year than the Banana-
lander swallows at his Sunday dinner.
. In financial tricks the Denham Goveru-
ment can teach most of them something.
Uijder the 1906 Railway Act the lands bene-
fited by a new line might be made respon-
sible for .earning net- interest on the cost of
construction up to 3 per cent. That is to
say, if'the line didn’t pay 3 per cent, on
cost in addition to working exes., the
owners could be called on to make 1 up jthe
shortage. Now Denham proposes to wipe
out the liability incurred by various districts,
capitalising the shortages for three years
and charging them to loan. This means
that part of Cohen’s money will be repre-
sented by deficits on the working bf lines">
in spite of which politicians will of course
go on referring to the State’s loans as being
represented by “permanent and reproductive”
undertakings.
Northern public servants are a grimly
humorous crowd. Every Bananalander knew
quite well that promotions in the service
were “off” this year, for obvious reasons.
The teachers therefore seized time by the
forelock and offered to forego their rises—
an offer which the Minister promptly
accepted with many compliments re the
patriotism of his flock. The rest of the
public servants, who didn’t think of tins
neat scheme for collecting marmalade, have
been gasring with admiration ever since.
The late Henry Majoribanks Chester,
police magistrate, was by instinct an adven-
turer. He soldiered in India and roamed
the AVestern Pacific, started a storekeeping
business in Brisbane, joined the Queensland
police, did some exploring, and hoisted the
British flag at Port Moresby, proclaiming
Queen Victoria owner of all Papua
except the part claimed by the _ Netherlands
Government. Thomas Mcllwraith, when lie
picked on Chester for the Papuan busi-
ness. thought that as he (Chester) had been
Sir Arthur Gordon’s right-hand man in the
Western Pacific, and virtually Commissioner
of Papua, Downing-street would ignore
the irregularity of the proceeding or condone
it. But Downing-street gave the back of
'its hand to Mcllwraitlf and emptied Chester
overboard. After that he was given odd
magisterial jobs, mostly in inaccessible
places, until old age drove him into retire-
ment at Brisbane.
“Tassy Hoe” : The farmer, being a simple
and innocent soul, knows nothing of the fine
city arts of adding sand to the sugar and
water to the milk. He is so-clumsy and
heavy-handed that a Too’woomba-firm recently
-had to write as follows in reproof of a client’s
“carelessness” : ,
Your truck of potatoes arrived this morning. Our
carter refused delivery owing to soil. I had a load
brought out; and emptied two bags. There was
1941 b. of soil in the two. We are taking the potatoes
over and re-bagging them before we send them out,
and please make a note that we will not take
delivery of any potatoes in future branded .
When the firm forwarded the cheque a reduc-
tion was made for 4cwt. 141 b. of dirt. It also
intimated that instead of sending his land
down by the bag, the famer might save a lot
of trouble and freight by posting the title
deeds.
The Blinky Way.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When little eyes commence to tire
And curly heads begin to nod,
W’hen funny faces fill the fire
And tiny brains have fancies odd,
The signs are full—the hour is near
To drop from gentle lap and stray
Among the flowers that slyly peer
Beside the children’s Blinky Way.
It starts from every fireside where
The young ones flock at eventide,
And gives a path to all who wear
A weary look at mother’s side;
’Tis planted all with poppy bloom
That nids and nods in fashion gay,
And seems to whisper: “Here is room
For them who walk the Blinky Way.”
It winds about a meadow green
(The stars a-spangle in the dusk) ;
’Tis half in shade and half in sheen,
And fragrant all with mint and musk ;
Its birds flit by on silver wing,
Its ponies all are dapple gray,
Its fairies featly dance and sing
For joy along the Blinky Way.
’Twas fashioned in -the Long Ago
When Ogres fought with Pippin Tim
(You see they loved Princesses so
They’d let you tear them Jmb from limb
Before they’d set the darlings free)
Yes, back in that delightful day
The children first, as all agree,
Began to take the Blinky Way.
They frisk along, a laughing troop,
Until they reach the Land of Dreams,
And there in many a smiling group
They dance beside enchanted streams;
No warning voice nor frowning face
May mar the sound of happy play
Or kill with care the tender grace
That lies along the Blinky Way.
And you and I, though big we be,
And hard of face and void of curls,
May catch a glimpse, if craftily
We crouch among our bQys and girls,
And still our tongues, and shut our eyes,
And so deceive the warders gray,
At whose behest the portals rise -
That open up the Blinky lyay.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 17, 1014.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,— .
Rain, patriotic entertainments, more
and the theatre have filled the menu for
week, with the Red Cross Society throw
in a few undignified rows and bickeri
There are some people who would s( l ua ._
even when bidden to the first and only P
formance of the Trump of Doom. -f t
latest news from the storm centre is t
the whole of one of the Red Cross co
mittees (or sub-committees) has resign
in a batch. If all the other warlike ang /
did likewise there might be some chance
each of Queensland’s soldier boys getting <
new shirt next Christmas. As it is, the
doesn’t seem much hope of the work P 1 °/
gressing as the necessities of the
demand. ~
Mrs. Boyd Irwin, of “Within the baw
co., is a sister of one of our most popuiai
young matrons, Mrs. (Dr. A. B.) Carvosso.
With Mr. Irwin she is staying under the
Carvosso roof, which is fatrjed in this out-
jpost of Empire for its atmosphere of cheer-
ful friendliness.
Ailsa Craig, a Sydney girl who also
belongs to the “Within the Law” party, is
staying with the McSwaines. at New Farm.
Her sister Dorothy is with her. On Friday
the McSwaines enlivened a large bunch or
friends at a party in honor of the visitors.
The Sunshine concert party dropped
round to the Army Medical Corps’ camp on
Thursday night and cheered the spirits or
the mending and patching brigade with
samples of patriotic music. It was a kind
thought, and if kind thoughts are not more
than bayonets these days, they are perhaps
next best.
“Within the Law” quite stirred this city,
and the theatre has been crowded every
night. At the first performance, in one of
the most pathetic scenes of the play, an
hysterical female in the front row of the
gallery delivered a sob that echoed all over
the house; and the house, turned its back on
studied tragedy to laugh its buttons loose at
the unrehearsed comicality.
On Wednesday Miss Katie Cannan’s danc-
ing pupils held a ball in the Centennial IlalJ,
and the. proceeds, which went to the Patri-
otic Fund, had a comfortable, bulgy feeling.
To-night Miss St. Ledger’s dancing under-
graduates are doing ditto, with the imper-
ceptible difference that the cash harvested in
this case is destined for the Red Cross Fund.
This afternoon, in between heavy showers,
the Hospital Sports happened at the Exhi-
bition Grounds. The Arthur Morgans and
the Mayor and Mayoress floated in aboard
four-wheeled rafts, but most of the rest of
those present consisted of the members of
the band, the athletes, and a few dozen
soldier-men.
A fete in aid of some deserving cause or
other was let off by Minister Barnes this
afternoon. Another, in aid of St. Colomb’s
Church at Clayfield, was uncorked by Mrs.
.Tenkinson yesterday, and has been bubbling
ever since.
To-night, after the performance of “Within
The Law,” “Snowy” Baker auctioned a
Union Jack. Lincoln Plumer supplied the
comic relief, while Muriel Starr provided the
ornamental relief. The audience, however,
failed to come to light with the cash relief,
ahd the flag fetched only £3l.
; When last heard of, Mr. and Mrs. Mark
Pringle Elliot, late of Brisbane and now of
Sydney, had just returned to London from
Bath, Tenby' and Northampton, after paying
a round of visits to Mr. Elliot’s sisters. The
Australians intended leaving for Scotland in
August. Continental Europe was to have
been included in the itinerary, ' but Con-
tinental Europe (barring Rome, Messina.
Spitzbergen, Madrid, Monaco, Lapland, etc.)
is a doubtful proposition now.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The champion easily-upset man has been
heard of from Rockhampton. He was
annoyed by fowls scratching in his yard, so
he got a rifle and fired two shots at them.
Both scored misses, whereupon there was the
usual chaff. It was more than he could
stand, so he put the rifle to his head and
blew his brains out.
The Patriarchs of Melbourne City Coun-
cil began a really big job the other day.
They perceived, in their/customary Argus-
eyed manner, that the/ traffic around the
Central Railway Station was congested, and
they decided that the time was ripe to spend
£50 —not a penny less—in rounding off the
pavements at the two northern corners of
the intersecting streets. At this crucial
stage it was borne in on them that money
was tight, and the entire splendid scheme
fell dead. Yet it still lives, in the sense
that it indicates something of the elan and
imaginativeness and grasp of large affairs
which characterise the Melbourne City
Council.

The Inky Way.
One day this month J. L. Fordo, who has
spent half a century in journalism, in Syd-
ney, .Melbourne and Tasmania, completed
his 70(h year. lie got his early journalistic
schooling on Melbourne Age. His father
was a journalist, as was his father-in-law,
and the three of them together have made the
fortunes of four ink manufacturers.
* * * *
fin, _ _ uixlon’s biggest scares at the start
of the war was over printing-paper, or the
woodpulp that it is made from. Of the
1 • >,OOO tons used weekly in England, about
a fifth goes from the Continent—chiefly Nor-
way and Sweden —and it all stopped. So
did the supply from Newfoundland and other
American countries. Just when big editions
were demanded, the papers, or nearly all of
them, had to come out as thin shadows of
themselves. Even then 50 per cent, more
paper was consumed than usual. The imme-
diate result was a sharp rise in price, from
Id. per lb. to l id. Then about 50 papers of
various sorts fell down dead. Most of them
were “organs” of some restricted cause, either
a social movement in which everybody had
lost interest, or a game AVhich nobody had
the heart to play; and most of them grasped
the respectable excuse for dying as a blessing
in disguise.
* * * *
“Ilomer D.T. Shales” : The art of quota-
tion, as prgefised by Sydney Telegraph :—-
The Australian people will gladly accept the
opportunity of showing in a formal way their appre-
cuation of the magnificent courage of Belgium, that
, 1 1 iV ld ,. S0 B' r eat of heart” which has truly

Note the addition of the musical and majestic
which has truly”! The idea is worth ex-
perimenting with. Let’s try again
Though Councillor Culvert’s speech on the dis-
graceful state of the Council Chamber walls was plain
and direct, his general demeanor reminded our re-
porter of the Bard of Avon’s unforgettable speech in
leference to Julius Caesar: “Imperial Caesar, dead and
turned to clay, might stop a hole,” which, no doubt,
had been an eyesore, “to keep the wind away.”
P. u " n K the first 10 days of the war
no official information was made public here
(London) concerning the disposition of the
British soldiers or ships. Regiments, fully
equipped, marched the streets of the big city
and disappeared. Nobody knew where. It
was all part of the policy of Kitchener, and
the public did not actually know whether
the troops were going abroad for active ser-
™ce w s l '®. simply out for show purposes.
The Court Circular, issued daily from Buck-
ingham Palace, however, nearly gave the
show away. One of the first pars, said:
tjueen Alexandra and ladies in attendance
visited Chelsea barracks and witnessed the
departure of the Grenadier Guards and Cold-
stream Guards for active service abroad.”
llie nowlj -born AYar Press Bureau received
one or these circulars Just after it was issued

remarks on odds and ends.
THE MAX WHO WRITES THE AVAR NOTES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You will know him by his very solemn look
As he walks with fingers locked behind his back,
By the witness of the newlv-borrowed book
In cloth of flaring red or sombre black.
He is pondering a method to defeat
The Kaiser’s hosts with unexampled gore;
And yet he looks so harmless in the street,
The man who writes the notes upon the war.
The books that taught the nations how to fight,
He reads them all, from Jomini to .Jones ;
In strategy he takes his one delight,
And tosses army corps like knuckle-bones ;
In Kitchener he meets a kindred soul
(He handled Kitch. in Africa before),
And none will help the Allies to their goal
Like him who writes the notes upon the war.
In fancy he is strolling by the Aisne,
Unshaken by the screams of German shell;
Its bloody chaos seems to him as plain
As Wollongong from Bulli’s citadel.
Napoleon’s'eagle gaze you’d hardly trust
To view all Europe’s realms from shore to shore;
lie does it every evening for a crust,
The man who writes the notes upon the war.
IVhen Russia claims a victory, and when
The Kaiser says lie's blown the Czar to pot,
V\ hen in the game of cut-and-come-again
We grumble: “Have they won, or lost, or what®”
He’s not allowed one adumbrated doubt;
Like Harald Baker in the Stadium’s roar,
Ho has to speak his verdict boldly out —
The man who writes the notes upon the war.
The cable man who dresses up his batch
Of self-accusing- lies from near and far
Has no perplexing theories to hatch ;
IHs job is finished when the head-lines are.
But later yet, till half-past two a.m.,
A pallid scribe on rows of proofs must pore,
Trying to squeeze a meaning out of them —
r Jhe man who writes the notes upon the war.
When Joffre fails, and Rennenkampf is dead,
And in the dust John French his alley slings,
They’ll have to find another man instead
Who takes a continental view of things.
A strategist is waiting to be bagged ;
For union rates, or half a sovereign more,
He’ll bring you home the Kaiser bound and gagged,
The man who writes the notes upon the war.
JT.S.W.
A. M.

The Sculptors.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
tN the prehistoric ages when the world
was very large,
Man achieved his primal sages through
the tiger’s sabre-charge!
Tea, the tiger and the lion and some tons of
ancient bear —
They were sculptors, raising Zion in our
neolithic lair !
For they taught that bows and arrows were
necessities of -Man —
Also spears, as found in barrows raised by
some forgotten clan !
So I hail the feral creatures and the giant
dinosaur—
For those sculptors shaped our features
with the chisel-blows of War;
In the very dim Beginnings, ere old History
began,
Through ten thousand lusts and sinnings
savage Nature chiselled Man!
We were rough indeed and shapeless in the
far ancestral days
When our Tree was seldom ape-less and our
ears were swift to raise!
When we listened for the foeman who was
shaggy and obscene,
W c were shaping—then the Roman came
and shaped anew our mien !
llv had studied things at college through a
thousand fighting years,
►So lie handed us good knowledge with his
swords and sudden spears !
Hence the Briton, hacked and chiselled on a
grim far-seeing plan,
Whilst a hundred nations fizzled, slowly came
to lead the van !
Fool-historians may garble facts and fancies
as they please—
Bull was like a block of marble shaped by
some Praxiteles !
leu, the seaman and the shoreman flew like
chips before the Dane,
And again before the Norman and the Anglo-
Saxon Cain !
All the years were swinging hammers, all
tire wars were heavy blows —
Thus through many dreads and clamors
Bull’s tremendous Form arose!.
Little Know-Alls ! Politicians ! Come and
sit around my door,
hilst I tell how Will’s \ volitions shaped
that Form in days of War!
For the Human Will is master' over soft,
insensate clay,
And the Sculptor works the faster when the
cannon boom and slay !
Hence, the Message of ' the Ages now is
written very clear—
Through this present war that rages see the
,°rm still rising sheer!
War is Art, my simple brothers ! Vain the
. Parrot-cries of Peace—
’Midst the gabble of the others, let me speak
a word on Greece!
Ileie is Form! The vast! —Whose glory
looms up deathless through the years—
Greece the War-hewn, famed in story for
her soldiers and her seers !
I onder well this land lonian, facing towards
the Asian shore,
V here the storm-blown Paphlagonian
scanned the dim Hellenic lore!
Tea, in days ere Master Homer sang the
epic songs of Troy,
Greece, the War-shaped, charmed the roamer
with the Forms of Strength and Joy!
As the Scotchman clad in tartan chiselled
Lngland through the years,
° ‘ w, ' UI un sparing Spartan sculptured
Athens with his spears !
Tmy States they were that wrangled till at
last a Kaiser Bill
Rose where Persia’s church bells jangled,
and all Hellas felt the thrill!
Ju Peloponesiau there were
Ulsters sixty-three,
So they used to fight each session with
Amphictyonic glee!
They were shaping one another, were those
little States and bold,
A here the Spartan slew his brother in
r , Olympian days of old !
I hey were Sculptors—junior artists who
were fighting very well,
I ill at last the Asian War-tests came and
gave all Hellas Hell!
Out of Persia came the Master-bogus War
Lord of his time —
Kaiser Xerxes, who was plaster though lie
looked like rock sublime!
And before him out of Sardis march five
million fighting men—
Aigh the Kaiser’s throne a bard is, singing
anthems now and then !
“You are great,” the Singer chanted, “you
are ruler of all lands.”
St) proud Xerxes ramped and ranted and
he gave his loud commands!
“I am Boss! Aye, Boss of Millions!” he
observed with pompous phrase
As he pitched his gay pavilions o’er the
blue lonian bays !
When Xerxes fell on Hellas, in the dim,
dead long ago.
He was sculping then, as well as pushing
Persia at the foe !
Yea, his Army was a Chisel and his Fleet
a Hammer-haft,
I hough this Phidias did a mizzle out of
Hellas on a raft!
He was shocked, that ancient Josser, when
. the chips began to fly,
A hilst his Ma, the old Atossa, found him
wiser by-and-bye!
Lo, the heart of Greece was granite, and a
• sample chip to-day
Where Leonidas began it stands at famed
Thermopylae !
Chips of Ages! Chips of Glory .’—whilst
the Sculptors buried far.
Greece is bulging yet in story through each
battle-wound and scar !
And the joy of burning Athens, pleasing
Xerxes proud and vain,
I inds historic aftermath in sundry doings
at Louvain !
For the continent of Europe—it is Greece
but written large;
And another Sculptor’s sure hope passes
with each Xerxian charge!
Yea, in Sardis of the Prussians, which is
built upon the Spree,
ilhelrn contemplates the Russians much as
Xerxes mourned B.C. !
Dad, in pre-Teutonic ages, when your world
teas very large,
iOU achieved your Shahs pear e-sages
through the foeman’s battle-charge!
Yea, the Shapers of the Lion were the
French—the Russian Bear
Was a Sculptor raising Eion also in your
British lair!
And the greatest Norman Angell with his
sword at Romney Marsh
Burst the ancient Peace-Evangel in a style
extremely harsh!
So T hail these Sculptor-Creatures^-hail!
. thou German dinosaur —
Shaping Bull’s new Empire-Features with
the Hammer-haft of War!
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
In Tassy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a beautiful drive on that sunny
morning from Circular head to Myalla
- —the sea quiet and the sun low and
the grass green, all as it should be, and not
a flower left out —too beautiful a day for
anybody to go to gaol in. Yet ahead there
was a constable—considerately wearing a
soft felt on his head and his official helmet
under the seat —driving in a gig with his
two prisoners very content on his near side.
They changed to a coach at Rocky Cape and
were asked to climb the steep hill forward
and wait for the coach at the head of the
down grade. The policeman had no
anxieties. He was protected by the
prisoners’ fear of losing a constable who was
their very own. They were off to Laun-
ceston for a month for vagrancy, and if
they will put into work —to be paid with
real money—the energy they devoted to a
safe arrival in gaol they can never again be
convicted. One was a long, shambling
cretin, who ran as if his leg joints had never
been lubricated. The other was a fat, well-
nourished, round-faced, little man made of
puddings. His trousers, of the red-tinged-
yellow of stewed rhubarb, were much too
large for him and reefed in front. lie
made hurried faces behind him as he ran
up the hill after his only friend, the
constable, as if he were afraid to lose his
letter of introduction to gaol. He gam-
bolled around his policeman as an affectionate
dog circumambulates his master, and his
round, fat, little legs twinkled and his
rliubarb-stew-colored pants made derisive
faces behind his back, as he revolved to the
top of the hill, and then was seen no more.
Rut why a coach or a gig? Why not a
motor and the hobo’s rapture as he drives
on aix% royally into the kindly gaol?
Launceston has such fine public and
municipal spirit that it is a pity it is situ-
ated at the end of a drain, (live the Laun-
ceston people Hobart, and Hobart would
move greatly. Hobart, with not so much
public spirit, moves by the grace of its
magnificent port, and Launceston stays at
its 25,000 of population. It has very fine
houses and good public service, and muni-
cipal trams, and light and power from the
cheapest power plant in Australia. It is
for this result that Hobart should complete
—if Tasmania will not —the suspended
hydro-electric power and lighting scheme
from the Great Lake. Coal horse-power
probably costs the Island State from £2B to
£33 per li.p. per annum, and the Great Lake
scheme in full operation should provide a
h.p. for £5 a year. The difference means
that Hobart could become one of the great-
est industrial centres in Australia.
They don’t tax enough for the upkeep of
roads. One of the finest things in the world
vtas the llobart-Launceston road —convict-
built, when time was nothing and wages
were dry bread. The bond were the builders
and the free the destroyers. Gorse, whin,
briar and thorn choking little fields to repre-
sent the work of the free; and as monu-
ments to the bond. Bridges built with the
conscientiousness and integrity of the Roman,
over the South Esk, the Elizabeth, and the
Macquarie at Perth, Campbelltown, and
Ross. The road is worn to the quick of its
strongest stone foundation by traffic and
patching and makeshift. In the solidity of
road and bridge you read the real secret of
the Pyramids; and it was slavery.
All these towns are pock-marked with
churches. These, to do them justice,
brought the only schools and the only' educa-
tion then possible to the country, although
that education was mostly British geography
and dates of kings and queens—such as that
of Henry the Eight’s murder of Ann Boleyn
—strongly diluted with Samuel and Samson
and the jawbone of the ass. It represented
the best of civilisation until the State took
hold of teaching. Anyhow, in Ross you
have civilisation as it was then —the old
Round House (which is a square house),
and the old gaol wall, and the place where
the triangles stood. Then came 'the Church
and the House of Rum. Later civilisation
advances successively .to the residential
hotel and the pawn-broker, the agitator, the
labor union and adult suffrage; and, yet a
long way off. the square deal. It was a very
crooked deal which built this asset of the
finest of Roman roads, and it is the crooked-
est. of all which allows meanness to dissipate
the asset to rains and wind.
Ross gaol was established to house the
builders of the road. It was a prettv idea —
that of getting the prisoners to build a nice,
strong gaol to keep themselves in. Above it
is a long, large church with a high spire like
a dying man stretching his hand to the im-
potent sky. Over the gaol is the inscription.
"Erected 1836. ’ There is a rat (or kan-
gaioo) and a common chook (or emu)
flanking the inscription, and there are
caiven three cannon on a shield above the
door, and by the door a big iron foot-scraper
and within a deeply worn step. This is the
gaoler’s house. The Round House lias a
centre corridor with a semi-circular fireplace
tor the turnkey. There were four dark cells
and four Jight boarded cells in which the
