How to be Beautiful.
By RITA MOYA.
It is not every woman’s good fortune to be endowed by Nature with, fine features and
a flawless complexion, but nowadays it is possible to make the plainest face attractive, and
that too in the privacy of one’s borne, without recourse to beauty specialists, which usually
involve expensive treatments and much loss of time. I shall endeavor to show you in the
following lines how much may be done in this direction by any woman of ordinary intelli-
gence When facial applications are necessary, use only the pure ingredients just as they
come to the chemist himself. Do not allow yourself to be persuaded into buying some
cheap ready-made preparation instead. Any chemist will gladly obtain the original con-
centrated ingredients for you, if you insist, and although he may not have them in stock,
vou will be well advised to wait while he orders them for you. The improvement in your
appearance will be ample compensation for any trouble taken to obtain these simple and
harmless beautifiers.
Beauty in Breathing—A great deal can be
done towards brightening the eyes by system-
atic breathing for 10 minutes each morning
and evening. Breathe slowly and deeply to
the fullest capacity of the lungs. You should
stand erect by an onen window. The corsets
should not be worn during this exercise.
About Shampooing.—Even the best shampoo
is somewhat drying, and if the hair is not
naturally oily 1 suggest that just before the
shampoo you apply olive oil to the scalp, rub-
bing it into the hair roots vigorously. Then
use pure stallax for the shampoo. Dissolve a
teaspoonful in a cup of hot water. This will
leave the hair very clean, soft and fluffy.
An Instantaneous Beautifler. —Instead of face
powder, use a simple lotion made from one
ounce of cleminite and four teaspoonfuls of
water. This lotion will tone and clear the
skin, and acts as a protection against sun and
wind. A little applied with the finger-tips
instantly gives the skin a delightful “bloomy”
appearance. No powder is necessary, and the
result lasts all day long under the most try-
ing conditions.
To Permanently Remove Superfluous Hair.—
It is a simple matter to remove a downy
growth of hair temporarily, but to remove it
permanently is finite another matter. It is a
pity that it is not more generally known that
powdered pheminol may be used for this pur-
pose. Apply it. directly to the hair. The
recommended treatment is designed not merely
to instantly remove the hair, but also, to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Any chemist
should be able to supply you with an ounce
of pheminol, which quantity should be suffi-
cient,
Tlie Real Cause of Most Batl Complexions.—
It is an accepted fact that no truly beautiful
•omplexion ever came out of jars and bottles,
and the longer one uses cosmetics tlie worse
the complexion becomes. Skin, to be healthy,
must breathe. It also must expel, through the
pores, its share of the body’s effete material.
Creams and powders clog the pores, in-
terfering both with elimination and breathing.
If more women understood this there would
be fewer self-ruined complexions. If they
would use ordinary mercolised wax instead of
cosmetics they would have natural, healthy
complexions. This remarkable substance is
not absorbed by the skin: its action is just
the opposite. The skin repels mercolised wax.
and at the same time throws off all imperfec-
tions. An exquisite new complexion peeps
out. quite free from any appearance of artifi-
ciality. Apply nightly, like cold cream, for a
week or two, washing it off in the morning.
About Hair Tonics.—Each week almost one
hears of some wonderful discovery for im-
proving the hair, and although this paragraph
may seem a little superfluous, an old-fashioned
recipe may conic as a welcome change. One
thing about it is that it will grow hair, and
also prevent it falling out. From your chem-
ist get an original package of boranium. to
this add I-pint of bay rum, allow it to stand
c(i minutes, then add sufficient water to make
half a pint. Rub briskly into the scalp with
the finger-tips and you will immediately ex-
perience that clean, tingling sensation which
is a sure sign of healthy action. [2
confined were not so happy as the men im-
mured in blackness ; for in these light cells
there was not room to lie down. The door
of the exercise yard is still locked by a pair
of old and rusted handcuffs, and at the end
of the corridor is -the tragic simplicity of
old bars and worn stone, of a square lamp
above the centre door and the decaying
shingled roof admitting the freedom of the
wind. Probably many who were here im-
mured had heard St. Sepulchre’s bell toll for
others and were foolishly afraid to think it
might be for themselves—foolishly, because
they did not know that for them it was yet
to be terribly difficult to live, and horribly
easy to die.
There is an old hotel here with little
mullioned windows to the bar, unnecessarily
thick walls and break-neck stairs, and solid
stone stables like gaols, as if the architects
were Newgate turnkeys and the horses had
been transported for life. The pigeon-cote
above the stable is stone, and there are cobble
stones in the stables of the old reading days,
aud an ancient kitchen of stone, with very-
thick walls, so that the steak could not take
to the bush. At the street corner there is
an old stone mounting block weighing half a
ton or so. Down the street, too, there are
gate ‘•posts” of stone, very heavy and 7ft.
high, though timber is all about the hills.
It must have been the worst of all times
for the prisoners of brains and manliness.
They were forced to all kinds of unnecessary
things—(tod or the Devil knows why—
tearing down stone hills to build wide rubble
walls around stony little fields the size of a
bed-quilt, as in dear old England ; with bleak
hills to the east and the blue bulk of Ben
Nevis and Ben Lomond; and bare and
yellow hills to the south and west. Think
of.it —to have courage and manliness and
ability—to carry stone from one place to
another and then back again—the wind cry-
ing in the winter around the hills and over
the old walls of the cemetery 7 about the
nameless graves—now cut under by the rail-
way running at the foundations of the wall.
The stupidity of these old Tories! They
would not use convicts —that is. unassigned
men—to clear land and grow grain, because
in their view the functions of Government
were to make war. to flog, to hang, to do
useless things. So you see great stone-
troughs for water laboriously cut from mono-
liths, and a row of stone blocks 6ft. high
along a low embankment to prevent free
gentlemen from falling over. The fine
stone bridges at Ross, Tunbridge, Antill
Ponds and other places are well enough,
for the road was intended for all time,
though the present taxpayer will see it
sink to a ravine during his lifetime if he
doesn’t pay up; but crimes were these stone
walls of foolish and unnecessary thickness,
great stone troughs, and chimneys with the
mark of hopeless labor on them —work whose
every chisel mark says, “I was made slowly
though not at ease; time here was nothing,
effort valueless.”
Stones for road-making were dug for—
not picked up off agricultural land, because
that would be useful work. The later agri-
culturist has collected the stones, and with
them partially filled the quarries the con-
victs excavated.
They were whales on imported names—
were these early 7 home-sick gentlemen. They
have Oat lands and Melton Mowbray. Now
Green Ponds has become ashamed of
describing itself and has changed its name
to Kempton. Oatlands was to be a great
place once. It has a big, nice gaol, and a
flour mill with a tall, conical tower. pierced
with windows like the hind-castle of an old
frigate. It had once thirteen hotels and
now has three. In the country on the north
of it there are the ruins of a mud gaol, and
a few miles to the south of it is the Bath
Hotel, with the word “TAP” on its gable
in big letters to signify that here was the
Place of Beer. Kempton has a little hotel
■whose floor is lower than the street, and
polished forms of rough-hewn timber in its
tap-room, and the ancient game of rope-
quoits in the bar.
All along this road are fine estates and
solid houses and lands that do not appear to
be sufficiently used to justify them being
held in large areas. Jericho is a village on
the Jordan also, and Jerusalem has recently
altered its name to Colebrook; although, as
England is now 7 owned by Jews, and the
English are alleged to be the lost Ten Tribes,
the old name Avas English enough for any-
thing. Bagdad is a fine riot of apples and
hop-gardens, and the close cultivation of the
orchards makes the rabbits fewer. Further
north and out of the closely settled districts
the early settlers of Tasmania grew close
hedges, and then brought rabbits to live in
the hedges; so that they might look prettv
hopping in and out.
One bit of very old Hobart survives—a
wondrous black coach of the vintage of 170?,
- aei y long and A'ery spiderA 7 between the
tore-carriage aud the body. T believe it is
the original berline that Louis XVI and
Marie Antoinette and family used in their
attempt to escape from France; only that
it is black. Still one can buy paint.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
My Western Grave.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I FOUND that Morven had three nubs.
When I knew tin place first it had only
, one. Anybody who knows the innjr
< )uthack will be able to reckon up the time
that had elapsed.
They told me at Meredith’s that the banks
now owned about all the runs of any count.
Considering that I hit at least one sheep-
run to every cattle-station, I reckoned the
information Avas about right. There weren’t
many of the old hands about, either. When
tin. sheep-man comes he isn’t often found to
be a degenerated cattle-man. With the
passing of the bullocks the scrub-dasher
moves out. First he moved to the Diarnan-
tina and the Georgina: now he moves away
to the Gulf, or across the Pdue-bush. The
sheep-man keeps, hustling, however. At
Die time of my return to the Mulga he had
already passed across the Blue-bush, and
made a track into the Territory that looked
unpromising. And yet not quite so. The
cattle-man Avasn’t falling hack much farther,
lie opined that he AA'as safe enough
in his stronghold. There was yet plenty of
land to live in, especially along the Wave
Hill line; but the sheep-man who held his
end up in the Territory couldn’t be reckoned
small potatoes. There’ll be no room for
small potatoes in the N.T. this century. The
sheep-man down there had his points, too.
For one thing, he wasn’t counting himself a
Pioneer. He was leaving it to the next cen-
tury to provide this sort. The Pioneer, as
the cattle-man and the decent sheep-man
know him, is the sort of pot that hugs the
civilised seaboard, and digs out cedars, and
plants a dairy, and thinks cow all his
natural life. He looks over his mile of
grass and talks about the extent of the
“country” he owns; and, by sheer swank, he
buries in the nation’s forgetfulness the
memory of the wild characters who knew
what “country” meant —who tamed the colts
and grinned in the face of Death every
minute, in places where search for a missing
man was pretty well always search for a
dead or a mad man, and mostly to be as
reasonably made along a hundred miles of
country as one mile. Quite as reasonably,
also, by watching the sky as the ground,
because crows are seen in the sky. Such
men fade before the path of the Tioneer, and
call themselves Blokes.
The old place had altered a lot. On the
way out I met many fences, mostly with
sheep-wires, while now and then one was
a rabbit-proof. There weren’t many million
men about; and all the way to the
border of Thirsty Downs I met only one
nigger, and he a foreigner. You can tell
a black man, and the sort he is, as far off
as you can distinguish his outline. This
black was the kind that packs his swag on
his horse and trudges along in the dust,
leading the animal. The whole world
seemed to have altered. No Australian
nigger would lead his horse for the sake of
his swag, nor for the sake of anything else.
If his swag were likely to block his riding
lie wouldn’t hesitate to hurl the nuisance
away.
Yet this Hindoo had come thousands of
miles. He had probably done more than
any of . our niggers would ever contem-
plate doing—he had hit out for a new coun-
try; and in that land's wild spots he was
somehow wriggling along, in silence, a mys-
tery to the one who chose to ponder a bit.
He may have had a home —been a murderer
or an otherwise “wanted”; the chances are
he would never have got even as close out,
as Morven, but for being wanted. I won-
der where that silent old Hindoo is to-day?
But the change was pronounced enough ;
things had come to this pass that the white
man was hunting out one nigger and letting
in another.
* # * *
What used to be one great slab of country,
bisected by the Nebine, I found to be many
stations, all of them fenced along' the boun-
dary lines, and some of them watering to no
small extent from the river. For the Nebine
was now a constantly-running stream, rising
principally from a bore lately put down on
TJlarunda. At this bore a shearing-shed was
being rigged up. There are many similar
places in West Queensland to-day where the
traveller drinks bore water at the river. Seems
paradoxical.
And if ever a bore was a godsend, that
day I figured it was the one on TJlarunda;
for, with all the changes I have touched upon,
somehow the country was the same. There
was the same heat and dust and monotony
of mulga and brigalow and kangaroo-grass.
There was also the same thirstiness—but
now, a river to drink from.
The road from Toomo to Charleville, and
the one I had just travelled —Morven to
Toomo —are marked with the graves of men:
yet each track is merely a matter of 350
miles or so. I know’ three or four graves in
particular which the latter road crosses; and
I fancied that, one or two of the men below
might recognise that I w’as around again as
I reined up on top of them and thought about
them awhile. Most of these graves were dug
when they were pretty well unnecessary—
that is. long after they had been necessary—
when the men were found. Some of the men
died in the harshness and the heat; others
passed out under similar circumstances; and
all of consequence to me- were planted where
they crossed over—in the harshness and the
heat. I hope the shadow of my horses sank
through and cooled one or two of the beds.
* * * *
But the grave of my seeking was planted
differently, and I found it on the slope lead-
ing from a blue-grass gully, where the
shadow’s were cool under giant coolabalis.
and whence, stretching gently up and then
away, could be seen a w’onderful forest of
freshness. For out there ran a kurrajong
belt, ten miles across; a belt of giants of life
—food in the leaves for horses, water in the
roots, for man. I had a little trouble in
locating the gully at first, but then the old
landmarks came back to me. and I soon
dropped across my grave.
I don’t know that I was ever so struck by
the presence of birds about a grave. There
was a whole mob of jackasses, all in blue-
dress : and a great, calm, watchful head,
resembling nothing so much as the top of a
stick, faced me for about five minutes while
I thought awhile, a hundred yards away.
When I moved forward the plain turkey rose
to wing right from the top of the grave, and
quite 50 others lit out from the vicinity.
There were also emus, with chicks —many
emus and mobs of chicks, in fact, though
some people will persist in the belief that
only one mother and brood will be found at
the same place.
And there wasn’t a crow. A cool wind
came up ; and the light became denser, for it
was drawing towards evening. The remains
of the skeleton of His Dog Tom lay on top of
His grave. The blue-grass, which covered the
mound and sprouted through Tom’s ribs, was
soon weeded away. There was a red color in
the sky when I had straightened everything
out. It glowad on the kurrajongs np the rise
a little later, and then spread to the coola-
bahs over the grave, where now’ Tom’s re-
mains were in decent arrangement. Tom died
on guard there; I let him stay on guard. The
grave hadn’t changed much during my
absence, except for a shrinkage which I had
now’ mounded up. T thought I heard a dingo;
also some brumbies ; but Sam always loved
such sounds. I had planted Him well.
ARTHUR L. BREWER.
